Forgetful Acts During Fasting: Ruling and Proof
Lists the first item of things that do not invalidate fasting: forgetful acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forgetful eating, drinking, or intercourse during fasting and explain the proof from the hadith.

Core Idea
Forgetful acts do not break the fast; Allah fed and gave drink, so continue without qada.

This passage from Nur al-Idah opens the chapter on what does not invalidate fasting. The first category listed is forgetful acts: if a fasting person eats, drinks, or has intercourse out of forgetfulness, the fast remains valid. The text provides the primary proof—a hadith commanding completion of the fast—and extends the ruling to intercourse by analogy. It also addresses subtle details about when the person remembers mid-act and the separate issue of kaffara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules that eating, drinking, or intercourse done forgetfully does not invalidate the fast. The fast remains valid and must be continued.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from multiple sources: 'Whoever forgets while fasting and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink.' This is a Sunnah ahad hadith.
The hadith is also found in Bukhari, Muslim, and others.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'falyutimma' (let him complete) indicates the obligation to continue fasting. The reason clause 'fa innamā aṭ'amahu Allah' shows that the food was a provision from Allah, thus not a nullifier.
The Hanafi principle: al-amr yufidu al-wujub, but here it is not a new obligation but continuation.
4
Extend the ruling by dalala
The text extends the ruling from eating/drinking to intercourse by 'dalala' (indication), because intercourse is similar in meaning to consumption (it also breaks the fast if intentional). So the same forgetful exemption applies.
5
Note the detail on remembrance mid-act
If someone begins intercourse forgetfully and then remembers, he must withdraw immediately. If he continues, the fast is invalidated. Ejaculation is not the cause of invalidation but triggers possible kaffara if done voluntarily after remembering.
This nuance shows the precision of Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في بيان ما لا يفسد الصوم وهو أربعة وعشرون شيئاً ما لو أكل أو شرب، أوْ جَامَعَ ناسياً، وإن كان للناسي قُدْرَةٌ على الصوم باب في بيان ما لا يفسد الصوم (2) (وهو أربعة وعشرون (شيئاً تقريباً لا تحديداً بالمرة، منها ما لو أكل) الصائم (أو شرب، أو جَامَعَ أو جمع بينهما (ناسياً ) صومه، والنسيان: عدم استحضار الشيء عند الحاجة لا ناسياً فعله، لأنه متذكر لأكله وشربه وجماعه، ولم يفطر بالتشديد والتخفيف، فعلى الأول يكون مسنداً إلى الأكل وما يضاهيه لحديث الجماعة إلا النسائي : ( مَنْ نَسِيَ وَهُوَ صَائِمٌ فَأَكَلَ أَو شَرِبَ فَلْيَتَمَّ صَوْمَهُ فَإِنَّمَا أَطْعَمَهُ الله وَسَقَاهُ» (۱) والمراد الصوم الشرعي، للاتفاق على وجوبه وحملـه عـلـى الحقيقة الشرعية، حيث أمكن في لفظ الشارع، وروى ابن حبان في صحيحه، والدارقطني في سننه: أن رجلاً سأل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: إِنِّي كُنتُ صَائِمَاً فَأَكَلْتُ وَشَرِبْتُ نَاسِيَا، فَقَالَ رَسُولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: ( أتمَّ صَوْمَكَ، فَإِنَّ الله أطْعَمَكَ وَسَقَاكَ » (۳) وفي لفظ : « ولا قضاء عليك » (۳) ، كذا في «الفتح» . وفي « الدارقطني قال : إِذَا أَكَلَ الصَائِمُ نَاسِيَاً، أو شَرِبَ نَاسِيَا، فَإِنَّمَا هُوَ رِزْقٌ سَاقَهُ الله إِلَيْهِ، فَلا قَضَاءَ عَلَيْهِ ) وإسناده صحيح، فإذا ثبت في الأكل والشرب ثبت في الجماع دلالة، لأنه في معناهما)، ولو بدأ الجماع ناسياً فتذكر إن نزع فوره لم يفطر في الصحيح من الرواية، كما في «الخانية» وإلا فسد صومه، وقوله في « الفتح » : وإن دام على ذلك حتى أنزل فعليه القضاء، ليس الإنزال شرطاً في إفساد الصوم، وإنما ذكر الإنزال لبيان حكم الكفارة، لأنه عقبه بقوله، ثم قيل: لا كفارة عليه، وقيل: هذا إذا لم يحرك نفسه بعد التذكر حتى أنزل، فإن حرك نفسه بعده فعليه الكفارة، كما لو نزع ثم أدخل، ولو جامع عمداً قبل الفجر وطلع وجب النزع في الحال، فإن حرك نفسه فهو على هذا انتهى يعني: في لزوم الكفارة، أما فساد الصوم فيحصل بمجرد المكث فليتنبه له. ولــو نــزع خشية طلوع الفجر فأمنى بعد الفجر والنزع ليس عليه شيء، لأنه لم يوجد بعد الصبح الجماع لا صورة ولا معنى، كذا في « التجنيس». (وإن كان للنّاسي قُدْرَةٌ على إتمام (الصوم) إلى الليل بلا مشقة ظاهرة
Chapter on explaining what does not invalidate fasting. It is twenty-four things approximately, not strictly limited. From them: if the fasting person eats or drinks or has intercourse or combines them forgetfully (forgetful of his fast). And forgetfulness is the absence of recollection of the thing at the time of need, not that he forgot his action, because he remembers his eating, drinking, and intercourse, and it (the fast) does not break by emphasis or non-emphasis. According to the first, it is attributed to eating and similar due to the hadith of the group except al-Nasa'i: 'Whoever forgets while fasting and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink.' And the intended meaning is the legal fast, due to agreement on its obligation and carrying it upon the legal reality when possible in the word of the Lawgiver. And Ibn Hibban narrated in his Sahih and al-Daraqutni in his Sunan that a man asked the Messenger of Allah: 'I was fasting and ate and drank forgetfully.' So the Messenger of Allah said: 'Complete your fast, for Allah has fed you and given you drink.' And in a wording: 'No qada is upon you.' Thus in al-Fath. And in al-Daraqutni: 'When a fasting person eats forgetfully or drinks forgetfully, it is a provision that Allah has provided him, so no qada is upon him.' Its chain is authentic. So if it is established for eating and drinking, it is established for intercourse by indication, because it is in their meaning. And if he began intercourse forgetfully and then remembered, if he withdraws immediately, he does not break fast in the sound narration, as in al-Khaniyya; otherwise his fast is invalidated. And his statement in al-Fath: 'And if he continues until he ejaculates, then qada is upon him' – ejaculation is not a condition for invalidating the fast, rather he mentioned ejaculation to clarify the ruling of kaffara, because he follows it with his statement: 'Then it is said: no kaffara upon him; and it is said: this is if he did not move himself after remembering until he ejaculated, but if he moved himself after it, then kaffara is upon him, just as if he withdrew and then inserted.' And if he intentionally had intercourse before dawn and dawn rose, he must withdraw immediately, so if he moved himself, he is upon this – meaning regarding the necessity of kaffara. As for the invalidation of the fast, it occurs by just staying, so be mindful. And if he withdrew fearing the dawn and then emitted after dawn and withdrawal, nothing is upon him, because after dawn there was no intercourse in form or meaning. Thus in al-Tajnis. And (the forgetful person) has the ability to complete (the fast) until night without apparent hardship.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on what does not invalidate fasting. It lists the first item: a fasting person who eats, drinks, or has intercourse forgetfully (nasiyan) does not break his fast. Forgetfulness is defined as not recollecting the fast at the time of the act. The proof is a hadith from several sources commanding the forgetful person to complete his fast and that no qada is required. The text then extends the ruling from eating/drinking to intercourse by analogy (dalala). It then discusses a detail: if someone begins intercourse forgetfully and then remembers, if he withdraws immediately, his fast is valid; if he continues, his fast is invalid. The issue of kaffara (expiation) is separate: ejaculation is not the cause of invalidation but triggers possible kaffara. The text clarifies that invalidation occurs by merely remaining in the act after remembering, while kaffara depends on voluntary movement. An exception is if one intentionally had intercourse just before dawn and dawn rose, he must withdraw immediately; if he moves voluntarily, kaffara is due. Lastly, if one withdraws in fear of dawn and then emits after dawn, nothing is required. The final line states that the forgetful person has the ability to complete his fast until night without apparent hardship – this may be a conditional note that the ruling applies only if he is able to continue without difficulty. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi position that forgetful acts do not nullify the fast, based on clear hadith and careful reasoning.

Key Terms
النسيان (al-nisyān): Forgetfulness; defined as the absence of recollection of the fast at the time of the act, not forgetting the act itself.
القضاء (al-qaḍā'): Making up a missed fast; not required for the forgetful person in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Break the fast and make it up later

C) Stop eating but the fast is broken
D) Make up that day after Ramadan

Key Takeaway
Forgetful acts during fasting do not invalidate the fast; the person continues fasting and owes no qada.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
8 min
Forgetfulness Does Not Break the Fast
Ruling and dalil for forgetfulness in fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forgetful eating, drinking, or intercourse during fasting and explain the dalil (hadith) that supports it.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness is provision from Allah — the fast remains valid and no qada' is required.

Many assume that eating, drinking, or intercourse during fasting always invalidates the fast. However, the Prophet ﷺ explicitly ruled that forgetful acts do not break the fast; they are provision from Allah. This lesson examines the hadith evidence and its application, including the nuance of intercourse and immediate withdrawal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the specific act being ruled upon? (forgetful eating, drinking, intercourse). What is the hukm? (fast remains valid, no qada').
The ruling is stated clearly at the beginning.
2
Locate the dalil
What is the primary evidence? The hadith: 'Whoever forgets while fasting and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast…' Identify its sources and authenticity.
The author cites multiple narrations.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The imperative 'falyutimma' shows continuation; 'innamā aṭʿamahu Allāhu' indicates it is not the person's action. Also the explicit 'lā qaḍāʾa ʿalayka' in another version.
The proof is direct and clear.
4
Apply qiyas to intercourse
Why does the same ruling apply to intercourse? Because it is in the same meaning (ma'nā) as eating and drinking—acts that break the fast when done intentionally.
This is an example of qiyas jaliy.
5
Handle the nuance of remembering during intercourse
What happens if one remembers while still engaged? Immediate withdrawal is required; failure to withdraw invalidates the fast. Ejaculation only affects kaffara, not the validity.
This detail is important for correct application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في بيان ما لا يفسد الصوم
وهو أربعة وعشرون شيئاً ما لو أكل أو شرب، أوْ جَامَعَ ناسياً، وإن كان للناسي قُدْرَةٌ على الصوم
باب في بيان ما لا يفسد الصوم
(2)
(وهو أربعة وعشرون (شيئاً تقريباً لا تحديداً بالمرة، منها ما لو أكل) الصائم (أو شرب، أو جَامَعَ أو جمع بينهما (ناسياً ) صومه، والنسيان: عدم استحضار الشيء عند الحاجة لا ناسياً فعله، لأنه متذكر لأكله وشربه وجماعه، ولم يفطر بالتشديد والتخفيف، فعلى الأول يكون مسنداً إلى الأكل وما يضاهيه لحديث الجماعة إلا النسائي : ( مَنْ نَسِيَ وَهُوَ صَائِمٌ فَأَكَلَ أَو شَرِبَ فَلْيَتَمَّ صَوْمَهُ فَإِنَّمَا أَطْعَمَهُ الله وَسَقَاهُ» (۱) والمراد الصوم الشرعي، للاتفاق على وجوبه وحملـه عـلـى الحقيقة الشرعية، حيث أمكن في لفظ الشارع، وروى ابن حبان في صحيحه، والدارقطني في سننه: أن رجلاً سأل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: إِنِّي كُنتُ صَائِمَاً فَأَكَلْتُ وَشَرِبْتُ نَاسِيَا، فَقَالَ رَسُولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: ( أتمَّ صَوْمَكَ، فَإِنَّ الله أطْعَمَكَ وَسَقَاكَ » (۳) وفي لفظ : « ولا قضاء عليك » (۳) ، كذا في «الفتح» . وفي « الدارقطني قال : إِذَا أَكَلَ الصَائِمُ نَاسِيَاً، أو شَرِبَ نَاسِيَا، فَإِنَّمَا هُوَ رِزْقٌ سَاقَهُ الله إِلَيْهِ، فَلا قَضَاءَ عَلَيْهِ ) وإسناده صحيح، فإذا ثبت في الأكل والشرب ثبت في الجماع دلالة، لأنه في معناهما)، ولو بدأ الجماع ناسياً فتذكر إن نزع فوره لم يفطر في الصحيح من الرواية، كما في «الخانية» وإلا فسد صومه، وقوله في « الفتح » : وإن دام على ذلك حتى أنزل فعليه القضاء، ليس الإنزال شرطاً في إفساد الصوم، وإنما ذكر الإنزال لبيان حكم الكفارة، لأنه عقبه بقوله، ثم قيل: لا كفارة عليه، وقيل: هذا إذا لم يحرك نفسه بعد التذكر حتى أنزل، فإن حرك نفسه بعده فعليه الكفارة، كما لو نزع ثم أدخل، ولو جامع عمداً قبل الفجر وطلع وجب النزع في الحال، فإن حرك نفسه فهو على هذا انتهى يعني: في لزوم الكفارة، أما فساد الصوم فيحصل بمجرد المكث فليتنبه له. ولــو نــزع خشية طلوع الفجر فأمنى بعد الفجر والنزع ليس عليه شيء، لأنه لم يوجد بعد الصبح الجماع لا صورة ولا معنى، كذا في « التجنيس».
Chapter on explaining what does not break the fast. It is twenty-four things: if one eats, drinks, or has intercourse forgetfully, even if the forgetful person has the ability to fast. The chapter: Explaining what does not break the fast. (It is approximately twenty-four things, not exactly). Among them: if the fasting person eats, drinks, has intercourse, or combines them forgetfully. Forgetfulness is not being aware of the thing when needed, not forgetting the action itself, because he remembers eating, drinking, and intercourse, and it does not break the fast with emphasis or without emphasis. On the first interpretation, it is attributed to eating and similar actions based on the hadith of the group except al-Nasa'i: 'Whoever forgets while fasting and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink.' The intent is the legally-defined fast, due to consensus on its obligation and carrying it on the legal reality, where possible in the Lawgiver's words. Ibn Hibban in his Sahih and al-Daraqutni in his Sunan narrated that a man asked the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'I was fasting and ate and drank forgetfully.' The Messenger of Allah said: 'Complete your fast, for Allah has fed you and given you drink.' In another wording: 'And no qada' (make-up) is required of you.' So in al-Fath. And in al-Daraqutni: 'When the fasting person eats or drinks forgetfully, it is only provision that Allah has sent to him, so no qada' is required of him.' Its chain is authentic. Since it is established for eating and drinking, it is established for intercourse by implication, because it is in their meaning. If one begins intercourse forgetfully and then remembers, if he withdraws immediately, he does not break his fast according to the sound narration, as in al-Khaniyya; otherwise his fast is invalidated. His statement in al-Fath: 'And if he continues until ejaculation, then qada' is required,' ejaculation is not a condition for invalidating the fast; rather he mentioned ejaculation to explain the ruling of kaffara, because he followed it with his statement, then it is said: no kaffara upon him, and it is said: this is if he did not move himself after remembering until ejaculation; if he moves himself after it, then kaffara is required, as if he withdrew and then inserted. And if one had intercourse intentionally before dawn and dawn rose, withdrawal is obligatory immediately; if he moves himself, it is on this—meaning regarding the obligation of kaffara. As for invalidation of the fast, it occurs simply by staying, so be mindful. If one withdraws out of fear of dawn and then ejaculates after dawn, nothing is upon him, because the intercourse did not occur after dawn either in form or meaning, as in al-Tajnīs.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) from the Sunnah. The author explains that forgetfully eating, drinking, or having intercourse does not break the fast. The key hadith is from the 'group' (collectors except al-Nasa'i): the person who forgets and eats/drink should complete the fast; it is a provision from Allah. The Hanafi position is that no qada' is required, derived from the literal meaning of the hadith. The same rule applies to intercourse by analogy because it is of the same nature. A nuance: if one starts intercourse forgetfully and then remembers, immediate withdrawal is required; failure to do so invalidates the fast. Ejaculation is not a condition for breaking the fast; it only matters for kaffara. The discussion also addresses the case of withdrawal before dawn and ejaculation after—since no intercourse occurred after dawn, no harm. This ruling is based on Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
نسيان (nisyān): Not being aware of the thing when needed, not forgetting the action itself; the person remembers the act but forgets the ruling or context of fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a fasting person forgetfully eats a meal and then remembers, what is the ruling on his fast according to the Hanafi school?
A) His fast is invalid and he must make it up.
B) His fast is valid; no make-up is required.
C) His fast is valid but he must make up a different day.
D) His fast is invalid only if he continues eating after remembering.

Key Takeaway
Forgetful acts do not break the fast; the fast remains valid and no qada' is required, based on the authentic hadith.

Reminding a Forgetful Faster: When to Speak and When to Stay Silent
Ruling on reminding a forgetful faster

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reminding a forgetful faster and apply the criterion of weakness to determine the appropriate action.

Core Idea
Reminding a forgetful faster is recommended if they can endure; if they are weak, silence is kindness.

This lesson addresses a practical scenario during Ramadan: you see someone eating forgetfully. Should you inform them? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer that depends on the condition of the person. The key is not age but whether fasting would cause undue hardship. Let's examine the ruling from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
The text states that the one who sees a forgetful faster should remind him (yudhakkiruhu). It is makruh (disliked) not to remind him. So the default is that reminding is recommended.
The term 'kuriha' here indicates makruh tanzihi: it is better to avoid not reminding, but not sinful.
2
Recognize the exception
The ruling changes if the forgetful person has no strength (lam yakun lahu quwwah). In that case, it is better not to remind him.
The reason given is that reminding would cut off rizq and kindness.
3
Identify the criterion
The decisive factor is weakness (al-da'f), not old age. The passage explicitly says the restriction to old age in some texts is coincidental, not exclusive.
Al-Kamal (Ibn al-Humam) clarified that even a young person who is weak falls under the exception.
4
Apply the rule
In practice, assess the faster's ability: if they can complete the fast without harm, remind them; if fasting would weaken them and eating would help them worship, do not remind.
The ruling is a mercy (lutf) and considers the person's overall ability to perform worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يُذَكِّرُهُ به من رآه يأكلُ، وَكُرِهَ عدم تذكيرِهِ. وإن لم يكن له قوةٌ فالأولى عدم تذكيرِهِ، لما فيه من قطع الرزق واللطف به، سواء كان شيخاً أو شاباً، فالمدار عـلـى الضعف، والتقييد بالشيخوخة في بعض العبادات ليس احترازياً، بل اتفاقياً ولذا تركه « الكمال»، فقال: وإن كان بحال يضعف بالصوم ولو أكل يتقوى على سائر الطاعات يسعه أن لا يخبره انتهى.
One who sees him eating reminds him, and it is disliked (makruh) not to remind him. If he has no strength, then it is better not to remind him, because that would cut off provision and kindness to him, whether he is old or young, so the criterion is weakness. Restricting to old age in some acts of worship is not exclusive but coincidental, and therefore al-Kamal left it and said: If he is in a state where he would weaken by fasting but by eating he gains strength for other acts of worship, it is permissible for him not to inform him. End.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage discusses the etiquette of reminding a forgetful faster. The ruling is obtained from the earlier matn: if the forgetful person has the ability to complete the fast without hardship, it is recommended (mustahabb) to remind him, and it is makruh tanzihi to abstain from reminding. However, if he is weak (e.g., elderly or ill) and eating would strengthen him for other worship, it is better not to remind, as it would be a kindness and allow him to benefit from the provision. The author notes that the criterion is weakness, not old age per se. This is a practical legal guideline within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
كُرِهَ (kuriha): Disliked (makruh tanzihi), i.e., it is better to avoid but not sinful to do.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, in which situation is it better NOT to remind a forgetful faster?
A) When the forgetful person is old.
B) When the forgetful person is weak and would benefit from eating.
C) When the forgetful person is young and strong.
D) When the forgetful person is a close relative.

Key Takeaway
Reminding a forgetful faster is recommended if they are strong, but if they are weak, it is better to remain silent as a kindness.

Looking, Thinking, Oil, and Kohl Do Not Break the Fast
Rulings on four non-invalidating actions in fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four actions that do not invalidate the fast according to the Hanafi school and explain the reasoning and proof for each.

Core Idea
Looking, thinking, oil, and kohl do not break the fast—only direct entry or sexual contact invalidates.

Fasting is an act of worship with specific nullifiers. This passage from Imdad al-Fattah clarifies four actions that students might mistakenly think break the fast. We will examine each, uncover the underlying principle, and see the proof for kohl from the Prophet's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four actions
List the four actions explicitly mentioned: looking (with ejaculation), thinking (with ejaculation), applying oil, and applying kohl. Note the ruling: the fast remains valid.
The text also discusses bestiality and two women's acts as related sub-rulings.
2
Understand the general principle
The fast is only broken by something entering through a natural orifice (e.g., mouth, nose) or by direct sexual intercourse. Looking and thinking do not involve physical entry; oil and kohl enter through pores or the eye, which have no passage to the stomach.
This principle distinguishes these actions from eating, drinking, or intercourse.
3
Examine the proof for kohl
The text cites the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) applied kohl while fasting. Additionally, there is no direct passage from the eye to the brain or throat, so the taste or color reaching the throat does not break the fast.
This dalil is a direct action of the Prophet, indicating permissibility.
4
Note the dispute on bestiality ejaculation
Early scholars Abu al-Qasim and Abu Bakr held that ejaculation by bestiality does not break the fast. The majority of later Hanafis, including al-Sighnaqi, gave fatwa that it does break, though without kaffara. This is the mufta bihi position.
The dispute highlights the ijtihad process within the school.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi rulings
The relied-upon position: looking, thinking, oil, and kohl do not break the fast. Ejaculation by bestiality breaks the fast (no kaffara), and two women engaging in sexual activity with ejaculation requires qada'.
These rulings apply to both Ramadan and voluntary fasts.

Source Text

(أوِ اكْتَحَلَ) لم يفسد صومه، (ولوْ وَجَدَ طَعَمَهُ) أي: طعم الكحل (في حَلْقِهِ) أو لـونـه فـي نخامته أو بزاقه في الأصح كما في «الفتح» وهو قول الأكثر ، كما في ( الكفاية» انتهى. لما روي عن عائشة أنه : ( اكْتَحَلَ وَهُوَ صَائِمٌ » (٣) وليس بين العين والدماغ مسلك، والدمع يخرج بالترشح
Or he ejaculates by looking at a woman's genitals, it does not break the fast. And it is said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: ejaculation by kissing an animal or touching its genitals does not break the fast without dispute, similarly in al-Bahr from al-Dhakhira. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Muhit: there is dispute regarding ejaculation by having intercourse with an animal; if he does not ejaculate, it does not break the fast without dispute. I say: the narration of consensus on no break by ejaculation via touching an animal etc. is problematic with the issue of masturbation by hand. Shaykh al-Imam Abu al-Qasim and Faqih Abu Bakr said: it does not break his fast; the majority of our shaykhs preferred and gave fatwa that it does break. In al-Sighnaqi it is the chosen view. In al-Khulasa: no kaffara upon him, and this act is not permissible outside Ramadan if he intends to fulfill desire; if he intends to calm it, I hope there is no sin upon him. End. He sins if he persists. The Imam was asked about this act and said: head for head. It is said: he is rewarded if he fears desire; so in al-Kifaya from al-Waqi'at. Or he thinks, so it does not break by ejaculation through thinking, just as ejaculation by mere looking. Even if he continues looking and thinking until ejaculation because no form or meaning of intercourse has occurred from him, which is ejaculation by direct contact, and prohibition does not entail breaking the fast, as in al-Fath. Two women if they do the act of men intentionally and no ejaculation occurs, the fast is not broken due to absence of the nullifier; otherwise they have to make qada', as in al-Tajnīs. Or he applies oil, it does not break because there is no contradiction; entering through pores does not contradict it, just as if he bathes in cold water and feels its coolness in his liver. Or he applies kohl, it does not break his fast even if he tastes its taste in his throat or sees its color in his phlegm or saliva, according to the most correct view, as in al-Fath, which is the majority view, as in al-Kifaya. End. Due to what is narrated from Aisha that he applied kohl while fasting, and there is no passage between the eye and brain; tears come out by oozing.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage covers four actions that do not break the fast: (1) ejaculation by looking, (2) ejaculation by thinking, (3) applying oil to the body or hair, and (4) applying kohl to the eyes, even if its taste reaches the throat. The key reasoning is that these actions do not involve direct entry of a substance into the body through an open cavity (the stomach or brain), and they do not entail the form or meaning of sexual intercourse. The author notes a dispute regarding ejaculation by touching an animal or bestiality; some early scholars said it does not break the fast, but the majority of later Hanafis gave fatwa that it does break, making it the relied-upon position. However, even then no kaffara is required. Similarly, two women engaging in sexual activity without ejaculation does not break the fast; with ejaculation, qada' is required. The proof for kohl is the action of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as narrated by Aisha, and the anatomical reasoning that the eye does not have a direct passage to the brain. This section is a straightforward statement of rulings with some intra-Hanafi dispute noted.

Key Terms
ادَّهَنَ (iddahana): To apply oil to the body or hair, which does not break the fast because it enters through pores, not a digestive orifice.
اكْتَحَلَ (iktahala): To apply kohl to the eyes; the fast is not broken even if its taste reaches the throat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) His fast is valid only if he did not intend to taste it.

Key Takeaway
Looking, thinking, applying oil, and applying kohl do not break the fast; only direct entry into the body cavity or direct sexual intercourse invalidates the fast.

Ruling on Reminding a Forgetful Eater in Ramadan
Ruling on reminding a forgetful eater in Ramadan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reminding a forgetful fasting person and apply the exception for a weak person.

Core Idea
Reminding a forgetful faster is recommended; not reminding is disliked, but for the weak, not reminding is better.

The ruling on reminding a forgetful eater during Ramadan involves a balance between preserving the fast and showing kindness. The basic rule is that one who sees a fasting person eating forgetfully should remind him, and it is disliked to remain silent. However, if the person is weak and needs the nourishment, it is better not to interrupt him. This lesson examines the preferred Hanafi position and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is reminding a forgetful fasting person. The default ruling is mandub (recommended).
The matn states 'yudhakkiruhu' implying a recommended action.
2
Note the exception for weakness
If the person is weak, the ruling becomes that not reminding is better (afdal).
The criterion is weakness, not age.
3
Examine the evidence
The text cites 'kurihat 'adam tadhkirihi' (disliked not to remind) for the strong, and 'fa al-ula 'adam tadhkirihi' (better not to remind) for the weak.
These phrases establish the default and exception.
4
Understand the dispute
Some Hanafi scholars say not to inform because the fast is not broken; the preferred view is to remind.
The minority opinion is recorded in Majma' al-Riwayat.
5
Apply the ruling when reminded person does not remember
If reminded and still does not recall, he must make up the fast, as one person's testimony is sufficient in religious matters.
This is mentioned in al-Tajnis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يُذَكِّرُهُ به من رآه يأكلُ، وَكُرِهَ عدم تذكيرِهِ. وإن لم يكن له قوةٌ فالأولى عدم تذكيره. كشاب قوي (يذكره به من رآه يأكل، و) إن تركه كره عدم تذكيره في المختار، كذا في «الفتح»، وقيل: من رأى غيره في رمضان يأكل ناسياً لا يخبره لأن بأكله هذا لا يفسد صومه، كذا في «مجمع الروايات»، وإذا ذكر الناسي وهو يأكل فقيل له: إنك صائم، فلم يتذكر يلزمه القضاء في المختار، لأن قول الواحد حجة في الديانات، كذا في «التجنيس». (وإن لم يكن له قوة فالأولى عدم تذكيره) لما فيه من قطع الرزق واللطف به، سواء كان شيخاً أو شاباً، فالمدار على الضعف، والتقييد بالشيخوخة في بعض العبادات ليس احترازياً، بل اتفاقياً، ولذا تركه الكمال، فقال: وإن كان بحال يضعف بالصوم ولو أكل يتقوى على سائر الطاعات يسعه أن لا يخبره انتهى.
He reminds him, one who sees him eating, and it is disliked to not remind him. And if he has no strength, then it is better not to remind him. — A young strong [man] (he reminds him, one who sees him eating, and) if he leaves it, not reminding him is disliked in al-Mukhtar, likewise in al-Fath. And it is said: Whoever sees another in Ramadan eating forgetfully should not inform him, because by this eating he does not break his fast, likewise in Majma' al-Riwayat. And if the forgetful one is reminded while he is eating and it is said to him: You are fasting, and he does not remember, he must make up [the fast] in al-Mukhtar, because the statement of one is proof in religious matters, likewise in al-Tajnis. (And if he has no strength, then it is better not to remind him) because it cuts off provision and (shows) kindness to him, whether he is old or young; the criterion is weakness, and the restriction to old age in some devotions is not exclusive but coincidental. Hence al-Kamal left it and said: If he is in a condition of weakness due to fasting, and if he eats he gains strength for other acts of obedience, it is permissible for him not to inform him. End.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on reminding a forgetful eater in Ramadan. The matn states the basic rule: it is recommended to remind, and not reminding is disliked. However, if the person is weak and needs the food to sustain himself for fasting or other acts of worship, it is better not to remind. The commentary adds that this applies to both old and young; the key factor is weakness. There is a difference of opinion: some scholars say not to inform because the fast is not broken anyway. The preferred position (mukhtar) is to remind, and if reminded the person does not remember, he must make up the fast, as one person's testimony is sufficient in religious matters. This is the Hanafi view as per al-Fath and al-Mukhtar.

Key Terms
تذكير (tadhkir): Reminding someone of something they have forgotten; here, reminding a fasting person that he is fasting while he eats forgetfully.
ناسي (nasi): A person who forgets; here, one who forgets he is fasting and eats.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Reminding a forgetful faster is recommended, but for a weak person, it is better not to remind.

Ejaculation by Looking or Thinking in Fasting: Ruling and Rationale
Ruling on ejaculation due to looking, thinking, or prolonged contemplation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ejaculation caused by looking or thinking while fasting and explain the legal reasoning (istihsan) that distinguishes it from direct physical stimulation.

Core Idea
Ejaculation by looking or thinking does not break the fast; only direct contact invalidates.

In this lesson, we examine a well-known Hanafi ruling: a fasting person who ejaculates due to looking at a private part or due to lustful thoughts does not break his fast. This ruling may seem counterintuitive, but it is grounded in the principle that the fast is only invalidated by acts that involve the form or meaning of sexual intercourse, namely direct physical stimulation. The commentary in Imdad al-Fattah expands on this, clarifying the distinction between prohibited acts and nullifiers of the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The matn states that ejaculation by looking or thinking does not break the fast. The commentary affirms this by specifying looking at a woman's private part.
The ruling is on the validity of the fast, not the permissibility of the act.
2
Locate the key principle
The fast is broken only by the form or meaning of intercourse (surah al-jima' wa ma'anahu). Looking/thinking lacks both.
Direct physical contact (mubasharah) is the key element.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Ejaculation by looking/thinking occurs without direct stimulation, so it does not constitute a nullifier.
The prohibition of the act (hurmah) does not entail nullification (ifsad).
4
Apply to continuation
Even if one continues looking or thinking, the ruling remains the same; no nullification occurs.
Prolonged contemplation does not change the legal status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ أَنْزَلَ بِنَظَرِ. أَوْ فِكْرِ، وإنْ أَدَامَ النظر والفِكْرَ أَوْ اذْهَنَ أَوِ اكْتَحَلَ؛ ولَوْ وَجَدَ طَعْمُهُ في حَلْقِه. (أَوْ أَنْزَلَ بِنَظَرِ ) إلى فرج امرأة لم يفسد ، وقال في « التاتارخانية»: أنزل بتقبيل بهيمة، أو بمـس فــرج البهيمة لا يفسد صومه، بلا خلاف، وكذا في ( البحر » عن « الذخيرة انتهى. وفي ( التاتارخانية» عن « المحيط » : اختلف فيما لو أنزل بإيتان البهيمة، وإن لم ينزل لا يفسد صومه بلا خلاف، قلت: وحكاية الاتفاق على عدم الإفساد بالإنزال بمس البهيمة ونحوه مشكلة بمسألة الاستمناء بالكف. قال الشيخ الإمام «أبو القاسم» والفقيه « أبو بكر » : لا يفسد صومه، وعامة مشايخنا استحسنوا وأفتوا بفساده، وفي « السغناقي» هو المختار وفي ( الخلاصة : ولا كفارة عليه، ولا يحل هذا الفعل خارج رمضان أيضاً إن قصد قضاء الشهوة، وإن قصد تسكينها أرجو أن لا يكون عليه وبال انتهى. ويأثم إذا داوم عليه، وسئل الإمام عن ذلك الفعل فقال: رأساً برأس، وقيل: يؤجر إذا خاف الشهوة، كذا في « الكفاية » عن « الواقعات» (أو فكر)، فلا يفسد بالإنزال به كالإنزال بمجرد النظر، (وإنْ أَدَامَ النَّظَرَ والفِكْرَ) حتى أنزل لأنه لم يوجد منه صورة الجماع ولا معناه، وهو الإنزال عن مباشرة، ولا يلزم من الحرمة الإفطار، كذا في «الفتح» ، والمرأتان إذا عملتا عمل الرجال يعني: عمداً ولم يحصل إنزال لا يفسد الصوم لانعدام المفطر، وإلا فعليهما القضاء، كذا في « التجنيس»
Or he ejaculates by looking, or by thinking, and if he continues looking and thinking, or applies oil, or applies kohl, even if he finds its taste in his throat. (Or he ejaculates by looking) at a woman's private part does not break [the fast]. And it is said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If he ejaculates by kissing an animal, or by touching an animal's private part, his fast is not broken, without dispute, likewise in al-Bahr from al-Dhakhira. End. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Muhit: There is difference regarding if he ejaculates by having intercourse with an animal; if he does not ejaculate, his fast is not broken without dispute. I say: The narration of consensus on non-breaking by ejaculation from touching an animal and the like is problematic with the issue of masturbation with the hand. Shaykh al-Imam Abu al-Qasim and al-Faqih Abu Bakr said: His fast is not broken. And the generality of our mashayikh considered it good and gave fatwa that it breaks. And in al-Sighnaqi it is the chosen view. And in al-Khulasa: There is no kaffarah upon him, and this act is not permissible outside Ramadan either if he intends to fulfill desire; if he intends to calm it, I hope there is no evil upon him. End. And he sins if he persists. And the Imam was asked about that act and said: Head for head. And it is said: He is rewarded if he fears desire, likewise in al-Kifaya from al-Waqi'at. (Or by thinking), so it does not break by ejaculation from it, like ejaculation by mere looking. (And if he continues looking and thinking) until he ejaculates, because neither the form nor the meaning of intercourse occurred from him, and ejaculation from direct contact [is not present], and the prohibition does not necessitate breaking the fast, likewise in al-Fath. And two women if they do the act of men, meaning intentionally and ejaculation does not occur, the fast is not broken due to absence of the nullifier; otherwise, they must make up, likewise in al-Tajnis.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage addresses ejaculation during fasting. The matn states that ejaculation by looking or by thinking (i.e., without physical contact) does not break the fast, even if one continues looking or thinking. The commentary expands on this: looking at a woman's private part and ejaculating does not break the fast. Similarly, ejaculation from kissing or touching an animal's private part does not break according to explicit texts, but there is a dispute regarding intercourse with an animal. The author notes an inconsistency with the ruling on masturbation with the hand, where the majority of later scholars (mashayikh) gave fatwa that it does break the fast, though some early authorities said it does not. The chosen view (mukhtar) in al-Sighnaqi is that it breaks. There is no kaffarah (expiation) but the act is sinful if done intentionally, especially if repeated. The passage also clarifies that if a woman performs a sexual act with another woman and ejaculation does not occur, the fast is not broken; if it does occur, qada is required. The legal reasoning is that the fast is only broken by the form or meaning of intercourse, which is absent in looking or thinking; the prohibition of such acts does not automatically invalidate the fast.

Key Terms
إنزال (inzal): Ejaculation; the emission of seminal fluid, which may or may not break the fast depending on the cause.
فكر (fikr): Thinking or contemplation; here, thinking lustful thoughts that lead to ejaculation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A fasting man looks at a non-mahram woman and ejaculates. According to the Hanafi position in this text, what is the ruling on his fast?
A) The fast is broken and he must make it up with kaffarah.
B) The fast is broken and he must make it up without kaffarah.
C) The fast is not broken, but the act is sinful.
D) The fast is not broken and the act is permissible if he fears fitnah.

Key Takeaway
Ejaculation by looking or thinking does not invalidate the fast; only direct physical contact does.

Controversies: Animal Contact and Masturbation in Fasting
Detailed rulings on animal contact and masturbation in fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on ejaculation caused by animal contact and masturbation, and identify the intra-madhhab difference on masturbation.

Core Idea
Hanafis hold that ejaculation from animal contact does not break the fast, but masturbation does — though early scholars dissented.

This lesson expands on the previous ruling by addressing borderline cases: kissing or touching an animal's private part, intercourse with an animal, and masturbation with the hand. The text presents a dispute among Hanafi scholars on masturbation, with the later majority ruling that it breaks the fast. Understanding these distinctions clarifies the principle that only direct intentional stimulation invalidates the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The text discusses three types of contact: kissing/touching animal private part, intercourse with animal, and masturbation (istimna' bi-l-kaf).
Also includes case of two women engaging in sexual acts.
2
Note the consensus on animal contact
Ejaculation by kissing or touching an animal's private part does not break the fast, according to explicit texts (bila khilaf).
Intercourse with animal is disputed if ejaculation occurs.
3
Examine the dispute on masturbation
Early scholars (Abu al-Qasim, Abu Bakr) held it does not break; later mashayikh preferred the view that it does, and this is the mukhtar.
The author notes an inconsistency with the animal case.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Masturbation involves direct manual stimulation, analogous to intercourse, while animal contact (like looking/thinking) lacks directness.
The later scholars applied istihsan to break the fast in masturbation.
5
Apply to the two women case
If two women engage in acts and one ejaculates, she must make up the fast; if no ejaculation, fast valid.
This confirms that ejaculation from any direct sexual act (even between women) nullifies.

Applying Oil or Kohl During Fasting
Rulings with proof on oil and kohl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on applying oil or kohl during fasting and explain why taste reaching the throat does not break the fast, citing the Hanafi proof.

Core Idea
Kohl and oil do not break the fast even if taste is perceived, because no substance enters the stomach through the normal route.

This lesson examines two common concerns during fasting: applying oil to the body and kohl to the eyes. Many assume that if the taste of kohl reaches the throat, the fast is broken. The Hanafi school, relying on the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) and the principle of pore entry, rules that neither act invalidates the fast. We will explore the textual proof and the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Applying oil and kohl. The ruling: they do not break the fast.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of Aisha: the Prophet (ﷺ) applied kohl while fasting.
This is a dalil naqli (textual proof).
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? The explicit action indicates permissibility. Also, the absence of a direct passage from eye to stomach.
4
Understand the principle of masam (pores)
What enters through pores does not break the fast, similar to finding coolness from a bath.
This is a distinct Hanafi concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(أو ادَّهَـنَ) لم يفسد ، لعدم المنافي له، والداخل من المسام لا ينافيه، كما لو اغتسل بالماء البارد ووجـد بــرده في كبده. (أوِ اكْتَحَلَ) لم يفسد صومه، (ولوْ وَجَدَ طَعَمَهُ) أي: طعم الكحل (في حَلْقِهِ) أو لـونـه فـي نخامته أو بزاقه في الأصح كما في «الفتح» وهو قول الأكثر ، كما في ( الكفاية» انتهى. لما روي عن عائشة أنه : ( اكْتَحَلَ وَهُوَ صَائِمٌ ) (٣) وليس بين العين والدماغ مسلك، والدمع يخرج بالترشح كالعرق، والداخل من المسام لا ينافيه، كما ذكرناه، ولو وضع في عينيه لبناً أو دواء مع الدهن فوجد طعمه أو مرارته في حلقه لا يفسد صومه، كذا في البحر» عن « الظهيرية»، لأن إحدى المفطرات الثلاث لم يوجد، ولا يقال: هو تعليل بالعدم وأنه لا يجوز إذا لم يكن مختصاً؛ لأنـه مختص بالنظر إلى الأصل، كما في المستصفى ولو ابتلع طعاماً مشدوداً بخيــط والخيط بيده لا يفسد ما لم يسقط من الخيط، وإن سقط فسد، كذا في ( الكفاية » و ( البحر ) . وفي « التاتارخانية» عن العتابية » : إذا ابتلع عنباً مربوطاً بخيط ثم أخرجه لم يفطر، ولو أدخل حلقه خشبة أو نحوها وطرف منه في يده لم يفطر، وكذا إذا أدخل إصبعه في إسته والمرأة في فرجها على المختار، إلا أن تكون مبتلة بالماء أو الدهن
(Or if he applies oil) it does not break the fast, because there is nothing contradictory to it, and what enters through pores does not contradict it, just as if he bathes with cold water and finds its coolness in his liver. (Or if he applies kohl) his fast is not broken, (even if he finds its taste) i.e., the taste of the kohl (in his throat) or its color in his sputum or saliva, according to the most correct view, as in al-Fath, and it is the opinion of the majority, as in al-Kifaya. End. Because of the narration from Aisha that he (ﷺ) applied kohl while fasting. And there is no passage between the eye and the brain, and tears exit by oozing like sweat, and what enters through pores does not oppose it, as we mentioned. And if he puts milk or medicine with oil in his eyes and finds its taste or bitterness in his throat, his fast is not broken, likewise in al-Bahr from al-Dhahiriyya, because one of the three nullifiers is not present. And it is not said: This is a negative proof, and it is not valid if it is not specific, because it is specific with consideration to the original, as in al-Mustasfa. And if he swallows food tied to a thread with the thread in his hand, his fast is not broken as long as nothing falls from the thread; if it falls, it breaks, likewise in al-Kifaya and al-Bahr. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Atabiyya: If he swallows a grape tied to a thread and then pulls it out, he does not break his fast. And if he inserts a piece of wood or similar into his throat with its end in his hand, he does not break. Similarly, if he inserts his finger into his anus, or a woman into her vagina, according to the chosen view, unless they are wet with water or oil.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage covers two related issues: applying oil (e.g., to the body or hair) and applying kohl (antimony) to the eyes during fasting. The matn states that these do not break the fast. The commentary explains that oil does not break because no substance enters the stomach through a normal route; what enters through pores is not considered breaking. For kohl, even if the taste or color reaches the throat, the fast remains valid, as reported from Aisha that the Prophet (ﷺ) used kohl while fasting. The reasoning is that there is no direct passage from the eye to the brain; tears come out by oozing. Similarly, if one puts milk or medicine in the eyes and tastes it, the fast is not broken because the three nullifiers (food, drink, and sexual intercourse) are absent. The passage then discusses related cases: swallowing food tied to a thread does not break unless part detaches; inserting a foreign object into the throat or anus does not break as long as nothing is introduced that remains (like a wet finger). This is based on the Hanafi principle that the fast is only broken by something that reaches the stomach through the normal digestive passage (pharynx/esophagus) in a manner deemed equivalent to eating or drinking.

Key Terms
كحل (kuhl): Antimony or any substance applied to the eyes as cosmetic or medicine.
مسام (masam): Pores of the skin; what enters through pores (like water during washing) does not reach the stomach and thus does not break the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A fasting person applies kohl to his eyes and later tastes it in his throat. What is the ruling on his fast according to the Hanafi school?
A) The fast is broken because something entered the body.
B) The fast is broken only if he swallowed it intentionally.
C) The fast remains valid, as kohl does not break the fast.
D) The fast is broken and he must make it up.

Key Takeaway
Kohl and oil do not break the fast, even if their taste is perceived in the throat, because there is no direct passage to the stomach and the Prophet (ﷺ) practiced it.

Cupping, Backbiting, and Intention: What Does Not Break the Fast
Rulings on non-breaking actions with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of the three actions (cupping, backbiting, intention to break) and cite the proof for each.

Core Idea
Cupping, backbiting, and mere intention do not break the fast — only actual consumption of food or drink does.

This lesson clarifies common misconceptions about actions that might be thought to nullify the fast. The Hanafi school relies on prophetic practice and legal analogy to confirm that these actions, while potentially sinful, do not invalidate the obligation of fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify each action and its ruling
The text lists three actions: cupping, backbiting, and intending to break the fast. For each, the ruling is that the fast remains valid.
2
For cupping: locate the primary proof
The proof is the Prophet's (ﷺ) own action: he cupped while fasting (Sahih al-Bukhari). This explicit act is the strongest evidence that cupping does not break the fast.
The hadith 'the cupper and the cupped break their fast' is interpreted as referring to loss of reward, not invalidation.
3
For backbiting: reinterpret the contrary hadith
The same hadith about cupping is reinterpreted: the Prophet said it when he saw two people backbiting, meaning they lost reward, not that the fast was invalidated. There is consensus that backbiting does not break the fast.
4
For intention: draw analogy with prayer
Intending to break the fast without acting is analogous to intending to speak in prayer without speaking: neither invalidates the act because no action occurred.
This is based on qiyas (analogy) and the principle that rulings depend on actual actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو احتجم: لا يفسد لأنه ﷺ احتجم وهو محرم، واحتجم وهو صائم (رواه البخاري). أو اغتاب: لم يفسد لأن قوله ﷺ: 'أفطر الحاجم والمحجوم' تأويله ذهب أجرهما، لأنه مر بينهما وهما يغتابان آخر فقال ذلك: أي ذهب أجرهما بالغيبة لأنه سوى بينهما، ولا خلاف أنه لا يفسد صوم الحاجم. أو نوى الفطر ولم يفطر: كما لو نوى أن يتكلم في صلاته ولم يتكلم لم تفسد لعدم الفعل. أو دخل حلقه دخان بلا صنعه: لعدم قدرته على الامتناع عنه، فصار كبلل يبقى في فمه بعد المضمضة لدخوله من الأنف إذا أطبق الفم، ونظيره ما في الخزانة: إذا دخل عرقه، أو دموعه، حلقه وهو قليل كقطرة أو قطرتين لا يفطر، وإن أكثر بحيث يجد ملوحته في الحلق فسد، قال الكمال: وفيه نظر لأن القطرة يجد ملوحتها، فالأولى عندي الاعتبار بوجدان الملوحة بصحيح الحس، لأنه لا ضرورة في أكثر من ذلك القدر، وما في فتاوى قاضي خان: لو دخل دمعه أو عرق جبينه، أو دم رعافه في حلقه فسد صومه انتهى. يوافـق مـا ذكرته، فإنه علق بوصوله إلى الحلق بمجرد وجدان الملوحة دليل ذلك انتهى. وقال شيخ مشايخنا العلامة المقدسي رحمه الله : أقول: القطرة لقلتها لا يجد طعمها في الحلق، لتلاشيها قبل الوصول إليه، ولعله أراد بوجدان ملوحتها ذاتها على سبيل الكناية؛ لأن الصفة لازمة للموصوف، وإلا فليس الحلق مدركاً للطعوم كما هو معلوم، وإنما أراد أنها إذا انتشرت في الفم فوصلت إلى الحـلــق فـقـد
Or if he cupped himself: it does not break [the fast] because he (ﷺ) cupped while in ihram and cupped while fasting (narrated by Bukhari). Or if he backbit: it does not break because his (ﷺ) saying 'The cupper and the cupped break their fast' is interpreted as 'their reward is lost', because he passed by them while they were backbiting another and said that: i.e., their reward is lost due to backbiting, because he made them equal. And there is no dispute that the fast of the cupper is not broken. Or if he intended to break the fast and did not break: just as if he intended to speak in his prayer and did not speak, it does not become invalid due to lack of action. Or if smoke enters his throat without his doing: because he is unable to avoid it, so it is like the moisture that remains in the mouth after rinsing, due to its entry from the nose when he closes the mouth. Its analogy is what is in al-Khazana: if his sweat or tears enter his throat, and it is little, like a drop or two, he does not break his fast; if it is more, such that he finds its saltiness in the throat, it breaks. Al-Kamal said: There is consideration in this, because a drop has its saltiness found, so the better view in my opinion is to consider finding the saltiness with sound senses, because there is no necessity for more than that amount. And what is in Fatawa Qadi Khan: If his tear or forehead sweat or nosebleed enters his throat, his fast is broken. End. This agrees with what I mentioned, for he tied it to reaching the throat merely by finding saltiness, evidence of that. End. And the shaykh of our mashayikh, al-Allamah al-Maqdisi, said: I say: The drop, due to its smallness, does not have its taste found in the throat, because it dissipates before reaching it. Perhaps he intended by 'finding its saltiness' itself as a metonymy, because the attribute is inherent to the described; otherwise, the throat is not a perceiver of tastes as is known. Rather, he meant that when it spreads in the mouth and reaches the throat, then...
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage covers four separate actions that do not break the fast. First, cupping (hijama): the Prophet (ﷺ) cupped while fasting, so it is permissible and does not invalidate the fast. A hadith that says 'the cupper and the cupped break their fast' is interpreted as referring to loss of reward if they engage in backbiting, not actual nullification. Second, backbiting (ghiba): although a major sin, it does not break the fast, as the hadith is metaphorical. Third, intending to break the fast (niyyat al-fitr) without actually eating or drinking: just as intention alone does not invalidate prayer, it does not invalidate the fast. Fourth, smoke entering the throat involuntarily: if one cannot avoid it, the fast remains valid. The commentary discusses a related issue: if one's own sweat or tears enter the throat, if it is a small amount (like a drop or two), the fast is not broken; if it is more, and one perceives saltiness, it breaks. There is a difference of opinion: al-Kamal considers the perception of saltiness as the criterion, while others argue a drop dissipates and cannot be tasted. The passage ends with a discussion from al-Maqdisi that the throat is not a taste organ, and the sensation might be metaphorical.

Key Terms
احتجم (ihtajama): The act of cupping (hijama), drawing blood by suction; the Prophet did it while fasting.
غيبة (ghiba): Backbiting, speaking ill of someone in their absence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a fasting person who intends to break his fast but does not actually eat or drink?
A) The fast is invalid because intention is enough.
B) The fast remains valid because no action occurred.
C) The fast is invalid but he can continue as if nothing happened.
D) The fast is valid only if he regrets the intention.

Key Takeaway
Actions that do not involve actual consumption of food, drink, or their substitutes do not break the Hanafi fast, even if they are sinful.

Involuntary Substances: Smoke, Sweat, and Tears During Fasting
Detailed ruling on smoke, sweat, and tears with internal Hanafi debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between voluntary and involuntary entry of substances and apply the Hanafi threshold for when sweat or tears break the fast.

Core Idea
Involuntary smoke does not break the fast; sweat/tears break only if they are more than a drop and one perceives saltiness.

This lesson tackles nuanced cases where substances enter the throat without direct eating or drinking. The Hanafi school uses the principle of ability to avoid and the concept of 'perceived saltiness' as a criterion for nullification, with some internal debate among scholars.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle for smoke
Smoke that enters the throat without one's action does not break the fast because one cannot avoid it, analogous to residual moisture after rinsing or smoke via the nose when the mouth is closed.
The key is the absence of choice (ikhtiyar).
2
Identify the issue with sweat/tears
When one's own sweat or tears enter the throat, the ruling depends on quantity: if a small amount (like a drop or two) and no taste is perceived, the fast is not broken. If more and one perceives saltiness, it breaks.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Al-Kamal objects that even a drop can be tasted, so perception alone is unreliable. He prefers using 'sound senses' to detect saltiness, implying that a drop might not break. Al-Maqdisi argues that the throat does not taste; 'finding saltiness' is a metonym for the spread of fluid.
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) leans toward a minimal amount not breaking, but with caution.
4
Apply the ruling to a modern case
Inhalation of polluted air or accidental swallowing of a small tear drop: apply the same principle of involuntariness and quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Three Actions That Do Not Break the Fast
Explaining three actions that do not break the fast with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cupping, backbiting, and intention to break fast, and cite the proof for each.

Core Idea
Cupping, backbiting, and mere intention do not invalidate the fast; only physical consumption breaks it.

This lesson examines three common actions that students often mistakenly believe break the fast. The Hanafi school, relying on authentic hadith and qiyas, confirms that the fast remains valid in all three cases. However, note that backbiting is a major sin even though it does not nullify the fast physically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three actions and their ruling
Read the source passage and note the three actions: cupping (hijamah), backbiting (ghibah), and intention to break fast (niyat al-fitr). The text states that none of these invalidate the fast.
2
Examine the proof for cupping
The proof is a hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari: the Prophet ﷺ cupped while fasting and while in ihram. This shows cupping is permissible and does not break the fast.
Contrast with other schools that may consider cupping a nullifier.
3
Examine the proof for backbiting
The hadith 'The cupper and the cupped have broken fast' is interpreted metaphorically: the reward is lost due to sin, not the fast itself. The text emphasizes there is consensus that the cupper's fast is not broken.
Backbiting is sinful but does not physically break the fast.
4
Examine the proof for intention to break
Mere intention without action does not invalidate, by analogy (qiyas) to intending to speak in prayer without actually speaking.
This principle is consistent across schools.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position
Combine the three rulings: the fast remains valid (sahih). The common thread is that only actual, physical consumption or its direct equivalent nullifies the fast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أوِ احْتَجَمَ) لا يفسد لأنه : ( احْتَجَمَ وَهُوَ مُحْرِمٌ، وَاحْتَجَمَ وَهُوَ صَائِمٌ ( رواه البخاري. (أوِ اغْتَابَ) لم يفسد لأن قوله : ( أفْطَرَ الحَاجِمُ وَالمَحْجُومُ ، ) تأويله ذهب أجرهما، لأنه مر بينهما وهما يغتابان آخر فقال ذلك :أي: ذهب أجرهما بالغيبة لأنه سـوى بينهما، ولا خلاف أنه لا يفسد صوم الحاجم (أو نوى الفِطْرَ. ولم يُفْطِر) كما لـو نــوى أن يتكلم في صلاته ولم يتكلم لم تفسد لعدم الفعل.
(Cupping) does not break [the fast] because he cupped while in ihram and he cupped while fasting, narrated by Bukhari. (Backbiting) does not break [the fast] because his statement 'The cupper and the cupped have broken fast' is interpreted as their reward has gone, because he passed by them while they were backbiting another, so he said that, meaning their reward went due to backbiting because he equated between them, and there is no dispute that the cupper's fast is not broken. (Intention to break fast without actually breaking) [does not break], just as if one intends to speak in prayer but does not speak, it does not invalidate due to lack of action.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct rulings from the Hanafi school, each concerning actions that do not invalidate the fast. First, cupping (hijamah) is permissible for the fasting person; the proof is the hadith where the Prophet ﷺ cupped while fasting and while in ihram. Second, backbiting (ghibah) is a sin but does not break the fast; the hadith about the cupper and cupped breaking fast is interpreted metaphorically as loss of reward. Third, merely intending to break the fast without carrying out the action does not break the fast, analogous to intending to speak in prayer without doing so. All three rulings are based on the principle that the fast is only invalidated by specific, physical acts that are explicitly categorized as nullifiers.

Key Terms
حجامة (hijamah): Cupping, a medical procedure involving bloodletting; the text rules that it does not break the fast.
غِيبة (ghibah): Backbiting, speaking ill of an absent person; a sin but not a nullifier of the fast.
نية الفطر (niyat al-fitr): Intention to break the fast without actual consumption; does not invalidate the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It invalidates the fast because it removes blood.
B) It does not invalidate the fast because the Prophet ﷺ did it while fasting.
C) It invalidates only if the cupper swallows blood.
D) It is makruh but does not break the fast.

Key Takeaway
The fast is only broken by actual physical consumption; cupping, backbiting, and mere intention do not nullify it.

Involuntary Entry of Smoke, Dust, and Particles Does Not Break the Fast; Intentional Smoke Does
Clarifying that involuntary entry of particles does not invalidate fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between involuntary and intentional entry of substances during fasting and state the Hanafi ruling on each, including the exceptions for smoke, dust, sweat, and tears.

Core Idea
If a particle enters the throat without your action and cannot be avoided, the fast remains valid; if you intentionally inhale it, the fast is broken.

Fasting requires abstaining from food, drink, and anything that enters the body intentionally. But what about smoke from a neighbour's fire, dust from a mill, or a fly that flies into your mouth? The Hanafi school addresses these scenarios with a clear principle: that which enters without your doing and cannot be avoided does not break the fast. This lesson explores the details and warns against common oversights like intentionally inhaling incense smoke.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling category
The passage deals with substances that may enter the throat of a fasting person. The overall ruling is that if entry is involuntary and unavoidable, the fast remains valid (sahih).
2
Locate the general principle
The key principle is 'bila san'ihi' (without his action) and inability to avoid. The text analogizes smoke to moisture remaining after rinsing because the person had no control.
Dalil aqli: analogy based on avoidability.
3
Examine the exceptions: sweat and tears
A small amount (a drop or two) does not break the fast; a larger amount where saltiness is perceived does. Later scholars differ on the threshold, with some focusing on perception of saltiness by sound sense.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the exact criterion.
4
Analyze the caution (tanbih) on intentional smoke
The text explicitly warns that if one intentionally brings smoke into his throat, even incense smoke, the fast is broken. This includes fumigating and inhaling while mindful of the fast.
Common mistake: thinking incense smoke is like perfume.
5
Compare with similar cases
Smelling perfume (musk, rose) does not break the fast because it is air carrying scent, not particles. Kohl and mustache oil also do not break. This reinforces the distinction between substance and air.
Second caution from the text.

Source Text

أنه كشم الورد ومائه والمسك، لوضوح الـفــرق بـيـن هـواء تطيب بريح المسك وشبهه، وبين جوهر دخان وصل إلى جوفه بفعله. تنبيه آخر يؤخذ من مسألة الاكتحال ودهن الشارب أنه لا يكره للصائم شم رائحة المسك والورد ونحوه، مما لا يكون جوهراً متصلاً كالدخان، فإنهم قالوا: لا يكره الاكتحال بحال، وهـو شامل للطيب وغيره، ولم يخصوه بنوع منه، وكذا دهن الشارب (أو) دخل حلقه (غبار ولو) كــان (غبار) دقيق من (الطاحون أو ) دخل حلقه ( ذباب أو ) دخل (أَثَرُ طَعْمِ الأدويـة فـيـه) أي في حلقه لأنه لا يمكن الاحتراز عنها، فلا يفسد الصوم بدخولها (وهو ذاكر لصومِهِ) لما ذكرنا.
(Or if smoke enters his throat without his doing) because he is unable to avoid it, so it is like moisture remaining in his mouth after rinsing due to entering from the nose when the mouth is closed. Its analogy is in al-Khazanah: if his sweat or tears enter his throat, a little like a drop or two, it does not break the fast; if more such that he finds its saltiness in the throat, it invalidates. Al-Kamal said: There is consideration because a drop has saltiness, so the correct view with me is to consider the perception of saltiness by sound sense, because there is no necessity for more than that amount. In Fatawa Qadi Khan: if his tears or forehead sweat or nosebleed blood enters his throat, his fast is invalidated. This agrees with what I mentioned, for he linked it to reaching the throat by merely perceiving saltiness as evidence. The teacher of our teachers, the scholar al-Maqdisi, said: I say: the drop due to its smallness does not find its taste in the throat, because it dissipates before reaching it; perhaps he meant by 'finding its saltiness' itself as a metaphor, because the attribute is attached to the described, otherwise the throat does not perceive tastes as is known; he only meant that when it spreads in the mouth and reaches the throat, it approaches the interior from every side unlike the mouth, which is external. Evidence for this is in al-Dhakhirah and al-Waqi'at by al-Sadr al-Shahid: if tears enter the fasting person's mouth, if little like a drop or two, no, because avoidance is not possible; if much until he finds saltiness in all his mouth and swallows, his fast is broken. Similarly for facial sweat. In the dictation of Muhammad ibn Muqatil, a companion of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan: if his tears fall from his eye to his mouth and enter his throat, it breaks the fast. (End quote from his writing). Caution: We conditioned non-breaking of fast on smoke entering without one's action and we mentioned what follows, as a caution that whoever brings smoke into his throat in any manner of entry, his fast is broken, whether it is ambergris smoke or wood smoke etc., even if one fumigates with incense and brings it to himself and inhales the smoke while mindful of his fast, he breaks his fast, because it is possible to avoid introducing a nullifier into his interior and brain. This is something many people neglect, so be aware of it, and do not think it is like smelling rose, its water, and musk, because the difference is clear between air perfumed with the scent of musk and the like and between the substance of smoke that reaches his interior by his action. Another caution derived from the issue of kohl and mustache oil: it is not disliked for the fasting person to smell the scent of musk, rose, and the like, which are not a continuous substance like smoke, for they said it is not disliked to apply kohl in any case, and it includes perfumed and otherwise, and they did not specify a type; similarly for mustache oil. (Or) if dust enters his throat, even if it is (fine dust) from (the mill), or (a fly) enters his throat, or (the trace of the taste of medicine) in it, i.e., in his throat, because it is not possible to avoid them, so the fast is not invalidated by their entry (while he is mindful of his fast) for the reason we mentioned.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage deals with a set of substances that may enter the throat of a fasting person involuntarily, and provides detailed rulings. The general principle is that if something enters the throat without one's action (bila san'ihi) and it is difficult to avoid, the fast remains valid. This includes smoke, dust, a fly, and the lingering taste of medicine. The text also discusses the case of sweat and tears: a small amount does not break the fast, but a large amount where saltiness is perceived in the throat does. Scholars differ on the exact threshold, with some focusing on the perception of saltiness. A crucial warning (tanbih) distinguishes between involuntary smoke and intentional inhalation: if one actively brings smoke into the throat, even the smoke of incense, the fast is broken. This is a common oversight. The passage also clarifies that smelling perfume like musk or rose does not break the fast because it is merely air carrying scent, not a solid particle. Thus, the rule is based on the ability to avoid the substance: if one can avoid it (like incense smoke) but chooses not to, the fast is invalid; if one cannot (like dust from a mill), it remains valid.

Key Terms
دخان (dukhan): Smoke; if it enters the throat without one's action, the fast is not broken; if intentionally inhaled, it breaks the fast.
غبار (ghubar): Dust, e.g. from a mill; its entry does not break the fast because it cannot be avoided.
أثر طعم الأدوية (athar ta'm al-adwiyah): The lingering taste of medicine in the throat; does not break the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a fasting person who intentionally inhales incense smoke?
A) The fast remains valid because incense is not food.
B) The fast is broken because he could have avoided it and chose to introduce a substance into his body.
C) The fast is broken only if he swallows the smoke; inhaling through the nose is fine.
D) The fast remains valid if he does not taste the smoke.

Key Takeaway
The fast is only broken by entry of substances that are intentional and avoidable; involuntary and unavoidable particles do not nullify the fast.

Fasting While Junub: The Ruling and Proof
Proving that fasting while in a state of major impurity is valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting in a state of janabah and explain the proof from the hadith.

Core Idea
A state of major impurity does NOT invalidate the fast; the fast is valid even if you remain junub all day.

Many people assume that purification is required before starting the fast, but this is not the case. In this lesson, we examine the classical Hanafi text that clarifies the ruling and supports it with explicit proof from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is fasting (sawm). The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) even if the person begins the day in a state of janabah and remains so the entire day.
This applies to both obligatory and voluntary fasts.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The author cites two proofs: the Quranic verse permitting marital relations until dawn (2:187), which implies that ghusl may occur after dawn, and a hadith from Aishah where the Prophet ﷺ confirms fasting while junub.
The hadith is found in Muslim and Abu Dawud.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof indicates the ruling)
The Prophet's explicit statement 'I enter the morning junub... so I perform ghusl and fast' directly shows that janabah does not prevent fasting. The verse also indicates that intimacy is allowed up to dawn, meaning one may become junub at the start of the fast.
The fast is for abstaining from food, drink, and sexual relations during daylight; janabah is not a nullifier.
4
Note the consensus (ijma)
All major schools of fiqh agree that janabah does not invalidate the fast. The difference is only in recommendation: some prefer ghusl before dawn.
This is a muttafaq alayh ruling with no substantial ikhtilaf.
5
Confirm the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school relies on the zahir al-riwayah and considers this the mufta bihi position. The fast remains valid, and ghusl may be performed later in the day.
The ruling applies equally to men and women.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أَصبحَ جُنُباً، ولو اسْتَمَرَّ يوماً بالجَنَابَةِ) لما تلوناه، لاستلزام جواز المباشرة إلى قبيل الفجر وقوع الغسل بعده ضرورة، وعن عائشة أن رجلاً قال لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو واقــف عـلـى الباب: يا رسول الله، إني أُصبح جنباً وأنا أريد الصيام، فقال: رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: « وَأَنَا أُصْبِحُ جُنُباً وأَنَا أرِيدُ الصَّيَامَ فَأَغْتَسِلُ وَأصُومُ (٢) كما في ( البرهان »
(Or if he enters the morning in a state of major impurity, even if he continues the day in that state) because we have recited (the verse), due to the necessity that the permissibility of intercourse until just before dawn entails the occurrence of ghusl after it necessarily; and from Aishah that a man said to the Messenger of Allah while he was standing at the door: O Messenger of Allah, I enter the morning in a state of major impurity while I intend to fast. He said: And I enter the morning in a state of major impurity while I intend to fast, so I perform ghusl and fast. (As in al-Burhan).
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage establishes that a person who is in a state of janabah (major impurity) at the start of the day can still fast, and the fast is valid even if he remains in that state all day. The proof is twofold: first, the Quranic verse that permits marital relations until dawn (2:187) implies that one may become junub after that, and ghusl can be performed later without affecting the fast. Second, a direct hadith from Aishah confirms that the Prophet ﷺ himself would be in a state of janabah in the morning and would fast after performing ghusl. Thus, there is no requirement to be in a state of purification before starting the fast. The ruling applies to both men and women, and the fast remains valid even if one delays ghusl.

Key Terms
جنب (junub): A state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, typically after sexual intercourse or seminal emission. The text rules that this state does not affect the validity of the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a person who wakes up in a state of janabah (major impurity) after Fajr and intends to fast?
A) The fast is invalid because purification is required before starting the fast.
B) The fast is valid, and he must perform ghusl as soon as possible.
C) The fast is valid only if he performs ghusl before midday.
D) The fast is invalid unless he had the intention to purify before sleeping.

Key Takeaway
Fasting remains valid even if one is in a state of major impurity; purify for prayer, not for the fast.

Pouring Liquid into the Urethra: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Fasting Nullification
Intra-madhhab dispute with partial proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pouring water/oil into the urethra during fasting, identify the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the mufta bihi position in a modern medical context.

Core Idea
The urethra is not a direct passage to the stomach, so inserting liquid does not break the fast according to the majority Hanafi view; Abu Yusuf's dissent depends on whether it reaches the bladder.

This short passage addresses a specific intra-madhhab disagreement regarding a common medical scenario: instilling liquid into the urethra during fasting. The text presents the positions of the three major Hanafi imams, delimits the scope of their disagreement, and provides a partial proof for Abu Hanifa's view. Because the passage is incomplete (cuts off mid-sentence), we supplement it with the established Hanafi preference, which is that the fast remains valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is pouring water or oil into the urethra. The dispute: does it break the fast? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say no, Abu Yusuf says yes if it reaches the bladder.
2
Note the agreed-upon case
All agree that if the liquid remains in the urethral canal (qasabat al-dhakar), the fast is not broken.
This is the area of consensus.
3
Locate the point of disagreement
The disagreement is whether the liquid reaches the bladder (mathanah) and whether that constitutes reaching an interior that would break the fast.
The text calls this the 'khilaf'.
4
Recognize the preponderant view
The mufta bihi is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the fast remains valid. Abu Yusuf's view is a minority position within the school.
The tarjih is based on the majority and the fact that the bladder is not a direct passage to the stomach.
5
Understand the practical consequence
In practice, a person who instills liquid medicine via the urethra during fasting has a valid fast according to the relied-upon opinion.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو صَب في إحليله ماء أو دُهْناً) لا يفطر عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، خلافاً لأبي يوسف قال الفقيه أبو بكر البلخي رحمه الله تعالى: الخلاف فيمـا إذا وصل إلى المثانة، أما مادام في قصبة الذكر لا يفسد صومه بالاتفاق، لأبي حنيفة أن المثانة ليس
(Or if he pours water or oil into his urethra) it does not break the fast according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. The jurist Abu Bakr al-Balkhi said: The dispute is when it reaches the bladder; as long as it is in the penis canal, it does not break the fast by agreement. For Abu Hanifa, the bladder is not...
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of pouring liquid into the urethra (ihlil). According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, doing so does not break the fast, while Abu Yusuf holds that it does. The dispute is localised to when the liquid reaches the bladder (mathanah); as long as it remains in the urethral canal (qasabat al-dhakar), all agree that the fast is not invalidated because the canal is not considered a direct passage into the body's internal cavity that transmits nourishment. The text begins to explain Abu Hanifa's reasoning that the bladder is not a place where nourishment reaches, but the passage is incomplete. The mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, so pouring water/oil into the urethra does not break the fast.

Key Terms
إحليل (ihlil): The urethra, the external opening of the urinary tract; pouring liquid into it is under dispute.
مثانة (mathanah): The bladder; the text indicates that the dispute depends on whether the liquid reaches the bladder or stays in the canal.
قصبة الذكر (qasabat al-dhakar): The shaft or canal of the penis; the fast remains valid by agreement as long as the liquid is confined there.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
