A) It always breaks the fast regardless of where it goes.
B) It does not break the fast if it remains in the urethral canal (qasabat al-dhakar).
C) It breaks the fast only if it reaches the bladder.
D) It breaks the fast if the water is warm.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view is that pouring liquid into the urethra does not nullify the fast, as the urethra and bladder are not passages to the digestive system.

Matters That Do Not Break the Fast: Unavoidable Entries and Janabah
Lists matters that do not break the fast with proofs and an intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on dust, flies, medicine taste, janabah, and urethral insertion and identify the evidence and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding urethral insertion.

Core Idea
Not all entry into the body breaks the fast; ruling depends on avoidability and direct passage.

Fasting requires restraint, but not every substance that enters the body invalidates it. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between avoidable and unavoidable entries, and between direct and indirect passages. This lesson examines several cases where the fast remains valid: dust, flies, the taste of medicine, remaining in a state of janabah, and even pouring water or oil into the urethra. We will also explore the intra-Hanafi dispute on the latter issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify each exempted matter and its ruling
List each case mentioned (dust, mill dust, flies, medicine taste, tears/sweat in small amounts, janabah, urethral insertion) and state that the fast remains valid.
Focus on the principle: unavoidable entries do not break the fast.
2
Locate the proof for janabah
The hadith from A'ishah: the Prophet was junub in the morning and still fasted. This is a direct proof that janabah does not invalidate the fast.
The hadith is ahad but clear in meaning.
3
Understand the logic for unavoidable entries
Dust, flies, and medicine taste are exempt because guarding against them is extremely difficult or impossible. The same applies to small amounts of tears or sweat.
Contrast with deliberate smoke, which breaks the fast because it is avoidable.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on urethral insertion
Abu Hanifah and Muhammad hold that pouring water or oil into the urethra does not break the fast, because the bladder has no direct passage to the stomach. Abu Yusuf disagrees if it reaches the bladder.
The dispute hinges on anatomical knowledge; the relied-upon view is that there is no passage.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The fatwa is on the view of Abu Hanifah and Muhammad: the fast remains valid.
Al-Zayla'i and later authorities confirm this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو غبار. ولو غبار الطاحون. أو ذُبَابٌ أوْ أَثَرُ طَعْمِ الأدوية فيه، وهو ذاكر لصومِهِ، أَوْ أَصبحَ جُنُباً، ولوِ اسْتَمَرَّ يوماً بالجنابة، أو صَب في إخلِيلِهِ ماءً، أو دُهْناً.. يفسد فسد صومه، قربت من الداخل من كل وجه بخلاف الفم، فإنه خارج من وجه ويشهد لذلك ما في « الذخيرة» وفي الواقعات» للصدر الشهيد: إذا دخل الدموع فم الصائم إن كان قليلاً نحو القطـرة والقطـرتـيـن لا لأن التحرز عنه غير ممكن، وإن كان كثيراً حتى وجد ملوحته في جميع فمه وابتلعه صومه، وكذا الجواب في عرق الوجه، وفي إملاء محمد بن مقاتل صاحب محمد بن الحسن: إذا نزل دمعه من عينه إلى فمه ودخل حلقه فَطَّر انتهى. من خطه رحمه الله تعالى. تنبيه: قيدنا عدم الفطر بدخول الدخان بلا صنعة وذكرنا ما بعدها، تنبيهاً على أن من أدخـل دخـانـاً حلقه بأي صورة كان الإدخال، فسد صومه سواء كان دخان عنبر أو عود وغيرهما، حتـى مـن تبخر ببخـور فأواه إلى نفسه واشتم دخانه ذاكراً لصومه أفطر، لإمكان التحرز عن إدخال المفطر جوفه ودماغه، وهذا ممـا يغفل عنه كثير من الناس، فليتنبه له، ولا يتوهم أنه كشم الورد ومائه والمسك، لوضوح الـفــرق بـيـن هـواء تطيب بريح المسك وشبهه، وبين جوهر دخان وصل إلى جوفه بفعله. تنبيه آخر يؤخذ من مسألة الاكتحال ودهن الشارب أنه لا يكره للصائم شم رائحة المسك والورد ونحوه، مما لا يكون جوهراً متصلاً كالدخان، فإنهم قالوا: لا يكره الاكتحال بحال، وهـو شامل للطيب وغيره، ولم يخصوه بنوع منه، وكذا دهن الشارب (أو) دخل حلقه (غبار ولو) كــان (غبار) دقيق من (الطاحون أو ) دخل حلقه ( ذباب أو ) دخل (أَثَرُ طَعْمِ الأدويـة فـيـه) أي في حلقه لأنه لا يمكن الاحتراز عنها، فلا يفسد الصوم بدخولها (وهو ذاكر لصومِهِ) لما ذكرنا. (أو أَصبحَ جنباً، ولو اسْتَمَرَّ على حالته ( يوماً) أو أياماً ( بالجَنَابَةِ) لما تلوناه، لاستلزام جواز المباشرة إلى قبيل الفجر وقوع الغسل بعده ضرورة، وعن عائشة أن رجلاً قال لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو واقــف عـلـى الباب: يا رسول الله، إني أُصبح جنباً وأنا أريد الصيام، فقال: رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: « وَأَنَا أُصْبِحُ جُنُباً وأَنَا أرِيدُ الصَّيَامَ فَأَغْتَسِلُ وَأصُومُ (٢) كما في ( البرهان » ، أو صَب في إحليله ماء أو دُهْناً) لا يفطر عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، خلافاً لأبي يوسف قال الفقيه أبو بكر البلخي رحمه الله تعالى: الخلاف فيمـا إذا وصل إلى المثانة، أما مادام في قصبة الذكر لا يفسد صومه بالاتفاق، لأبي حنيفة أن المثانة ليس لها منفذ وإنما يخرج البول بالترشيح، وهذا الكلام يرجع إلى الطب، كذا في « الخانية». وقال الزيلعي: وهذا الاختلاف مبني على أنه هل بين المثانة والجوف منفذ أم لا؟ وهـو ليـس بـاختـلاف فيـه على التحقيق، والأظهر أن لا منفذ له، وإنما يجتمع البول فيها بالترشيح كذا يقول الأطباء، انتهى.
Or dust, even the dust of a mill. Or a fly, or the trace of medicine taste in it, while he is mindful of his fast. Or he becomes junub in the morning, even if he remains in janabah for a day. Or he pours water or oil into his urethra... it breaks his fast. Close to the interior in every respect unlike the mouth, for it is outward in one respect. This is something many people are heedless of, so he should be attentive. And let him not imagine that it is like smelling rose, rosewater, or musk, for the difference is clear between air perfumed with the scent of musk and similar, and between the substance of smoke that reaches his belly by his action. Or he becomes junub in the morning, even if he remains in that state for a day or days in janabah, because we recited (the verse), since permissibility of intercourse up to just before dawn necessitates that washing occurs after it necessarily. And from A'ishah that a man said to the Messenger of Allah while he was standing at the door: O Messenger of Allah, I become junub in the morning and I intend to fast. So the Messenger of Allah said: 'And I become junub in the morning and I intend to fast, so I wash and I fast.' As in al-Burhan. Or he pours water or oil into his urethra: it does not break the fast according to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Al-Faqih Abu Bakr al-Balkhi said: The disagreement is when it reaches the bladder. As long as it is in the urethra, it does not break the fast by consensus. For Abu Hanifah, the bladder has no passage and urine only comes out by filtration. This statement relates to medicine. Similarly in al-Khaniyyah. Al-Zayla'i said: This disagreement is built on whether or not there is a passage between the bladder and the belly. It is not a real disagreement in reality. The more apparent is that there is no passage, and urine collects by filtration as physicians say.

Commentary
This passage addresses several matters that seemingly could invalidate the fast but do not. The author begins by stating that dust, mill dust, flies, and the taste of medicine entering the throat do not break the fast, because it is impossible to guard against them. The same applies to tears or sweat that enter the mouth in small amounts. However, if much tears enter and one swallows them, the fast is broken. A caution is given about smoke: if one deliberately inhales smoke from incense or wood, the fast is broken because one can avoid it. This is unlike smelling the perfume of musk or rose, which only involves scent without a substance entering the belly. Then the author discusses janabah: becoming junub before dawn and remaining in that state all day does not break the fast, based on the Quranic permission for intercourse until dawn and the hadith where the Prophet himself was junub in the morning and still fasted. Finally, the issue of pouring water or oil into the urethra is presented with an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifah and Muhammad say it does not break the fast, while Abu Yusuf says it does if it reaches the bladder. The reason is that the bladder is not a direct passage to the stomach; urine collects by filtration. Al-Zayla'i notes that the disagreement hinges on anatomy, and the predominant view is that there is no passage. Therefore, the fast is not broken by this action according to the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
جنب (junub): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.
مثانة (mathanah): Bladder; the organ where urine collects.
إحليل (ihlil): Urethra; the canal through which urine exits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling on pouring water into the urethra while fasting?
A) It invalidates the fast because it enters the body.
B) It does not invalidate the fast because there is no direct passage to the stomach.
C) It invalidates the fast only if it reaches the bladder.
D) It invalidates the fast only if done deliberately.

Key Takeaway
The fast remains valid for unavoidable entries, janabah, and urethral insertion (mufta bihi), as per the Prophet's practice and anatomical reasoning.

Water Entering Ear, Earwax, and Swallowing Mucus: Rulings on the Fast
States that ear, nose, and vomit actions do not break the fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on actions that do not break the fast concerning ear, nose, and vomit, and distinguish them from the Shafi'i position on phlegm.

Core Idea
Water in ear, earwax, and sniffing mucus do not break the fast — only deliberate ingestion through the throat invalidates.

The fasting person must avoid anything entering the belly through the natural passage. But what about water entering the ear? Earwax? Sniffing back mucus? This lesson clarifies several common situations where the fast remains valid according to the Hanafi school, based on the principle that only intentional consumption breaks the fast. The passage also covers involuntary vomiting and deliberate vomiting of less than a mouthful, both of which do not invalidate the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions
List the specific actions mentioned: wading into water causing water in ear, scratching ear with stick bringing out earwax and reinserting, deliberately sniffing nasal mucus and swallowing, and vomiting (involuntary or induced less than a mouthful).
2
State the ruling for each
For each action, state that the fast remains valid (sahih). The water in ear does not break the fast because the ear has no passage to the stomach. Earwax reinserted likewise does not reach the belly. Swallowing nasal mucus is akin to swallowing saliva. Involuntary vomiting does not break the fast even if mouthful; deliberate vomiting of less than a mouthful also does not break.
The text uses 'في الصحيح' (in the correct view) for vomiting rulings.
3
Identify the rationale
The common rationale: the fast is only broken by deliberate introduction of a substance into the belly via the throat. None of these actions involve that. Ear and nose are not channels to the stomach; vomiting is involuntary or less than a mouthful.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference on phlegm
The passage notes that Imam al-Shafi'i recommends expelling phlegm to avoid breaking the fast, implying that in his school swallowing phlegm that has reached the mouth may break the fast. Hanafis hold it does not break unless it has left the mouth and been re-swallowed.
This highlights the importance of the principle: only ingestion of an external substance breaks the fast.
5
Apply to new scenarios
Use the ruling to evaluate similar cases: e.g., water in nose? (does not break unless swallowed), deliberate vomiting of a mouthful? (breaks), swallowing saliva? (does not break).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو خاض نهراً، فدخل الماء أُذُنَهُ، أَوْ حَكَ أُذُنَهُ بعُودٍ فَخَرَجَ عليه دَرَنٌ، ثمَّ أدخلهُ مِرَارًا إِلَى أَذْنِهِ أَوْ دَخَلَ أنفَهُ مُخاط، فاستنشقهُ عَمْدَاً، وابتلعه، وينبغي القاءُ النَّخَامَةِ حتى لا يَفْسُدَ صومُهُ، على قول الإمام الشافعي - رَحِمَهُ اللهُ - أَوْ نَرَعَهُ، القَيْءُ ، وعادَ بغير صُنْعِهِ، ولو ملا فمه، في الصحيح أو استقاء أقل من ملء فمه، على الصحيح ..
Or he waded into a river and water entered his ear; or he scratched his ear with a stick and dirt came out on it, then he inserted it repeatedly into his ear; or mucus entered his nose, then he deliberately sniffed it and swallowed it; and it is recommended to expel phlegm so that his fast is not broken, according to Imam al-Shafi'i; or vomiting overpowered him and it returned without his action, even if it filled his mouth, in the correct view; or he induced vomiting less than a mouthful, in the correct view.
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage covers several actions related to the ear, nose, and vomit that do not break the fast according to the Hanafi school. First, if a fasting person wades into a river and water enters his ear, his fast is not broken. This is because the ear does not have a direct passage to the stomach, and water entering it is not considered consumption. Similarly, if a person cleans his ear with a stick and some earwax comes out, then reinserts the stick multiple times, the fast remains valid by consensus. This is because the earwax does not reach the brain or belly. Regarding nasal mucus: if mucus descends from the head to the nose and one deliberately sniffs it back and swallows it, the fast is not broken according to most Hanafis. However, there is a discussion about phlegm and saliva. The author notes that it is recommended to expel phlegm to avoid breaking the fast according to Imam al-Shafi'i, but Hanafis generally do not consider phlegm that has not left the mouth to break the fast. The passage also mentions vomiting: if vomiting overcomes a person involuntarily, even if it fills the mouth, the fast is not broken. Similarly, if one induces vomiting but it is less than a mouthful, the fast is not broken. These rulings are based on the principle of necessity and the lack of intentional ingestion.

Key Terms
قئ (qay'): Vomit; the matter ejected from the stomach.
نخامة (nukhamah): Phlegm from the chest or throat.
استقاء (istiqa'): To deliberately induce vomiting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) His fast is not broken because it is from his own body and analogous to saliva.
C) His fast is broken only if the mucus came from the head.
D) His fast is broken and he must make up the day.

Key Takeaway
The fast is not invalidated by water entering the ear, earwax, or swallowing mucus that originates internally, as these do not constitute consumption through the throat.

Non-Invalidating Acts in Fasting: Incidental and Involuntary Ingestion
Commentary on multiple cases where fasting remains valid despite incidental ingestion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which common acts do not invalidate the fast and explain the Hanafi distinction between voluntary and involuntary ingestion.

Core Idea
Only deliberate ingestion of a nutritive substance invalidates the fast; incidental or unavoidable acts do not.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah clarifies several everyday scenarios that fasting students often worry about: water in the ear, swallowing phlegm, or vomiting. The commentator reinforces the Hanafi principle that the fast is robust and not broken by accidental or involuntary occurrences. By studying these rulings, you will understand the underlying logic that protects the worshipper from unnecessary hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their common feature
List each act mentioned: water entering ear while wading, earwax from stick, mucus swallowed, phlegm, involuntary vomiting, and intentional vomiting less than a mouthful. Notice all involve something entering the body without a deliberate intention to consume food.
The common thread is lack of willful ingestion of nutritive substance.
2
Classify each act as voluntary or involuntary
Separate acts that occur without choice (water in ear, earwax, involuntary vomiting) from those that involve some intent (deliberate sniffing of mucus, deliberate swallowing of phlegm). The Hanafi ruling often hinges on whether the act is avoidable or harmful to prevent.
Even deliberate acts like sniffing mucus are ruled valid because the substance is not food.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle: only nutritive substance breaks the fast
Hanafi fiqh holds that the fast is invalidated only by food, drink, or something that serves as nourishment (ta'dhiya). Items like water that merely enters the ear, earwax, phlegm, or even non-nutritive foreign bodies do not break the fast.
This is based on the Quranic verse 'eat and drink' and the principle of 'al-haraj marfu' (hardship is removed).
4
Analyze the hadith on vomiting
The hadith 'Whoever is overcome by vomiting while fasting, no qada is upon him; whoever intentionally vomits must make up the fast' is cited. The Hanafi school applies this strictly: involuntary vomiting never breaks the fast, even if it fills the mouth. Intentional vomiting only breaks if it returns deliberately or if a full mouthful is swallowed.
Intra-Hanafi detail: Abu Yusuf says intentional vomiting less than a mouthful does not break the fast.
5
Contrast with Shafi'i position on phlegm
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that if phlegm reaches the mouth and one can expel it but swallows it instead, the fast is broken. The Hanafi response disagrees, citing difficulty (haraj) and the fact that phlegm is not nutritive.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether qada is required for the same action.

Or if he waded in a river and water entered his ear, or he scratched his ear with a stick and dirt came out, then he inserted it repeatedly into his ear, or mucus entered his nose, he deliberately sniffed it and swallowed it. Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on various actions that do not invalidate the fast. The author addresses several cases: water entering the ear while wading; removing earwax with a stick and reinserting it; mucus entering the nose and being deliberately swallowed; and phlegm. For phlegm, the text notes a difference from Imam al-Shafi‘i: the Hanafi position is that swallowing phlegm does not break the fast because it is not considered food. The Shafi‘i view is that if one can expel it and instead swallows, the fast is invalid. The commentary also covers involuntary vomiting – citing a hadith that no qada is required – and intentional vomiting less than a mouthful, where the fast remains valid according to Abu Yusuf. The underlying principle is that only deliberate ingestion of a substance that nourishes or is considered food breaks the fast; involuntary or unavoidable acts do not.

Key Terms
نخامة (nukhamah): Phlegm or sputum that originates from the chest or head and is expelled through the mouth.
قيء (qay'): Vomiting; the expulsion of stomach contents through the mouth.
ذرعه (dhara'ahu): To overcome or overpower, used for involuntary vomiting.

Rulings on Returning Vomit and Eating Residue While Fasting
Commentary on eating residue and returning vomit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning vomit and eating small food residue during fasting, and explain the preferred view (mufta bihi) and its rationale.

Core Idea
The fast is not broken by returning vomit (Abu Yusuf's view) or by eating trace residue less than a chickpea, due to difficulty of avoidance.

This passage addresses two common concerns for a fasting person: what if one deliberately swallows vomit that has come up, and what if one inadvertently eats tiny food particles left between teeth or chews a small morsel from outside the mouth? The Hanafi school provides nuanced rulings based on the amount and the ease of avoidance, with a notable internal dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf. Understanding these exceptions helps avoid unnecessary nullification of a valid fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two distinct scenarios: (1) a fasting person deliberately returns (swallows) vomit that had exited the stomach, and (2) a fasting person eats residue between teeth (less than a chickpea) or chews a small morsel (like a sesame seed) from outside the mouth until it dissolves.
These are not the same as vomiting itself, which breaks the fast if deliberate and a mouthful.
2
State the intra-Hanafi dispute on returning vomit
Imam Muhammad holds that returning vomit invalidates the fast (Zahir al-Riwayah). Imam Abu Yusuf holds it does not, because the vomit is considered not to have fully exited (no legal exit) and thus does not break the fast. This is the chosen view according to al-Kamal.
Abu Hanifa’s view is not explicitly given here but aligns with Abu Yusuf in other texts.
3
Apply the principle of unavoidable traces to residue
For residue between teeth, if it is less than a chickpea, it is considered part of the saliva and difficult to avoid, so the fast remains valid. For chewing a morsel from outside, if it disintegrates without reaching the throat as food, it is also not a nullifier.
The key is that the substance does not reach the stomach as identifiable food.
4
Identify the criterion for 'small' amounts
Scholars distinguish between what requires conscious effort to swallow (much) and what flows naturally with saliva (little). The latter is excused.
This rational basis (dalil aqli) underlies the rulings.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that returning vomit does not break the fast (following Abu Yusuf), and eating residue less than a chickpea or dissolving a tiny morsel is permissible and does not nullify the fast.
This is the view of the later Hanafi masters as preferred by al-Kamal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أعاده، في الصحيح أو أكل ما بين أسنانِهِ، وكانَ دونَ الحِمْصَةِ، أَوْ مَضَغَ مِثلَ سِمْسِمَةٍ، مِنْ خارج فَمِهِ، حتى تلاشت، ولم يجد لها طَعْمَاً في حَلْقِهِ. وقال محمد: يفسد وهو ظاهر الرواية ولو أعاده، في الصحيح لا يفسد عند أبي يوسف، كما في المحيط» لعدم الخروج حكماً حتى لا ينقض الطهارة. وقال « الكمال»: وهـو الـمخـتـار لـعــدم الخروج شرعاً. وقال محمد: يفسد وهو ظاهر الرواية ورواية عن أبي يوسف بإطلاق ما روينا. (أو أكل ما بين أسنانِهِ) مما بقي بفيه من سحوره (وكان دونَ الحِمْصَةِ)؛ لأنه تبع لريقه، وهذا القدر لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه عادة أو يتعسر، وقال ( الكمال من المشايخ من جعل الفاصل بين القليل والكثير كون ذلك مما يحتاج في ابتلاعه إلى الاستعانة بالريق، أو لا يحتاج، الأول قليل والثاني كثير، وهو حسن؛ لأن المانع من الحكم بالإفطار بعد تحقق الوصول كونه لا يسهل الاحتراز عنه، وذلك مما يجري بنفسه مع الريق، لا فيما يتعمد في إدخاله لأنه غير مضطر فيه انتهى. (أوْ مَضَغَ مِثلَ سمسمةٍ) أي: قدرها وقد تناولها ( من خارج فَمِهِ، حتى تلاشت، ولم يجد لها طَعْمَاً في حَلْقِهِ) كذا في الكافي ) قال ( الكمال ) : وهذا حسن جداً فليكن الأصل في كل قليل مضغه انتهى. :
And if he returned it (the vomit) deliberately – in the sound view – or ate what was between his teeth and it was less than a chickpea, or he chewed something like a sesame seed from outside his mouth until it disintegrated and he did not find its taste in his throat. Muhammad said: it invalidates, and that is the apparent narration. And if he returned it, in the sound view it does not invalidate according to Abu Yusuf, as in al-Muhit, because there is no effective exit (from the stomach) such that it would break ablution. Al-Kamal said: this is the chosen view due to lack of exit legally. Muhammad said: it invalidates, and that is the apparent narration, and a narration from Abu Yusuf also supports this. (Or ate what was between his teeth) from remaining suhur, and it was less than a chickpea – because it is part of his saliva, and this amount cannot usually be avoided or is difficult. Al-Kamal said some scholars distinguish between little and much by whether one needs to use saliva to swallow it: if yes, it is little; if no, it is much. This is good because the prevention of ruling of breaking fast after confirming arrival (to the stomach) is that it is not easy to avoid, and that which flows with saliva is not like deliberately inserting (food). (Or chewed something like a sesame seed) i.e., its size, from outside his mouth until it disintegrated and he did not find its taste in his throat – as in al-Kafi. Al-Kamal said: this is very good, so let this be the principle for every small thing chewed.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage deals with two specific issues: (1) returning vomit deliberately, and (2) eating residue between teeth or chewing small morsels. For returning vomit, there is ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf holds that if one deliberately returns vomit that had already exited, the fast is not invalidated because the vomit is not considered to have fully left (being still in the mouth) and thus does not break the fast. Muhammad argues it does invalidate, and this is the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah). Al-Kamal prefers Abu Yusuf's view. For eating residue between teeth: if the amount is less than a chickpea (about the size of a chickpea), it is considered part of the saliva and difficult to avoid, so it does not break the fast. For chewing a small morsel from outside the mouth (like a sesame seed) until it dissolves without leaving a taste in the throat, the fast remains valid. The principle is that if the substance disintegrates and mixes with saliva without reaching the throat as food, it is not a nullifier. The text emphasizes that the determining factor is whether one can easily avoid it: if it requires conscious effort and use of saliva, it is considered minimal and not breaking the fast.

Key Terms
حمصة (himsah): Chickpea; used as a measure for the size of food residue that can be swallowed without breaking the fast.
سمسمة (simsimah): Sesame seed; a smaller measure for a morsel chewed from outside the mouth.
أعاده (a'ada): He returned it; in this context, deliberately swallowing vomit that had already exited the stomach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on a fasting person who chews a small piece of food from outside his mouth, like a sesame seed, until it dissolves and he does not taste it in his throat?
A) The fast is broken and qada is required.
B) The fast remains valid because the substance disintegrated and did not reach the throat as food.
C) It is makruh but the fast is still valid.
D) It breaks the fast only if it is a solid food.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school excuses negligible amounts of food residue and returning vomit (per preferred view) due to difficulty of avoidance and legal continuity.

Swallowing Small Remnants and Chewing Tiny Pieces During Fasting
Intra-Hanafi dispute on small amounts eaten during fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding swallowing remnants and chewing small pieces during fasting, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A fasting person's fast is not broken by swallowing remnants smaller than a chickpea or chewing a sesame-sized piece that dissolves without taste.

This lesson examines a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute about what constitutes food entering the stomach that invalidates the fast. The key distinction is between unavoidable remnants that mix with saliva and deliberate intake of small pieces. Understanding the criteria used by the Hanafi masters—such as the size threshold and the role of intention—will clarify when a fast remains valid despite something entering the throat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
The passage discusses two situations: (1) swallowing remnants from between the teeth that are less than a chickpea, and (2) chewing a sesame-sized piece taken from outside the mouth until it dissolves without taste. Determine the ruling for each in the preferred view.
2
Identify the dispute and its narrators
Note the disagreement: Abu Yusuf holds that the fast is not invalidated in the correct view (al-sahih), while Imam Muhammad holds it is invalidated, and his view is the ẓāhir al-riwāyah (apparent transmission of the school). Also note a second narration from Abu Yusuf agreeing with Imam Muhammad.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
Understand why Abu Yusuf's view is preferred: (a) swallowing remnants less than a chickpea is unavoidable and thus excused; (b) chewing a sesame-sized piece from outside until dissolved without taste does not constitute deliberate eating because of the minimal amount and lack of taste. The barrier to invalidation is the difficulty of avoidance.
Al-Kamal endorses Abu Yusuf's view as al-mukhtār (the chosen position).
4
Apply the criterion from later scholars
Al-Kamal cites a criterion: if one needs saliva to swallow it, it is considered a small (excused) amount; if it can be swallowed without saliva, it is large. This is based on the ease of avoidance.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi (operative ruling for fatwa) according to al-Kamal is that neither scenario breaks the fast. However, note that the ẓāhir al-riwāyah is Imam Muhammad's position, so caution is needed in practice.

Source Text

ولو أعاده، في الصحيح أو أكل ما بين أسنانِهِ، وكانَ دونَ الحِمْصَةِ، أَوْ مَضَغَ مِثلَ سِمْسِمَةٍ، مِنْ خارج فَمِهِ، حتى تلاشت، ولم يجد لها طَعْمَاً في حَلْقِهِ. وقال محمد: يفسد وهو ظاهر الرواية ولو أعاده، في الصحيح لا يفسد عند أبي يوسف، كما في المحيط» لعدم الخروج حكماً حتى لا ينقض الطهارة. وقال « الكمال»: وهـو الـمخـتـار لـعــدم الخروج شرعاً. وقال محمد: يفسد وهو ظاهر الرواية ورواية عن أبي يوسف بإطلاق ما روينا. (أو أكل ما بين أسنانِهِ) مما بقي بفيه من سحوره (وكان دونَ الحِمْصَةِ)؛ لأنه تبع لريقه، وهذا القدر لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه عادة أو يتعسر، وقال ( الكمال من المشايخ من جعل الفاصل بين القليل والكثير كون ذلك مما يحتاج في ابتلاعه إلى الاستعانة بالريق، أو لا يحتاج، الأول قليل والثاني كثير، وهو حسن؛ لأن المانع من الحكم بالإفطار بعد تحقق الوصول كونه لا يسهل الاحتراز عنه، وذلك مما يجري بنفسه مع الريق، لا فيما يتعمد في إدخاله لأنه غير مضطر فيه انتهى. (أوْ مَضَغَ مِثلَ سمسمةٍ) أي: قدرها وقد تناولها ( من خارج فَمِهِ، حتى تلاشت، ولم يجد لها طَعْمَاً في حَلْقِهِ) كذا في الكافي ) قال ( الكمال ) : وهذا حسن جداً فليكن الأصل في كل قليل مضغه انتهى. :
And if he returns it, in the correct (view), or he eats what is between his teeth, and it is less than a chickpea, or he chews something like a sesame seed from outside his mouth until it dissolves, and he does not find its taste in his throat. Muhammad said: It invalidates (the fast), and that is the apparent narration. And if he returns it, in the correct (view) it does not invalidate according to Abu Yusuf, as in al-Muhit, because it does not exit legally so it does not break purity. And al-Kamal said: That is the chosen view because it does not exit according to Sharia. Muhammad said: It invalidates, and it is the apparent narration, and there is a narration from Abu Yusuf generally based on what we narrated. (Or he eats what is between his teeth) from the remnants of his suhur that remain in his mouth, (and it is less than a chickpea) because it is subservient to his saliva, and this amount cannot normally be avoided or is difficult. And al-Kamal said that some scholars make the distinction between little and much based on whether one needs aid of saliva to swallow or not; the first is little, the second is much, and this is good because the barrier against ruling that the fast is broken after confirming arrival (to the stomach) is that it is not easy to avoid, and that runs with saliva by itself, not in the case of deliberately introducing it because he is not compelled. End. (Or he chews something like a sesame seed) meaning its size, and he took it from outside his mouth until it dissolves and he does not find its taste in his throat. Thus in al-Kafi. Al-Kamal said: This is very good, so let this be the principle for every little thing chewed. End.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when a fasting person swallows food remnants that were between his teeth or chews a small piece from outside his mouth. It begins by addressing a previous scenario: if someone returns something to his mouth (presumably after spitting it out), the correct view according to Abu Yusuf is that it does not break the fast because it is not considered as having exited the mouth legally—just as it does not nullify wudu. Al-Kamal prefers this view because Sharia does not treat it as exiting. Muhammad holds that it does break the fast, and this is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). The passage then turns to the case of eating remnants between teeth from suhur that are less than a chickpea: this does not break the fast because it is inseparable from saliva and such a small amount is unavoidable. Al-Kamal cites a criterion from some scholars: if one needs to use saliva to swallow it, it is considered a small amount; if it can be swallowed without aid, it is large. This is because the reason for not ruling the fast broken is the difficulty of avoidance; saliva naturally brings such remnants down. Finally, the case of chewing a sesame-sized morsel taken from outside the mouth until it dissolves without taste: this also does not break the fast according to al-Kamal, who approves the principle from al-Kafi that this applies to any little thing chewed. The key point is that the fast is only broken by what enters the stomach deliberately and avoidably; negligible amounts are excused.

Key Terms
الحِمْصَة (al-ḥimṣah): A chickpea; used as a measure of size for remnants between teeth.
ظاهر الرواية (ẓāhir al-riwāyah): The apparent/sound narration from Imam Muhammad's six core books.
غير مضطر (ghayr muḍṭarr): Not compelled; one who deliberately introduces food.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, what is the ruling if a fasting person chews a sesame-sized piece taken from outside his mouth until it dissolves without tasting it?
A) The fast is broken and qada is required.
B) The fast is not broken because it is a small amount.
C) The fast is broken and both qada and kaffarah are required.
D) The fast is not broken only if he did not intend to eat.

Key Takeaway
The fast remains valid when swallowing remnants less than a chickpea from between teeth or chewing a sesame-sized piece from outside that dissolves without taste, according to the mufta bihi.

Conditions for Qada and Kaffarah in Fasting
Opens the chapter listing conditions for kaffarah with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions for qada and kaffarah, and apply them to determine which nullifiers require both penalties.

Core Idea
Kaffarah requires intention at night, no valid excuse, volition, deliberation, and no compulsion.

This passage opens the chapter on nullifiers that require both qada (make-up) and kaffarah (expiation). The author immediately clarifies that not every act breaking the fast triggers kaffarah; strict conditions apply. Understanding these conditions is essential for distinguishing between acts that only require qada versus those that require both. We will parse the conditions and apply them to the examples provided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nullifiers and the ruling
List the acts mentioned (approximately 22, but examples given: intercourse, eating, drinking). The ruling is that they require both qada and kaffarah when conditions are met.
The full list is implied; focus on the examples for this lesson.
2
Extract the conditions for kaffarah
From the text, identify the key conditions: intention for Ramadan before dawn, no permission to break (illness/travel), voluntary (not coerced), deliberate (not forgetful), and not compelled (by extreme necessity).
Each condition is explicitly stated with specific Arabic terms (طائعاً, متعمداً, غير مضطر).
3
Map the exceptions and preventers
Note what negates kaffarah: coercion (even by wife), forgetfulness, mistake, compulsion, intention after dawn, or a valid excuse arising after the act. These are the mawani (preventers).
The text mentions that if the intention was after dawn, no kaffarah; if menses or illness arises, kaffarah drops.
4
Apply to the specific examples
For each example (intercourse in front or anus, eating, drinking), confirm how the conditions apply. Note that for intercourse, both active and passive partner incur kaffarah, and the anus is treated like the front.
The text equates front and anus for kaffarah but not for hadd penalties.

(و) كذا (الأكل) حتـى لـو تـوهـم مجيء الحيض، أو الحمى، وهو معتادها، فلم تحم ولم تحض لزمت الكفارة، كما في «التجنيس» (وَ) كذا الشرب سواءٌ فيه ) أي: المفطر ( ما يُتَغَذَّى) أي: يربَّى ويقام البدن (به) أي: الغذاء -هو : بالغين والذال المعجمتين - : اسم للذات المأكولة غذاء، قال في «الجوهرة» : واختلفوا في معنى التغذي، قال بعضهم: أن يميل الطبع إلى أكلة وتنقضي شهوة البطن به وقال بعضهم: هو ما يعود نفعه إلى (٤) ص (٦٥٠).
The chapter of what invalidates the fast and for which expiation is obligatory along with making up. It (the chapter) is about twenty-two things approximately. When the responsible fasting person (who) has made the intention at night for performance of Ramadan, and nothing has arisen that permits breaking the fast after it like illness or before it like travel, and he has done something from them, i.e., the invalidators, voluntarily - guarding against the coerced person, even if his wife coerced him in the most correct view as in al-Jawhara, and that is given as fatwa, as in al-Tajnisa, so no expiation (is due). Even if volition occurs during intercourse, because it is after breaking the fast under coercion in the beginning, as in al-Bahr from al-Zahiriyya, as we will mention. (Deliberately) guarding against the forgetful and the mistaken. (Not compelled) because the compelled person has no expiation upon him. He must make up the fast to recover the lost benefit, and expiation is due on him because of the completeness of the offense. Among that is intercourse in one of the two passages, i.e., the two passages of a living human being. By intercourse, the qada and kaffarah are required from the active partner, even if he does not ejaculate, and from the passive partner. The anus is like the front in the most correct view, because the offense is complete, unlike the hadd penalty because it pertains to zina, and intercourse in the anus is not zina in truth because it is defined as intercourse in the specific private part. We conditioned that the intention be made at night, because if he made his intention after dawn then had intercourse, the kaffarah is not due due to the doubt about the validity of his fast. We conditioned that nothing permitting breaking the fast does not arise, because if something like menses, illness, or war arises, the expiation falls, as we will mention. And similarly eating, even if one imagined the arrival of menses or fever while it is usual for her, but it did not occur, the kaffarah is due, as in al-Tajnisa. And similarly drinking, it is the same in what is nourishing, i.e., what strengthens and maintains the body. Food - with ghayn and dhal - is a name for the substance eaten as nourishment. He said in al-Jawhara: They differed on the meaning of nourishment. Some said: that the inclination of nature to eat it and the stomach's desire is satisfied by it. Some said: it is what its benefit returns to... (reference to page 650).
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on nullifiers of the fast that require both qada (make-up) and kaffarah (expiation). It begins by enumerating approximately twenty-two such acts, then immediately specifies the conditions for these penalties: the person must be a sane, responsible fasting person who had the intention for Ramadan before dawn, and no permission to break like illness or travel arises. The act must be done voluntarily (not coerced), deliberately (not forgetful or mistaken), and without compulsion (e.g., extreme hunger). If these conditions are met, the fast must be made up and kaffarah is due. The passage then gives the first example: intercourse in one of the two bodily passages (front or rear) of a living human. Both the active and passive partner incur qada and kaffarah regardless of emission. The anus is equated with the front in the most correct view because the offense is complete, though the hadd penalty differs. The passage also notes that if the intention was made after dawn, no kaffarah is due due to doubt about the fast's validity. Similarly, if a valid excuse arises after the act, kaffarah drops. It then extends the ruling to eating and drinking, stating that both are same regardless of whether the substance is nourishing or not. There is a discussion about the definition of 'nourishing' (al-taghadhi), but the page ends before giving the conclusion. The core idea: not every break of fast requires kaffarah; specific conditions must be met.

Key Terms
طائعاً (ṭā'i'an): Voluntarily, as opposed to being coerced.
متعمداً (muta'ammidan): Deliberately, with intent and knowledge.
مضطر (muḍṭarr): Compelled by extreme necessity (e.g., starvation).
مبيت النية (mubayyit al-niyyah): Making the intention at night before dawn for the Ramadan fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for kaffarah to be obligatory?
A) The fasting person intended the fast for Ramadan before dawn.
B) The act was done deliberately.
C) The person was compelled by severe hunger.
D) The act was done voluntarily.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah for breaking a Ramadan fast is only due when the person has valid intention, no excuse, and acts voluntarily, deliberately, and without compulsion.

Conditions for Kaffarah in Invalidating the Fast: The Case of Intercourse
Opening chapter on nullifiers with kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for kaffarah and explain why intercourse in either passage requires qada and kaffarah.

Core Idea
Intercourse invalidates the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah, even without ejaculation, in both passages.

This lesson examines the general rule for acts that invalidate a Ramadan fast and require both make-up (qada) and expiation (kaffarah). The commentary specifies that there are approximately 22 nullifiers. The first detailed act is sexual intercourse. We will focus on the conditions that trigger kaffarah and the ruling on vaginal and anal intercourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for kaffarah
The text lists three conditions: the act must be voluntary (طائعاً), intentional (متعمداً), and the person must not be compelled by necessity (غير مضطر). Also, the person must have made the intention from the night (تبييت النية) and no valid excuse (like illness or travel) may arise.
Coercion from spouse is considered like coercion—no kaffarah even if volition occurs mid-act.
2
Understand the ruling on intercourse
Intercourse in either of the two passages (front or back) invalidates the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah. This applies to both the active and passive participant, even without ejaculation.
The ruling is based on the completeness of the offense (تكامل الجناية).
3
Note the special case of anal intercourse
In the soundest view (الأصح), anal intercourse is treated like vaginal intercourse for kaffarah because the offense is complete. A less sound view exists that does not equate them, but the mufta bihi is the soundest view.
The distinction from hadd (penalty for zina) is highlighted: hadd does not apply to anal intercourse, but kaffarah does.
4
Recognize exceptions to kaffarah
Kaffarah drops if the person was coerced, or if the intention was not made from the night (e.g., one intended after dawn), or if a valid excuse (like menstruation or illness) occurs after starting the fast.
In these cases, only qada is required, not kaffarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد به الصوم وتجب به الكفارة مع القضاء (وهو اثنان وعشرون شيئاً) تقريباً (إذا فعل المكلف الصائم) مبيتا النية في أداء رمضان، ولم يطرأ ما يبيح الفطر بعده كمرض أو قبله كسفر، وقد فعل شيئاً منها أي: المفسدات (طائعاً) احترز به عن المكره، ولو أكرهته زوجته في الأصح كما في «الجوهرة» وبه يفتى، كما في التجنيس فلا كفارة ولو حصلت الطواعية في أثناء الجماع؛ لأنها بعد الإفطار مكرهاً في الابتداء، كذا في «البحر» عن «الظهيرية»، كما سنذكره (متعمداً) احترز عن الناسي والمخطئ (غير مضطر) إذ المضطر لا كفارة عليه، لزمه القضاء استدراكاً للمصلحة الفائتة (و) لزمه (الكفارة) لتكامل الجناية ومن ذلك: (الجماع في أحد السبيلين) أي: سبيلي آدمي حي، وبالجماع يلزم القضاء والكفارة على الفاعل، وإن لم ينزل (و) على (المفعول به)، والدبر كالقبل في الأصح؛ لتكامل الجناية، بخلاف الحد لأنه متعلق بالزنا، وليس الجماع في الدبر زنا حقيقة، لأنه عبارة عن الجماع في الفرج المخصوص، وقيدنا بتبييته النية، لأنه لو نوى بعد طلوع الفجر ثم جامع، لا تلزمه الكفارة لشبهة الاختلاف في صحة صومه، وقيدنا بعدم طرو ما يبيح الفطر لأنه لو طرأ نحو حيض ومرض وحرب سقطت الكفارة كما سنذكره
Chapter on what invalidates the fast and for which kaffarah is obligatory along with qada. (It is approximately twenty-two things). If the responsible fasting person, having made the intention from the night for performing Ramadan fast and no excuse to break the fast occurring after it like illness or before it like travel, does something from them, i.e., the nullifiers, voluntarily, guarding against the coerced, and if his wife coerced him, in the soundest view as in al-Jawhara, and according to it is given fatwa as in al-Tajnīs, so no kaffarah, even if volition occurs during intercourse because it is after breaking the fast under coercion at the beginning, as in al-Bahr from al-Zahīriyya, as we will mention. (Intentionally), guarding against the forgetful and mistaken. (Not compelled) for the compelled has no kaffarah. (Qada is obligatory) to make up for the lost benefit. (And) (kaffarah) is obligatory for fulfillment of the offense. And from that: (intercourse in one of the two passages), i.e., the two passages of a living human. By intercourse, qada and kaffarah are obligatory upon the active one even if he does not ejaculate, and upon the passive one. And the anus is like the vagina in the soundest view, for the fulfillment of the offense, unlike the hadd penalty because it pertains to zina, and anal intercourse is not actually zina, because it is defined as intercourse in the specific genital. And we restricted with making the intention from the night, because if he intended after dawn then had intercourse, kaffarah is not obligatory due to the doubt in the validity of his fast. And we restricted with the non-occurrence of what permits breaking the fast, because if something like menstruation, illness, or war occurs, kaffarah drops, as we will mention.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah commentary on Nur al-Idah opens the chapter on what invalidates the fast and requires both qada (make-up) and kaffarah (expiation). It states that there are approximately twenty-two nullifiers. The conditions for kaffarah are: the person must be a mukallaf (legally responsible), must have made the intention for fasting from the night (tabyit al-niyya) for Ramadan, and no excuse such as illness or travel may arise before or after. The act must be done voluntarily (ta'i'an), intentionally (muta'ammidan), and not under coercion (ghayr mudtarr). If coerced, even if volition occurs mid-act, kaffarah is not due. The first nullifier detailed is sexual intercourse in either the front or back passage. Both the active and passive participants incur kaffarah even without ejaculation. The anus is treated like the vagina in the soundest view because the offense is complete, though the hadd penalty for zina is not applicable. If one intended the fast after dawn, kaffarah is not due due to a difference of opinion on the fast's validity. If an excuse like menstruation or illness arises after starting the fast, kaffarah is dropped.

Key Terms
الجماع (al-jimā'): Sexual intercourse, specifically penetration in either the front or back passage.
القضاء (al-qaḍā'): Making up the missed fast after Ramadan.
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation for deliberately breaking a valid Ramadan fast by specified acts.
طائعاً (ṭā'i'an): Voluntarily, without coercion.
متعمداً (muta'ammidan): Intentionally, knowing the act breaks the fast and that it is prohibited.
مضطر (muḍṭarr): One compelled by necessity, e.g., life-threatening hunger or thirst.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on kaffarah for a person who was coerced into intercourse by his spouse?
A) Kaffarah is obligatory because he eventually acted voluntarily.
B) Kaffarah is not obligatory because the act began under coercion.
C) Only qada is required.
D) It depends on whether he intended the fast from the night.

Key Takeaway
Intercourse invalidates the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah when done voluntarily, intentionally, without necessity, and with proper intention from the night; anal intercourse is treated like vaginal in the soundest Hanafi view.

Defining Taghaddi: When Does Eating Incur Kaffarah?
Defining nourishment for kaffarah with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two Hanafi definitions of taghaddi and apply them to determine whether kaffarah is due for specific substances.

Core Idea
Kaffarah requires true nourishment and custom; harmful or non-beneficial substances do not incur kaffarah.

The precise definition of 'taghaddi' (nourishment) is critical for determining when kaffarah becomes obligatory for eating during fasting. The Hanafi school has two opinions on its meaning, and the chosen view affects many modern cases. This lesson unpacks the dispute and provides a clear criterion based on custom and benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core question: what type of eating or drinking triggers kaffarah? It depends on whether the substance is considered 'taghaddi' (nourishing).
Kaffarah is a severe penalty, so it applies only to clear violations.
2
State the two opinions on taghaddi
First opinion: whatever nature inclines to and satisfies hunger. Second opinion: whatever benefits the body and contributes to its health. The second is declared al-ashahh (soundest).
The first view focuses on subjective satisfaction; the second on objective benefit.
3
Apply to the morsel case
If one chews a morsel, takes it out, then swallows it later: first opinion says no kaffarah (desire already satisfied), second says kaffarah (body still benefits from swallowing).
This illustrates the practical difference.
4
Apply the custom criterion
Majma' al-Riwāyāt establishes that custom (ʿādah) determines what is considered food. Items eaten intentionally or as a follower are nourishing; those not customarily eaten are not, even if nutritious.
Custom is a key Hanafi principle in defining food.
5
Note the tarjih
The second opinion (benefit to body) is preferred. Also, Abu Yusuf's view on chewing nuts with shell is mentioned, but the default is the custom-based rule.
For fatwa, rely on the sounder view and custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كذا الأكل حتى لو توهـم مجيء الحيض، أو الحمى، وهو معتادها، فلم تحم ولم تحض لزمت الكفارة، كما في التجنيس و كذا الشرب سواء فيه أي المفطر ما يتغذى أي يربى ويقام البدن به أي الغذاء هو بالغين والذال المعجمتين اسم للذات المأكولة غذاء، قال في الجوهرة : اختلفوا في معنى التغذي، قال بعضهم: أن يميل الطبع إلى أكلة وتنقضي شهوة البطن به وقال بعضهم: هو ما يعود نفعه إلى صلاح البدن، وفائدته فيما إذا مضغ لقمة ثم أخرجها، ثم ابتلعها فعلى القول الثاني تجب الكفارة، وعلى الأول لا تجب انتهى. وهذا هو الأصح لأنه بإخراجها تعافها النفس كذا في المحيط انتهى. ثم قال في الجوهرة : وعلى هذا الورق الحبشي، والحشيشة، والقطاط إذا أكله، فعلى الثاني لا تجب الكفارة لأنه لا نفع فيه للبدن وربما يضره وينقص عقله، وعلى القول الأول تجب لأن الطبع يميل إليه وتنقضي به شهوة البطن انتهى قلت وعلى هذا البدعة التي ظهرت الآن وهو الدخان إذا شربه في لزوم الكفارة، نسأل الله العفو والعافية انتهى. وقال في مجمع الروايات : ثم الفاصل بين ما يتغذى به وبين ما لا يتغذى أن كل ما يؤكل عادة مقصوداً أو تبعاً لغيره فهو مما يتغذى به، وكل ما لا يؤكل عادة أصلاً فهو ملحق بما لا يتغذى به، وإن كان في نفسه مغذياً، ولو ابتلع جوزة رطبة، أو لوزة يابسة لم تلزمه الكفارة، لأنه لا يؤكل مع قشره عادة، ولو ابتلع لوزة رطبة تلزمه الكفارة لأنها تؤكل عادة مع القشر، وعن أبي يوسف إذا مضغ الجوزة أو اللوزة اليابسة بقشرها حتى وصل إلى جوفه تلزمه الكفارة، لأنه أكل ما هو مأكول وهو اللب مع ما ليس بمأكول وهو القشر انتهى. وبه جزم قاضي خان ولم يذكره مسنداً ولم يذكر غيره انتهى. ثم قال في مجمع الروايات، وقيل: لا تلزمه الكفارة؛ لأن اعتبار وقوع الفطر بما يتغذى به، إن كان يوجب الكفارة، فاعتبار وقوع الفطر بما لا يتغذى به يمنع وجوب الكفارة، فوقع الشك في وجوبها، فلا تجب بالشك كما لو ابتلعها انتهى. وإن لم يكن فيها لب عليه القضاء دون الكفارة، الرطب واليابس فيه سواء، واللوزة اليابسة بمنزلة الجوزة، وكذا البندق والفستق إن كانت رطبة، فهي بمنزلة الجوز وإن كانت يابسة، إن مضغها كان عليه
And likewise eating, even if she imagines the coming of menses or fever while it is customary for her, and it did not occur, she is obligated kaffarah, as in al-Tajnīs. And likewise drinking, equally in it, i.e., the nullifier, what is nourishing, i.e., by which the body is reared and sustained, i.e., food – it is with the ghayn and dhāl mu'jama – a name for the substance eaten as food. He said in al-Jawhara: They differed on the meaning of 'tāghdhī'. Some said: that the nature inclines to eating it and the belly's desire is satisfied by it. And some said: it is that whose benefit returns to the rectification of the body. Its benefit is regarding when one chews a morsel then takes it out, then swallows it: according to the second opinion, kaffarah is obligatory; according to the first, it is not obligatory, end. And this is the soundest because by taking it out the soul abhors it, as in al-Muḥīṭ, end. Then he said in al-Jawhara: And on this, the Abyssinian leaves, hashish, and qat – if one eats them, according to the second, kaffarah is not obligatory because there is no benefit in them for the body and perhaps they harm him and diminish his intellect; and according to the first opinion, it is obligatory because the nature inclines to it and the belly's desire is satisfied by it, end. I said: And on this, the innovation that has appeared now, which is smoking, if one drinks it, regarding the obligation of kaffarah, we ask Allah for pardon and well-being, end. And he said in Majma' al-Riwāyāt: Then the distinction between what is nourishing and what is not nourishing is that everything that is customarily eaten intentionally or as a follower of something else is from what is nourishing, and everything that is not customarily eaten at all is annexed to what is not nourishing, even if it is nutritive in itself. And if one swallows a fresh walnut or a dry almond, kaffarah is not obligatory because it is not customarily eaten with its peel; and if one swallows a fresh almond, kaffarah is obligatory because it is customarily eaten with its peel. And from Abu Yusuf: if he chews a walnut or a dry almond with its peel until it reaches his stomach, kaffarah is obligatory because he ate what is eaten, i.e., the kernel, along with what is not eaten, i.e., the peel, end. And Qāḍī Khān affirmed it and did not relate it as a chain and did not mention other. Then he said in Majma' al-Riwāyāt: And it was said: kaffarah is not obligatory because the consideration of the occurrence of breaking the fast by what is nourishing, if it necessitates kaffarah, then the consideration of the occurrence of breaking the fast by what is not nourishing prevents the obligation of kaffarah, so doubt occurs in its obligation, so it is not obligatory due to doubt, as if he swallowed it. End. And if there is no kernel in it, upon him is qada without kaffarah, wet and dry are equal in it. And the dry almond is like the walnut. And likewise hazelnuts and pistachios: if they are wet, they are like the walnut; and if they are dry, if he chews them, upon him is...
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on eating and drinking as nullifiers of the fast. It states that eating and drinking incur kaffarah, even if a woman mistakenly thinks her period has started but it hasn't. The key issue is defining 'tāghdhī' (nourishment). Two opinions are presented: (1) whatever the nature inclines to and satisfies hunger, (2) whatever benefits the body. The second is declared sounder (al-ashahh). This affects cases like chewing a morsel and spitting it out then swallowing: according to the first view, kaffarah is not due because the nature might have been satisfied earlier; according to the second, it is due because the body benefited when swallowed. Similarly, substances like Abyssinian leaves, hashish, and qat: if they harm the body, the second view exempts them from kaffarah, but the first view imposes it because they satisfy hunger. The commentator adds that smoking (dukhan) is analogous to these, implying kaffarah is not due if it does not benefit the body. Then a criterion from Majma' al-Riwāyāt: custom determines what is food. If an item is customarily eaten, even as a complement (like a peel that is eaten with the fruit), it is considered food; if not customarily eaten, it is not, even if nutritious. Examples: swallowing a fresh walnut with its shell does not incur kaffarah because the shell is not customarily eaten; swallowing a fresh almond does because its shell is customarily eaten with it. Abu Yusuf says that if one chews a dry walnut or almond with shell, kaffarah is due because he eats both the edible kernel and the inedible peel. Some scholars argue that if there is doubt, kaffarah is not due due to uncertainty. For nuts without a kernel (empty shell), only qada is due, not kaffarah.

Key Terms
التغذي (al-tāghdhī): The quality of being nourishing or sustaining the body; the criterion for whether a substance incurs kaffarah when eaten.
القول الأول / القول الثاني (al-qaul al-awwal / al-qaul al-thānī): The two opinions on the meaning of taghaddi: first (satisfying desire) and second (benefit to body).
العادة (al-'ādah): Customary practice; what people normally eat as food, including parts like peel that are eaten with the fruit.
الأصح (al-ashahh): The soundest or most correct opinion among the Hanafi authorities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, does a person who swallows a fresh almond (with its peel) incur kaffarah?
A) Yes, because the almond is nutritious and the peel is customarily eaten with it.
B) No, because the peel is not edible.
C) Yes, because any food breaks the fast.
D) No, because almonds are not considered food for kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah for eating in fasting is only required for substances that are customarily eaten for nutritional benefit; harmful or non-beneficial substances do not incur kaffarah.

Custom as the Criterion for Kaffarah: The Case of Nuts with Shells
Defines the criterion distinguishing nutritious from non-nutritious food for kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi custom-based criterion for nutritious food in kaffarah and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on swallowing a nut with its shell, identifying the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Custom ('urf) determines whether a substance triggers kaffarah, not its nutritional value.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah clarifies the Hanafi criterion for what counts as 'nutritious' (ma yutaghaddha bihi) in the context of fasting kaffarah. The key is custom: only substances customarily eaten—whether as a main food or accompaniment—require kaffarah if consumed deliberately. The text then applies this rule to nuts with shells and presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: does chewing a walnut or almond with its shell change the ruling? Understanding this dispute deepens your grasp of how custom, doubt, and the weight of later fatwa operate within the school.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi criterion for kaffarah
Identify the primary rule: any substance customarily eaten (ma yutaghaddha bihi) triggers kaffarah when swallowed deliberately during a fast. Non-customary substances, even if nutritious, do not.
The passage explicitly states: 'كل ما يؤكل عادة مقصوداً أو تبعاً لغيره فهو مما يتغذى به'.
2
Apply the criterion to swallowing whole nuts with shells
Determine the ruling for a wet walnut or dry almond swallowed whole: since they are not customarily eaten with the shell, no kaffarah. Contrast with a wet almond, which is customarily eaten with its shell—kaffarah required.
Custom varies by substance; the shell of a wet almond is considered edible, while that of a walnut or dry almond is not.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute when the nut is chewed
Note Abu Yusuf's view: if the walnut or dry almond is chewed and the kernel (eaten) and shell (not eaten) both enter the stomach, kaffarah is required because the kernel is definitely eaten. Imam Muhammad holds no kaffarah due to doubt.
The dispute hinges on whether the presence of the non-eaten shell creates doubt that prevents obligation.
4
Analyze the doubt-based argument from Majma' al-Riwayat
Understand the counterargument: if the act involves both nutritious and non-nutritious elements, kaffarah's obligation is uncertain, and doubt prohibits its imposition, similar to swallowing it whole.
This argument is rejected in the mufta bihi because the kernel's consumption is certain.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
Recognize that Qadi Khan affirmed Abu Yusuf's view. Among later Hanafi authorities, this is the relied-upon position for fatwa, requiring kaffarah when a nut with its shell is chewed and swallowed.
The tarjih is based on the principle of caution (ihtiyat) and the clarity of the kernel being eaten.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الفاصل بين ما يتغذى به وبين ما لا يتغذى أن كل ما يؤكل عادة مقصوداً أو تبعاً لغيره فهو مما يتغذى به، وكل ما لا يؤكل عادة أصلاً فهو ملحق بما لا يتغذى به، وإن كان في نفسه مغذياً، ولو ابتلع جوزة رطبة، أو لوزة يابسة لم تلزمه الكفارة، لأنه لا يؤكل مع قشره عادة، ولو ابتلع لوزة رطبة تلزمه الكفارة لأنها تؤكل عادة مع القشر، وعن أبي يوسف إذا مضغ الجوزة أو اللوزة اليابسة بقشرها حتى وصل إلى جوفه تلزمه الكفارة، لأنه أكل ما هو مأكول وهو اللب مع ما ليس بمأكول وهو القشر انتهى. وبـه جزم « قاضي خان ولم يذكره مسنداً ولم يذكر غيره انتهى. ثم قال في مجمع الـروايـات»، وقيل: لا تلزمه الكفارة؛ لأن اعتبار وقوع الفطر بما يتغذى به، إن كان يوجب الكفارة، فاعتبار وقوع الفطـر بـمـا لا يتغذى به يمنع وجوب الكفارة، فوقع الشك في وجوبها، فلا تجب بالشك كما لو ابتلعها انتهى.
Then the distinction between what is nutritious and what is not is that everything that is customarily eaten, intentionally or as an accompaniment to something else, is considered nutritious; and everything that is not customarily eaten at all is included with what is not nutritious, even if it is in itself nutritious. And if one swallows a wet walnut or a dry almond, kaffarah is not required of him, because it is not customarily eaten with its shell. And if one swallows a wet almond, kaffarah is required because it is customarily eaten with the shell. And from Abu Yusuf: if he chewed the walnut or dry almond with its shell until the chewed material reached his stomach, kaffarah is required because he ate what is eaten (the kernel) along with what is not eaten (the shell). End. Qadi Khan affirmed this and did not attribute it to an authority and did not mention other than it. End. Then he said in Majma' al-Riwayat: And it is said: Kaffarah is not required because if considering that breaking the fast with nutritious food requires kaffarah, then considering that breaking the fast with non-nutritious food prevents the obligation of kaffarah, so doubt arises in its obligation, and it is not obligated due to doubt, just as if he swallowed it. End.
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi criterion for determining when eating a substance during fasting requires both qada' and kaffarah, as opposed to qada' alone. The key principle is custom ('urf): only substances that are customarily eaten—either as a main food or as an accompaniment—are considered "nutritious" (ma yutaghaddha bihi) and thus trigger kaffarah if swallowed. Substances not customarily eaten, even if they have nutritional value, are treated as non-nutritious. The text illustrates this with examples: a wet walnut or dry almond swallowed whole with its shell does not require kaffarah because they are not eaten that way. However, a wet almond (eaten with shell customarily) does. Then it presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that if one chews these items so that kernel and shell both enter the stomach, kaffarah is required because the kernel is eaten. The earlier position (Imam Muhammad) does not require kaffarah in such cases. Qadi Khan preferred Abu Yusuf's view. Finally, a doubt-based argument from Majma' al-Riwayat states that kaffarah should not be imposed when the substance's status is uncertain, as the conflicting considerations cancel out.

Key Terms
ما يتغذى به (ma yutaghaddha bihi): A substance customarily eaten as food or accompaniment, whether nutritious or not.
قشر (qishr): The outer shell or peel of a nut that is not customarily eaten.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation required for deliberately breaking a fast by eating something nutritious; typically freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary criterion for determining whether eating a substance during fasting requires kaffarah?
A) The nutritional value of the substance
B) Whether the substance is customarily eaten
C) Whether the substance is solid or liquid
D) The quantity of the substance consumed

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi criterion for kaffarah is custom, not nutrition, and when the kernel is eaten with its shell, the mufta bihi (Abu Yusuf) requires kaffarah if chewed.

Essential Rulings on Kaffarah for Food and Drink Items
Listing food items that require kaffarah with intra-Hanafi details

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which food and drink items require kaffarah when intentionally consumed while fasting, and apply the general principle that normally consumed food and medicine incur both qada' and kaffarah.

Core Idea
Any normally edible item consumed deliberately during fasting requires both qada' and kaffarah.

This lesson delves into the detailed enumeration of food and drink items that break the fast and require expiation (kaffarah) in the Hanafi school. The passage covers raw meat, fat, grains, walnuts, and other edibles, establishing that the criterion is whether the item is customarily consumed as food or medicine. It also highlights the distinction between qada' alone and qada' plus kaffarah, as seen with pomegranates and eggs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each item listed (e.g., raw meat, fat, wheat, etc.), determine whether kaffarah is required. Note the exceptions (e.g., wormy meat, dissipating grains).
Most items require kaffarah, but some only require qada' (pomegranate/egg).
2
Apply the general principle
The passage states: 'what is consumed as food or medicine incurs kaffarah'. Use this to judge novel items.
The principle is based on the completeness of the offense and human nature seeking nourishment.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Examine the walnut case: if the peel enters first, no kaffarah; if the pulp, yes. This involves different narrations from Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the detailed distinction.
4
Distinguish qada' from kaffarah
Some items require only qada' (pomegranate, egg) because they are not normally eaten in that form. Understand the difference.
Kaffarah is a heavier penalty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يُتداوى بهِ، وابتلاعُ مطر دخل إلى فمه، وأكل اللحم النيئ، وإنْ كانَ مُنْتِنَا إِلَّا إِذَا دَوَّدَ ، وأكلُ الشَّحْمِ، في اختيار الفقيه أبي اللَّيْثِ وقَدِيدِ اللحم بالاتفاق. وأكلُ الحِنْطَةِ، وَقَضْمُهَا، إِلَّا أَنْ يَمَضغَ قَمْحَةً فتلاشت وابتلاعُ حَبَّةٍ حِنْطَةٍ أو سَمْسِمَةٍ، أَوْ نحوهَا، مِنْ خارج فَمِهِ . الكفارة إذا كان فيها اللب لما قلنا في الجوزة انتهى وفي ( التجنيس » : وعن محمد في الجوزة الرطبة لـو مضغها مع قشرها حتى وصل الممضوغ إلى جوفه، فعليه الكفارة، وقال: يعني: المؤلف نفسه له هكذا ذكر صاحب ( الأجناس وعن محمد يجب مطلقاً، وهكذا روي عن أبي يوسف مطلقاً مــن غـيـر تفصيل، قال مشايخنا رحمهم الله: إن وصل القشر أولاً إلى حلقه فلا كفارة عليه وإن وصل اللـب أولاً فعليه كفارة؛ لأن في الوجه الأول الفطر حصل بالقشر وفي الفصل الثاني حصل باللب انتهى. قلت: وإذا وصلا معاً فقد ذكرنا ما يفيد حكمه عن ( مجمع الروايات) انتهى. وإن ابتلعها إن لم تكن مشقوقة الرأس فلا كفارة فيه عند الكل، وإن كانت مشقوقة فكذلك عند العامة. وقال بعضهم: إن كانت مملوحة فيها كفارة، وإن لم تكن مملوحة لا كفارة، وإن ابتلع تفاحة. روى هشام عن محمد إن عليه الكفارة؛ لأن جميعها مأكول انتهى. وتجب الكفارة لـو ابتلع هليلجة في الصحيح كما في التاتارخانية»، عن « الخلاصة ) ، وبابتلاع الرمانة والبيض القضاء دون الكفارة؛ لأنها لا تؤكل كذلك انتهى. وقال في «شرح الشيخ المقدسي : وبأكل نحو ورق كرم وقشر بطيخ طــري وكافور ومسك تجب انتهى. وقوله: طري قيد في ورق الكرم أيضاً، لما في «التجنيس والمزيد » أكـــل ورق الكرم في الابتداء عليه القضاء والكفارة، وإذا كثر فعليه القضاء دون الكفارة، لأنه لا يؤكل عادة لأنـه صــار غليظاً انتهى. فلذا استوى في الحكم ما يتغذى به أو يُتَدَاوى بِهِ) كالأشربة، واللبان، والأطعمة، والخبز لتكامل الجناية، والطباع السليمة تدعو إلى تناول ما يتغذى به، أو ما يتداوى به لمـا فـيـه مـن إصلاح البدن فتقع الحاجة إلى شرع الزاجر فيه. (وَ) منه : ( ابتلاعُ) ماء ( مطر)، وثلج، وبرد (دخل إلى فمه) لإمكان التحرز عنه بتيسر طبق الفم، (وَ) منه: (أكل اللحم النيئ وإن كان منتنا) لــو مــن ميتة (إلا إذَا دَوَّدَ) لخروجـه بـه عـن الغذائية. (وَ) منه: (أكلُ الشَّحْمِ) في المختار كذا في التجنيس وهذا ( في اختيار الفقيه أبي اللّيْثِ رحمه الله، ولا خلاف في قديده، كذا في «الفتح» (و) كذا (قَدِيدِ اللّحم بالاتفاق) للعادة بأكله، (وَ) منه: (أكلُ) حب (الحِنْطَةِ، وقَضْمُهَا ) لما ذكرنا إلا أنْ يَمضغَ قَمْحَةً) أو قدرها من جنس ما يوجب الكفارة (فتلاشت) واستهلكت بالمضغ فلم يجد لها طعماً، فلا كفارة بل ولا فساد لصومه به كما قدمناه (و) من موجب الكفارة ( ابتلاع حبة حنطة، أو ابتلاع (سمسمة، أو ) ابتلاع (نحوها ) وقد تناولها ( من خارج فيه، ولزوم الكفارة بهذا في المختارِ، وأكلُ الطِّينِ الأرْمَنِي، مطلقاً، والطين غير الأرمني، كالطفل، إنِ اعْتَادَ أَكْلَهُ، وَاللحُ القَلِيلُ، في المختارِ. وابتلاعُ بُزَاق زوجتِهِ، أو صديقِهِ، لا غيرهما. وأَكْلُهُ عَمْداً بعدَ غِيبَةٍ .
Or what is used as medicine, and swallowing rainwater that enters his mouth, and eating raw meat even if it is stinking except if it becomes wormy, and eating fat, in the choice of Abu al-Layth, and dried meat by agreement. And eating wheat and chewing it, unless he chews a grain and it dissipates, and swallowing a grain of wheat or sesame or similar from outside his mouth. The expiation if there is pulp in it as we said regarding the walnut. And in al-Tajnis: from Muhammad regarding the fresh walnut: if he chewed it with its peel until the chewed matter reached his stomach, he must expiate. And he said: the author himself said so. Thus the author of al-Ajnas mentioned: from Muhammad it is obligatory absolutely, and thus it is related from Abu Yusuf absolutely without detail. Our scholars said: if the peel reaches his throat first, no expiation; if the pulp reaches first, expiation; because in the first case the breaking of fast occurred by the peel, in the second by the pulp. I say: if they reach together, we have mentioned what benefits his ruling from Majma' al-Riwayat. And if he swallows it, if it is not split at the head, no expiation according to all; if it is split, similar according to the general body. Some said: if it is salted, expiation; if not salted, no expiation. And if he swallows an apple, Hisham narrated from Muhammad that he must expiate because all of it is eaten. And expiation is obligatory if he swallows a myrobalan in the correct view as in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Khulasa. And by swallowing a pomegranate and an egg, qada' without expiation because they are not eaten that way. And in Sharh al-Shaykh al-Maqdisi: by eating like grape leaves, fresh watermelon peel, camphor, and musk, expiation is obligatory. And his saying 'fresh' is a condition for grape leaves too, because in al-Tajnis and al-Mazid: eating grape leaves initially, qada' and expiation; if abundant, qada' without expiation because it is not usually eaten for it becomes thick. Therefore, the ruling is the same for what is consumed as food or medicine, like drinks, frankincense, foods, and bread, because the crime is complete, and sound natures incline to consuming what is food or medicine for bodily repair, so the need for a deterrent legislated. And from that: swallowing rainwater, snow, hail that enters his mouth, because it is possible to avoid by easily closing the mouth. And from that: eating raw meat even if stinking from a dead animal, except if it becomes wormy because it then leaves the category of food. And from that: eating fat, in the choice, thus in al-Tajnis, and this is in the choice of Abu al-Layth, and no dispute about its dried meat as in al-Fath. And similarly dried meat by agreement due to custom of eating it. And from that: eating wheat grain and chewing it as we mentioned, unless he chews a grain or the amount of what obligates expiation and it dissipates and is consumed by chewing so he does not find its taste, then no expiation nor even invalidation of his fast as we have stated. And from that which obligates expiation: swallowing a grain of wheat or swallowing a sesame seed or similar, having taken it from outside his mouth, and the obligation of expiation in this is the chosen view. And eating Armenian clay absolutely, and other than Armenian clay like potter's clay if he is accustomed to eating it. And a little salt, in the chosen view. And swallowing the saliva of his wife or friend, not others. And his eating intentionally after backbiting.
Source pages p. 647

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of actions that invalidate the fast and require kaffarah (expiation) in the Hanafi school. The commentary expands on the matn of Nur al-Idah, explaining the conditions and exceptions. Key points: Raw meat, even from a dead animal, requires kaffarah unless it is wormy (excluded from food). Fat and dried meat are included by agreement. Wheat and grains require kaffarah when swallowed whole or chewed, but if a grain dissipates in the mouth without taste, no kaffarah. The walnut case involves a intra-Hanafi dispute: if the peel reaches first, no kaffarah; if the pulp, yes; if together, there is a ruling from Majma' al-Riwayat. Swallowing a pomegranate or egg only requires qada' (make-up), not kaffarah, as they are not normally eaten in that manner. Grape leaves and fresh watermelon peel require kaffarah initially but not when abundant and thick. The general principle: anything normally consumed as food or medicine requires kaffarah. Swallowing rainwater, snow, etc. requires kaffarah because one can avoid by closing the mouth. Eating clay: Armenian clay always requires kaffarah; other clay only if one is accustomed. A little salt requires kaffarah; there is a difference of opinion on quantity. Swallowing the saliva of one's spouse or friend requires kaffarah due to pleasure; others do not. Eating after backbiting requires kaffarah due to a hadith that 'backbiting breaks the fast', interpreted by consensus as removing the reward, but here deliberate eating after backbiting incurs kaffarah.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation; a penalty required for deliberately invalidating the fast without a valid excuse, typically feeding sixty poor people or fasting two months consecutively.
قضى (qada'): Making up a missed fast on another day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling if a fasting person swallows a whole pomegranate?
A) Qada' and kaffarah are both required.
B) Only qada' is required, no kaffarah.
C) The fast is valid, no penalty.
D) Only kaffarah is required, no qada'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires kaffarah for deliberately consuming anything normally considered food or medicine, with specific exceptions for items not customarily eaten in that manner.

Sawm — Fasting

Specific Cases: Clay, Salt, Saliva, and Backbiting
Special non-food cases requiring kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling on kaffarah to non-food items such as clay and salt, and explain the unique cases of swallowing another's saliva and eating after backbiting.

Core Idea
Non-food items can also require kaffarah if consumed intentionally, with conditions based on custom and pleasure.

This lesson covers the remaining unique cases from the passage: eating clay (Armenian and non-Armenian), swallowing a little salt, swallowing the saliva of one's spouse or friend, and the case of eating deliberately after backbiting. Each has its own ruling based on the principles of custom, pleasure, and the hadith on backbiting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item and its ruling
For each item (clay, salt, saliva, backbiting), determine whether kaffarah is required. Note the conditions: e.g., custom for clay, quantity for salt, relationship for saliva.
Armenian clay always incurs kaffarah; other clay only if accustomed.
2
Apply the custom principle
For clay and salt, custom ('urf) plays a role. If one is accustomed to eating it, it is treated like food.
This highlights the Hanafi reliance on 'urf.
3
Analyze the saliva case
Swallowing the saliva of one's spouse or friend incurs kaffarah due to the element of pleasure; swallowing another's saliva does not.
The distinguishing factor is the pleasure derived, which makes it analogous to food.
4
Understand the backbiting case
If one intentionally eats after backbiting, kaffarah is required. The hadith 'backbiting breaks the fast' is interpreted as loss of reward, and eating deliberately after that incurs kaffarah.
This is a secondary ruling; the hadith refers to breaking the reward, not the fast itself.
