Eating Non-Food Substances: Clay and Salt
Lists rulings on eating clay, salt, barley, corn cobs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for eating Armenian clay, non-Armenian clay, salt, barley, and corn cobs during fasting and apply the principle of habitual consumption to determine whether kaffarah is required.

Core Idea
What counts as nutritive or medicinal determines kaffarah; habitual consumption changes the ruling.

The passage begins by listing substances that, when eaten deliberately during a fast, require both make-up (qada) and expiation (kaffarah). These include types of clay, salt, barley, and corn cobs. The key factor is whether the substance is considered nutritive or medicinal, and for non-Armenian clay, whether one habitually eats it. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind these classifications and the differences between the types of clay and salt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of substances mentioned
List the substances: Armenian clay, non-Armenian clay (tifl), salt, barley (dry vs. fresh/roasted), and corn cobs (ka'ub qawa'im al-dhurah). Note the different rulings attached to each.
Focus on the basis: Armenian clay is always kaffarah because it is eaten for medicine; non-Armenian clay only if habitually eaten.
2
Examine the principle of habitual consumption (i'tiyad)
For non-Armenian clay, the ruling depends on whether one habitually eats it. Habitual consumption makes it a regular nutrient, thus kaffarah is due; otherwise, it merely invalidates the fast without kaffarah.
This is a Hanafi nuance: the same substance can have different rulings based on the person's habit.
3
Analyze the salt rulings and the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Tracing the debate: some narrations require kaffarah for any amount, others only for a little; the relied-upon view (al-mukhtar) is that a small amount of salt requires kaffarah. Note that salt is considered an 'imtihani' (examinatory) issue, testing knowledge of different riwayat.
The commentator resolves the ikhtilaf by stating the mukhtar position: kaffarah for little salt.
4
Apply the ruling to barley and corn cobs
Barley: if dry and unroasted, no kaffarah; if roasted (or fresh from the ear), kaffarah is due. Corn cobs: no direct narration, but al-Zandawisti rules qada with kaffarah. Note the rationale: unroasted barley is not normally eaten, so it's not a nutrient.
This shows the principle of 'what is eaten for nourishment' triggers kaffarah.

(و) مما يوجب الكفاره أَكْلُهُ عَمْداً بعدَ غِيبَةٍ)، وهي : ذكر أخاك بما يكرهه حال غيبته، سواء بلغه الحديث أو لم يبلغه عرف تأويله أو لم يعرفه أفتاه مفت أو لم يفته؛ لأن الفطر بالغيبة يخالف القياس والحديث، وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « الغِيْبَةُ تُفْطِرُ الصَّائِمَ » ) مؤول بالإجماع بذهاب الثواب. بخلاف حديث الحجامة): فإن بعض العلماء أخذ بظاهره، مثل الأوزاعي وأحمد كما في العناية»، وقد قال قاضي خان : قال بعضهم هذا وفضل الحجامة سواء في الوجوه كلها، وعامة العلماء قالوا عليه الكفارة على كل حال انتهى. فلذا تجب الكفارة بأكله عمداً بعد غيبة، (أو) أكله ( بعد حِجَامَةٍ. أوْ ( أكله ( بعد مَسَّ ، أو أكله بعد (قُبْلَةٍ، بشهوة. أو ( أكله ( بعد مضاجعة) ومباشرة فاحشة ( من غير إنزال) ظاناً أنه أفطر بالمس، والقبلة لزمته الكفارة إلا إذا تأوّل حديثاً، أو استفتى فقيهاً فأفطر، ر، فلا كفارة عليه، وإن أخطأ الفقيه ولم يثبت الحديث لأن ظاهر الفتوى والحديث يصير شبهة، قاله « الكمال»، عن « البدائع » . ( أو ) أكله ( بعد دهن شاريه، ظانا أنه أفطر بذلك) لأنه متعمد، ولم يستند ظنه إلى دليل شرعي، فلزمته الكفارة، وإن استفتى فقيهاً فأفتاه بالفطر بدهن الشارب أو تأول حديثاً؛ لأنه لا يعتد بفتوى الفقيه ولا بتأويل الحديث هنا، لأن هذا مما لا يشتبه على من له شيمة من الفقه، نقله ( الكمال » عن ( البدائع » . قلت : لكن يخالفه ما في «قاضي خان»، وكذا الذي اكتحل أو دهن نفسه أو شاربه، ثم أكل متعمداً عليه الكفارة، إلا إذا كان جاهلاً فاستفتى فأفتى له بالفطر، فحينئذ لا تلزمه الكفارة انتهى. فعلى هذا يكون قولنا ( إلا : إذا أفتاه فقيه) شاملاً لمسألة دهن الشارب، والمراد بالفقيه متبع لمجتهد، كالحنابلة وبعض أهل الحديث، ممن يرى الحجامة مفطرة، فلا كفارة عليه لأن الواجب على العامي الأخذ بقول المفتي، فتصير الفتوى شبهة في حقه، وإن كانت خطأ في حقها، كذا في ( البرهان» ، ( أو ) إلا إذا سَمِع) المحتجم والحاجم (الحديث) وهو قوله : ( أَفْطَرَ الحَاجِمُ والمَحْجُومُ ) ( ولم يعرف تأويله على المذهب) لأن قول الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم [ لا يكون أدنى درجة من قول المفتي، وقول المفتي صلح عندنا فبقول الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ] أولى، وعن أبي يوسف أنها تجب، لأن العامي إذا سمع الحديث ليس له أن يأخذ بظاهره، لجواز أن يكون مصروفاً عن ظاهره أو منسوخاً، (وَ) لذا (إنْ عَرَفَ تأويلَهُ وجبَتْ عليه الكفّارة) لانتفاء الشبهة، وتأويله أنه مر بهما وهما يغتابان آخر، فقال : ذلك أي: ذهب صومهما بالغيبة، كما قدمناه ، وقد علمت
In al-Mukhtar: eating Armenian clay absolutely, and non-Armenian clay like dry clay (tifl) if one habitually eats it, and a small amount of salt, in al-Mukhtar. (In al-Mukhtar) because it is of the type that is consumed for nourishment; this is a narration from Muhammad, al-Kamal said: if one swallows barley, no kaffarah is due unless it is roasted, thus al-Quduri mentioned it because unroasted barley is not eaten; likewise in al-Tajnīs and al-Mazīd – this is for dry barley. As for fresh ears of corn, if he extracts and eats it, kaffarah is due, as in al-Tātārkhāniyyah from al-Hujjah. And from it: eating clay absolutely, i.e., whether he habitually eats it or not, because it is eaten as medicine, so it is a complete fast-breaking as in al-Tajnīs. And from it: eating non-Armenian clay called dry clay (tifl), if he habitually eats it, not for the one who does not habitually eat. And from it: eating a small amount of salt. He said in al-Mubtagha: kaffarah is required for eating a little salt, not much. In Sharḥ al-Dayrī, he said in al-Khulāṣah: if he eats salt, kaffarah is upon him, and it is the relied-upon view. Likewise it is absolute in al-Bazzāziyyah, he said: regarding salt, kaffarah is required in al-Mukhtar. Therefore I said: it is required for a little in al-Mukhtar. And in Majma' al-Riwāyāt: there are two narrations regarding eating salt; it is said that it is required for a little but not much, and it is from examinatory matters. If one eats the cores of corn stalks, there is no narration for this issue; al-Zandawīstī said: qada with kaffarah, as in al-Tātārkhāniyyah. And from it: swallowing the saliva of his wife or his friend because he derives pleasure, as stated in al-Kanz in miscellaneous issues. And in al-Tātārkhāniyyah: if he swallows the saliva of another, his fast is invalidated without kaffarah, except if it is the saliva of his friend, then kaffarah is obligatory because people do not find the saliva of their friends repulsive; therefore, kaffarah is not required for the saliva of others because he finds it repulsive. And from what necessitates kaffarah: his eating intentionally after backbiting (ghībah), which is: mentioning your brother in a manner he dislikes in his absence, whether the report reaches him or not, he knows its interpretation or not, a mufti gave a fatwa or not; because breaking the fast through backbiting is contrary to analogy and the hadith, which is his saying (peace be upon him): 'Backbiting breaks the fast of the fasting person' – interpreted by consensus as [nullifying] the reward, contrary to the hadith on cupping. Some scholars took it literally, like al-Awzā'ī and Aḥmad, as in al-‘Ināyah; and Qādī Khān said: some said this and cupping are equal in all respects; the majority of scholars said that kaffarah is upon him in all cases. Therefore, kaffarah is required for his eating intentionally after backbiting; or his eating after cupping; or his eating after touching; or his eating after kissing with desire; or his eating after sleeping together and obscene contact without ejaculation, thinking that he broke his fast by touching and kissing – kaffarah is due upon him unless he interpreted a hadith or sought a fatwa from a jurist and then broke his fast, then no kaffarah upon him, even if the jurist erred and the hadith is not established, because the apparent fatwa and hadith create a doubt (shubhah). Al-Kamāl said this from al-Badā’i‘. Or his eating after oiling his mustache, thinking that he broke his fast by that, because he is deliberate and his assumption is not based on a legal proof, so kaffarah is due. If he sought a fatwa from a jurist who gave a fatwa that oiling the mustache breaks the fast, or he interpreted a hadith, the fatwa or interpretation is not considered here because this is not obscure for one with any understanding of fiqh, as al-Kamāl transmitted from al-Badā’i‘. I (the commentator) say: but this is contradicted by what is in Qādī Khān: similarly, one who applies kohl or oils himself or his mustache, then eats intentionally – kaffarah is due, except if he was ignorant and sought a fatwa and was given a fatwa that it breaks the fast, then kaffarah is not required. According to this, our statement 'except if a jurist gives him a fatwa' includes the issue of oiling the mustache; and the jurist meant is a follower of a mujtahid, like the Ḥanābilah and some hadith scholars who consider cupping as breaking the fast – then no kaffarah upon him because it is obligatory for the layman to follow the mufti's statement, so the fatwa becomes a doubt in his favor, even if it is wrong in itself, as in al-Burhān. Or except if the cupper and cupped hear the hadith, which is his statement: 'The cupper and the cupped have broken their fast' and they do not know its interpretation according to the school, because the statement of the Messenger (peace be upon him) is not of a lesser degree than the statement of a mufti; and the mufti's statement is valid with us, so the Prophet's statement is more worthy. It is narrated from Abū Yūsuf that it is obligatory, because the layman when he hears a hadith may not take it literally, as it may be interpreted figuratively or abrogated. Therefore, if he knows its interpretation, kaffarah is due, because the doubt is removed. Its interpretation is that he passed by them while they were backbiting someone else and said: 'That is, their fast has gone due to backbiting,' as we mentioned earlier, and you have known.
Source pages p. 648

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling statement from the commentary on Nur al-Idah, listing various actions that require both qada and kaffarah when performed intentionally while fasting. The author organizes these actions under the rubric of what is considered 'nutritive' or 'pleasurable' intake. First, eating Armenian clay is absolutely cause for kaffarah, as it is eaten for medicinal purposes, thus considered a complete breaking of the fast. Non-Armenian clay (dry clay) only triggers kaffarah if one habitually eats it; otherwise, it is merely sinful but not requiring kaffarah. Eating even a small amount of salt is, in the relied-upon view (al-mukhtar), subject to kaffarah, though there is a weaker narration limiting it to small amounts; this is clarified as an 'examinatory' (imtihani) issue, meaning it tests the student's knowledge of different riwayat. Corn cobs (ka'ub qawa'im al-dhurah) also require both qada and kaffarah according to al-Zandawisti. Second, swallowing the saliva of one's wife or a close friend incurs kaffarah because it is deemed pleasurable; swallowing anyone else's saliva only invalidates the fast but does not require kaffarah, as it is repulsive. Third, eating intentionally after committing backbiting (ghibah) requires kaffarah. The passage defines ghibah as mentioning a fellow Muslim in a way he would dislike in his absence. The hadith 'Backbiting breaks the fast' is interpreted by consensus as meaning the loss of reward, not the invalidation of the fast itself. Therefore, the kaffarah here is for the subsequent eating, not for the ghibah itself. Fourth, eating after cupping (hijamah), touching (mass), kissing (qublah) with desire, or sleeping together (mudaja'ah) without ejaculation – all while mistakenly believing that these acts had already broken the fast – still require kaffarah, because the person deliberately ate without a valid legal excuse. However, an exception is made if the person acted upon a fatwa from a qualified jurist (faqih) or misinterpreted a hadith; in that case, the kaffarah is waived due to the presence of doubt (shubhah). The passage further elaborates that even if the jurist's fatwa was incorrect, the layman is excused because his duty is to follow the mufti. The same applies to oiling one's mustache; if a person thought that broke the fast and ate, kaffarah is due, unless he obtained a fatwa to that effect. The discussion then turns to the hadith 'The cupper and the cupped break their fast,' which some scholars (like al-Awza'i and Ahmad) took literally, but the Hanafi school interprets it as referring to backbiting, as in the story where the Prophet passed by people cupping and they were backbiting. The passage concludes that if a person hears this hadith but does not know its interpretation, he may be excused due to doubt; if he knows the correct interpretation, kaffarah is obligatory. This entire discussion demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of balancing textual evidence (dalil) with rational principles (qiyas) and the concept of shubhah (legal doubt) in imposing expiation.

Key Terms
الطين الأرمني (al-ṭīn al-armānī): Armenian clay, eaten for medicinal purposes; always requires kaffarah if eaten during fast.
الطفل (al-ṭifl): Non-Armenian dry clay; kaffarah only if habitually eaten.
الامتحانيات (al-imtiḥāniyyāt): Examinatory matters – issues where different riwayat exist and testing knowledge of them is important.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does eating non-Armenian clay (al-ṭīn ghayr al-armānī) require kaffarah?
A) Always
B) Only if one habitually eats it
C) Only if it is eaten for medicine
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah for eating non-food substances depends on their nutritive/medicinal value and whether one habitually consumes them.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
5 min
Swallowing Saliva of Others: Pleasure vs Disgust
Ruling on swallowing saliva of spouse, friend, others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for swallowing saliva of one's spouse, friend, or a stranger during fasting and apply the principle of pleasure vs. disgust to determine whether kaffarah is required.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is due for swallowing saliva of those whose saliva is considered pleasurable or not repulsive.

The passage addresses a specific scenario: a fasting person intentionally swallows the saliva of another person. The ruling hinges on whether the saliva is considered pleasurable or repulsive. Swallowing the saliva of one's spouse or a close friend incurs kaffarah because it is deemed pleasurable; swallowing that of a stranger does not, as it is considered repulsive. This lesson dissects the reasoning and the exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of persons
The passage distinguishes between: (1) spouse, (2) friend (sadiq), and (3) others (ghayruhuma). Note the ruling for each: spouse and friend require kaffarah; others only invalidate the fast without kaffarah.
The key is the feeling of pleasure (taladhdhudh) versus disgust (i'afah).
2
Examine the reasoning: pleasure vs. disgust
Spouse's saliva is pleasurable, so intentional swallowing is akin to consuming something enjoyable, triggering kaffarah. Friend's saliva is not repulsive to close friends, so it also triggers kaffarah. Others' saliva is generally repulsive, so no kaffarah.
The author cites al-Kanz and al-Tātārkhāniyyah to support this distinction.
3
Apply the rule to borderline cases
What about a casual acquaintance? The text specifies 'sadiq' (friend), implying a close relationship. The ruling would likely follow the 'others' category if not close.
This is a case of 'illa' (effective cause) being the presence or absence of pleasure/disgust.

(وَ) منه: (ابتلاعُ بزاق زوجتِهِ، أو بزاق (صديقِهِ ) لأنه يتلذذ به، كما قاله في «الكنز» في مسائل شتى وفي « التاتارخانية » : وإذا ابتلع بزاق غيره فسد صومه بغير كفارة، إلا إذا كان بزاق صديقة فحينئذ يلزمه الكفارة لأن الناس لا يعافون بزاق أصدقائهم، فلذا ( لا ) تلزم الكفارة ببزاق (غيرهما) لأنه يعافه. (و) مما يوجب الكفاره أَكْلُهُ عَمْداً بعدَ غِيبَةٍ)، وهي : ذكر أخاك بما يكرهه حال غيبته، سواء بلغه الحديث أو لم يبلغه عرف تأويله أو لم يعرفه أفتاه مفت أو لم يفته؛ لأن الفطر بالغيبة يخالف القياس والحديث، وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « الغِيْبَةُ تُفْطِرُ الصَّائِمَ » ) مؤول بالإجماع بذهاب الثواب. بخلاف حديث الحجامة): فإن بعض العلماء أخذ بظاهره، مثل الأوزاعي وأحمد كما في العناية»، وقد قال قاضي خان : قال بعضهم هذا وفضل الحجامة سواء في الوجوه كلها، وعامة العلماء قالوا عليه الكفارة على كل حال انتهى. فلذا تجب الكفارة بأكله عمداً بعد غيبة، (أو) أكله ( بعد حِجَامَةٍ. أوْ ( أكله ( بعد مَسَّ ، أو أكله بعد (قُبْلَةٍ، بشهوة. أو ( أكله ( بعد مضاجعة) ومباشرة فاحشة ( من غير إنزال) ظاناً أنه أفطر بالمس، والقبلة لزمته الكفارة إلا إذا تأوّل حديثاً، أو استفتى فقيهاً فأفطر، ر، فلا كفارة عليه، وإن أخطأ الفقيه ولم يثبت الحديث لأن ظاهر الفتوى والحديث يصير شبهة، قاله « الكمال»، عن « البدائع » . ( أو ) أكله ( بعد دهن شاريه، ظانا أنه أفطر بذلك) لأنه متعمد، ولم يستند ظنه إلى دليل شرعي، فلزمته الكفارة، وإن استفتى فقيهاً فأفتاه بالفطر بدهن الشارب أو تأول حديثاً؛ لأنه لا يعتد بفتوى الفقيه ولا بتأويل الحديث هنا، لأن هذا مما لا يشتبه على من له شيمة من الفقه، نقله ( الكمال » عن ( البدائع » . قلت : لكن يخالفه ما في «قاضي خان»، وكذا الذي اكتحل أو دهن نفسه أو شاربه، ثم أكل متعمداً عليه الكفارة، إلا إذا كان جاهلاً فاستفتى فأفتى له بالفطر، فحينئذ لا تلزمه الكفارة انتهى. فعلى هذا يكون قولنا ( إلا : إذا أفتاه فقيه) شاملاً لمسألة دهن الشارب، والمراد بالفقيه متبع لمجتهد، كالحنابلة وبعض أهل الحديث، ممن يرى الحجامة مفطرة، فلا كفارة عليه لأن الواجب على العامي الأخذ بقول المفتي، فتصير الفتوى شبهة في حقه، وإن كانت خطأ في حقها، كذا في ( البرهان» ، ( أو ) إلا إذا سَمِع) المحتجم والحاجم (الحديث) وهو قوله : ( أَفْطَرَ الحَاجِمُ والمَحْجُومُ ) ( ولم يعرف تأويله على المذهب) لأن قول الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم [ لا يكون أدنى درجة من قول المفتي، وقول المفتي صلح عندنا فبقول الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ] أولى، وعن أبي يوسف أنها تجب، لأن العامي إذا سمع الحديث ليس له أن يأخذ بظاهره، لجواز أن يكون مصروفاً عن ظاهره أو منسوخاً، (وَ) لذا (إنْ عَرَفَ تأويلَهُ وجبَتْ عليه الكفّارة) لانتفاء الشبهة، وتأويله أنه مر بهما وهما يغتابان آخر، فقال : ذلك أي: ذهب صومهما بالغيبة، كما قدمناه ، وقد علمت
In al-Mukhtar: eating Armenian clay absolutely, and non-Armenian clay like dry clay (tifl) if one habitually eats it, and a small amount of salt, in al-Mukhtar. (In al-Mukhtar) because it is of the type that is consumed for nourishment; this is a narration from Muhammad, al-Kamal said: if one swallows barley, no kaffarah is due unless it is roasted, thus al-Quduri mentioned it because unroasted barley is not eaten; likewise in al-Tajnīs and al-Mazīd – this is for dry barley. As for fresh ears of corn, if he extracts and eats it, kaffarah is due, as in al-Tātārkhāniyyah from al-Hujjah. And from it: eating clay absolutely, i.e., whether he habitually eats it or not, because it is eaten as medicine, so it is a complete fast-breaking as in al-Tajnīs. And from it: eating non-Armenian clay called dry clay (tifl), if he habitually eats it, not for the one who does not habitually eat. And from it: eating a small amount of salt. He said in al-Mubtagha: kaffarah is required for eating a little salt, not much. In Sharḥ al-Dayrī, he said in al-Khulāṣah: if he eats salt, kaffarah is upon him, and it is the relied-upon view. Likewise it is absolute in al-Bazzāziyyah, he said: regarding salt, kaffarah is required in al-Mukhtar. Therefore I said: it is required for a little in al-Mukhtar. And in Majma' al-Riwāyāt: there are two narrations regarding eating salt; it is said that it is required for a little but not much, and it is from examinatory matters. If one eats the cores of corn stalks, there is no narration for this issue; al-Zandawīstī said: qada with kaffarah, as in al-Tātārkhāniyyah. And from it: swallowing the saliva of his wife or his friend because he derives pleasure, as stated in al-Kanz in miscellaneous issues. And in al-Tātārkhāniyyah: if he swallows the saliva of another, his fast is invalidated without kaffarah, except if it is the saliva of his friend, then kaffarah is obligatory because people do not find the saliva of their friends repulsive; therefore, kaffarah is not required for the saliva of others because he finds it repulsive. And from what necessitates kaffarah: his eating intentionally after backbiting (ghībah), which is: mentioning your brother in a manner he dislikes in his absence, whether the report reaches him or not, he knows its interpretation or not, a mufti gave a fatwa or not; because breaking the fast through backbiting is contrary to analogy and the hadith, which is his saying (peace be upon him): 'Backbiting breaks the fast of the fasting person' – interpreted by consensus as [nullifying] the reward, contrary to the hadith on cupping. Some scholars took it literally, like al-Awzā'ī and Aḥmad, as in al-‘Ināyah; and Qādī Khān said: some said this and cupping are equal in all respects; the majority of scholars said that kaffarah is upon him in all cases. Therefore, kaffarah is required for his eating intentionally after backbiting; or his eating after cupping; or his eating after touching; or his eating after kissing with desire; or his eating after sleeping together and obscene contact without ejaculation, thinking that he broke his fast by touching and kissing – kaffarah is due upon him unless he interpreted a hadith or sought a fatwa from a jurist and then broke his fast, then no kaffarah upon him, even if the jurist erred and the hadith is not established, because the apparent fatwa and hadith create a doubt (shubhah). Al-Kamāl said this from al-Badā’i‘. Or his eating after oiling his mustache, thinking that he broke his fast by that, because he is deliberate and his assumption is not based on a legal proof, so kaffarah is due. If he sought a fatwa from a jurist who gave a fatwa that oiling the mustache breaks the fast, or he interpreted a hadith, the fatwa or interpretation is not considered here because this is not obscure for one with any understanding of fiqh, as al-Kamāl transmitted from al-Badā’i‘. I (the commentator) say: but this is contradicted by what is in Qādī Khān: similarly, one who applies kohl or oils himself or his mustache, then eats intentionally – kaffarah is due, except if he was ignorant and sought a fatwa and was given a fatwa that it breaks the fast, then kaffarah is not required. According to this, our statement 'except if a jurist gives him a fatwa' includes the issue of oiling the mustache; and the jurist meant is a follower of a mujtahid, like the Ḥanābilah and some hadith scholars who consider cupping as breaking the fast – then no kaffarah upon him because it is obligatory for the layman to follow the mufti's statement, so the fatwa becomes a doubt in his favor, even if it is wrong in itself, as in al-Burhān. Or except if the cupper and cupped hear the hadith, which is his statement: 'The cupper and the cupped have broken their fast' and they do not know its interpretation according to the school, because the statement of the Messenger (peace be upon him) is not of a lesser degree than the statement of a mufti; and the mufti's statement is valid with us, so the Prophet's statement is more worthy. It is narrated from Abū Yūsuf that it is obligatory, because the layman when he hears a hadith may not take it literally, as it may be interpreted figuratively or abrogated. Therefore, if he knows its interpretation, kaffarah is due, because the doubt is removed. Its interpretation is that he passed by them while they were backbiting someone else and said: 'That is, their fast has gone due to backbiting,' as we mentioned earlier, and you have known.
Source pages p. 648

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling statement from the commentary on Nur al-Idah, listing various actions that require both qada and kaffarah when performed intentionally while fasting. The author organizes these actions under the rubric of what is considered 'nutritive' or 'pleasurable' intake. First, eating Armenian clay is absolutely cause for kaffarah, as it is eaten for medicinal purposes, thus considered a complete breaking of the fast. Non-Armenian clay (dry clay) only triggers kaffarah if one habitually eats it; otherwise, it is merely sinful but not requiring kaffarah. Eating even a small amount of salt is, in the relied-upon view (al-mukhtar), subject to kaffarah, though there is a weaker narration limiting it to small amounts; this is clarified as an 'examinatory' (imtihani) issue, meaning it tests the student's knowledge of different riwayat. Corn cobs (ka'ub qawa'im al-dhurah) also require both qada and kaffarah according to al-Zandawisti. Second, swallowing the saliva of one's wife or a close friend incurs kaffarah because it is deemed pleasurable; swallowing anyone else's saliva only invalidates the fast but does not require kaffarah, as it is repulsive. Third, eating intentionally after committing backbiting (ghibah) requires kaffarah. The passage defines ghibah as mentioning a fellow Muslim in a way he would dislike in his absence. The hadith 'Backbiting breaks the fast' is interpreted by consensus as meaning the loss of reward, not the invalidation of the fast itself. Therefore, the kaffarah here is for the subsequent eating, not for the ghibah itself. Fourth, eating after cupping (hijamah), touching (mass), kissing (qublah) with desire, or sleeping together (mudaja'ah) without ejaculation – all while mistakenly believing that these acts had already broken the fast – still require kaffarah, because the person deliberately ate without a valid legal excuse. However, an exception is made if the person acted upon a fatwa from a qualified jurist (faqih) or misinterpreted a hadith; in that case, the kaffarah is waived due to the presence of doubt (shubhah). The passage further elaborates that even if the jurist's fatwa was incorrect, the layman is excused because his duty is to follow the mufti. The same applies to oiling one's mustache; if a person thought that broke the fast and ate, kaffarah is due, unless he obtained a fatwa to that effect. The discussion then turns to the hadith 'The cupper and the cupped break their fast,' which some scholars (like al-Awza'i and Ahmad) took literally, but the Hanafi school interprets it as referring to backbiting, as in the story where the Prophet passed by people cupping and they were backbiting. The passage concludes that if a person hears this hadith but does not know its interpretation, he may be excused due to doubt; if he knows the correct interpretation, kaffarah is obligatory. This entire discussion demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of balancing textual evidence (dalil) with rational principles (qiyas) and the concept of shubhah (legal doubt) in imposing expiation.

Key Terms
التلذذ (al-taladhdhudh): Pleasure derived from something, used here as the criterion for requiring kaffarah when swallowing saliva.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, swallowing the saliva of which of the following requires kaffarah?
A) A stranger
B) A close friend
C) A casual acquaintance
D) A business partner

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah for swallowing another's saliva depends on whether it is considered pleasurable or not repulsive.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
7 min
Eating After Backbiting: Interpreting the Hadith
Ruling on eating after backbiting with hadith interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that eating intentionally after backbiting requires kaffarah and evaluate the interpretation of the hadith 'al-ghībatu tufṭiru al-ṣā’im'.

Core Idea
Backbiting destroys fasting reward, but kaffarah is only for the subsequent eating, not the backbiting itself.

The passage discusses a controversial issue: does backbiting break the fast and require kaffarah? The Hanafi school interprets the hadith 'Backbiting breaks the fast' as referring to loss of reward, not invalidation. However, if a person backbites and then intentionally eats believing the fast is already broken, kaffarah is due for the eating. This lesson examines the evidence and the role of consensus in interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the hadith
The issue: does backbiting (ghibah) break the fast? The hadith: 'Al-ghibatu tufṭiru al-ṣā’im' (Backbiting breaks the fast). The Hanafi school does not take it literally.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and open to interpretation.
2
Understand the Hanafi interpretation
The Hanafis interpret 'tufṭiru' as meaning 'nullifies the reward' (dhahab al-thawab), not the fast itself. This interpretation is based on consensus (ijma') of the majority and qiyas (fast is only invalidated by ingestion).
The text says: 'مؤول بالإجماع بذهاب الثواب'.
3
Analyze the consequence: eating after backbiting
If someone backbites and then eats intentionally, kaffarah is required for the eating, not for the backbiting. The backbiting creates a sin but does not invalidate the fast; the eating is a separate act of fast-breaking.
The passage explicitly says: 'فلذا تجب الكفارة بأكله عمداً بعد غيبة'.
4
Contrast with the cupping hadith
The text compares to the cupping hadith: some scholars take it literally, but Hanafis interpret it figuratively based on a story linking cupping to backbiting.
This comparison shows the Hanafi method of reconciling conflicting evidence.

In al-Mukhtar: eating Armenian clay absolutely, and non-Armenian clay like dry clay (tifl) if one habitually eats it, and a small amount of salt, in al-Mukhtar. (In al-Mukhtar) because it is of the type that is consumed for nourishment; this is a narration from Muhammad, al-Kamal said: if one swallows barley, no kaffarah is due unless it is roasted, thus al-Quduri mentioned it because unroasted barley is not eaten; likewise in al-Tajnīs and al-Mazīd – this is for dry barley. As for fresh ears of corn, if he extracts and eats it, kaffarah is due, as in al-Tātārkhāniyyah from al-Hujjah. And from it: eating clay absolutely, i.e., whether he habitually eats it or not, because it is eaten as medicine, so it is a complete fast-breaking as in al-Tajnīs. And from it: eating non-Armenian clay called dry clay (tifl), if he habitually eats it, not for the one who does not habitually eat. And from it: eating a small amount of salt. He said in al-Mubtagha: kaffarah is required for eating a little salt, not much. In Sharḥ al-Dayrī, he said in al-Khulāṣah: if he eats salt, kaffarah is upon him, and it is the relied-upon view. Likewise it is absolute in al-Bazzāziyyah, he said: regarding salt, kaffarah is required in al-Mukhtar. Therefore I said: it is required for a little in al-Mukhtar. And in Majma' al-Riwāyāt: there are two narrations regarding eating salt; it is said that it is required for a little but not much, and it is from examinatory matters. If one eats the cores of corn stalks, there is no narration for this issue; al-Zandawīstī said: qada with kaffarah, as in al-Tātārkhāniyyah. And from it: swallowing the saliva of his wife or his friend because he derives pleasure, as stated in al-Kanz in miscellaneous issues. And in al-Tātārkhāniyyah: if he swallows the saliva of another, his fast is invalidated without kaffarah, except if it is the saliva of his friend, then kaffarah is obligatory because people do not find the saliva of their friends repulsive; therefore, kaffarah is not required for the saliva of others because he finds it repulsive. And from what necessitates kaffarah: his eating intentionally after backbiting (ghībah), which is: mentioning your brother in a manner he dislikes in his absence, whether the report reaches him or not, he knows its interpretation or not, a mufti gave a fatwa or not; because breaking the fast through backbiting is contrary to analogy and the hadith, which is his saying (peace be upon him): 'Backbiting breaks the fast of the fasting person' – interpreted by consensus as [nullifying] the reward, contrary to the hadith on cupping. Some scholars took it literally, like al-Awzā'ī and Aḥmad, as in al-‘Ināyah; and Qādī Khān said: some said this and cupping are equal in all respects; the majority of scholars said that kaffarah is upon him in all cases. Therefore, kaffarah is required for his eating intentionally after backbiting; or his eating after cupping; or his eating after touching; or his eating after kissing with desire; or his eating after sleeping together and obscene contact without ejaculation, thinking that he broke his fast by touching and kissing – kaffarah is due upon him unless he interpreted a hadith or sought a fatwa from a jurist and then broke his fast, then no kaffarah upon him, even if the jurist erred and the hadith is not established, because the apparent fatwa and hadith create a doubt (shubhah). Al-Kamāl said this from al-Badā’i‘. Or his eating after oiling his mustache, thinking that he broke his fast by that, because he is deliberate and his assumption is not based on a legal proof, so kaffarah is due. If he sought a fatwa from a jurist who gave a fatwa that oiling the mustache breaks the fast, or he interpreted a hadith, the fatwa or interpretation is not considered here because this is not obscure for one with any understanding of fiqh, as al-Kamāl transmitted from al-Badā’i‘. I (the commentator) say: but this is contradicted by what is in Qādī Khān: similarly, one who applies kohl or oils himself or his mustache, then eats intentionally – kaffarah is due, except if he was ignorant and sought a fatwa and was given a fatwa that it breaks the fast, then kaffarah is not required. According to this, our statement 'except if a jurist gives him a fatwa' includes the issue of oiling the mustache; and the jurist meant is a follower of a mujtahid, like the Ḥanābilah and some hadith scholars who consider cupping as breaking the fast – then no kaffarah upon him because it is obligatory for the layman to follow the mufti's statement, so the fatwa becomes a doubt in his favor, even if it is wrong in itself, as in al-Burhān. Or except if the cupper and cupped hear the hadith, which is his statement: 'The cupper and the cupped have broken their fast' and they do not know its interpretation according to the school, because the statement of the Messenger (peace be upon him) is not of a lesser degree than the statement of a mufti; and the mufti's statement is valid with us, so the Prophet's statement is more worthy. It is narrated from Abū Yūsuf that it is obligatory, because the layman when he hears a hadith may not take it literally, as it may be interpreted figuratively or abrogated. Therefore, if he knows its interpretation, kaffarah is due, because the doubt is removed. Its interpretation is that he passed by them while they were backbiting someone else and said: 'That is, their fast has gone due to backbiting,' as we mentioned earlier, and you have known.
Source pages p. 648

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling statement from the commentary on Nur al-Idah, listing various actions that require both qada and kaffarah when performed intentionally while fasting. The author organizes these actions under the rubric of what is considered 'nutritive' or 'pleasurable' intake. First, eating Armenian clay is absolutely cause for kaffarah, as it is eaten for medicinal purposes, thus considered a complete breaking of the fast. Non-Armenian clay (dry clay) only triggers kaffarah if one habitually eats it; otherwise, it is merely sinful but not requiring kaffarah. Eating even a small amount of salt is, in the relied-upon view (al-mukhtar), subject to kaffarah, though there is a weaker narration limiting it to small amounts; this is clarified as an 'examinatory' (imtihani) issue, meaning it tests the student's knowledge of different riwayat. Corn cobs (ka'ub qawa'im al-dhurah) also require both qada and kaffarah according to al-Zandawisti. Second, swallowing the saliva of one's wife or a close friend incurs kaffarah because it is deemed pleasurable; swallowing anyone else's saliva only invalidates the fast but does not require kaffarah, as it is repulsive. Third, eating intentionally after committing backbiting (ghibah) requires kaffarah. The passage defines ghibah as mentioning a fellow Muslim in a way he would dislike in his absence. The hadith 'Backbiting breaks the fast' is interpreted by consensus as meaning the loss of reward, not the invalidation of the fast itself. Therefore, the kaffarah here is for the subsequent eating, not for the ghibah itself. Fourth, eating after cupping (hijamah), touching (mass), kissing (qublah) with desire, or sleeping together (mudaja'ah) without ejaculation – all while mistakenly believing that these acts had already broken the fast – still require kaffarah, because the person deliberately ate without a valid legal excuse. However, an exception is made if the person acted upon a fatwa from a qualified jurist (faqih) or misinterpreted a hadith; in that case, the kaffarah is waived due to the presence of doubt (shubhah). The passage further elaborates that even if the jurist's fatwa was incorrect, the layman is excused because his duty is to follow the mufti. The same applies to oiling one's mustache; if a person thought that broke the fast and ate, kaffarah is due, unless he obtained a fatwa to that effect. The discussion then turns to the hadith 'The cupper and the cupped break their fast,' which some scholars (like al-Awza'i and Ahmad) took literally, but the Hanafi school interprets it as referring to backbiting, as in the story where the Prophet passed by people cupping and they were backbiting. The passage concludes that if a person hears this hadith but does not know its interpretation, he may be excused due to doubt; if he knows the correct interpretation, kaffarah is obligatory. This entire discussion demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of balancing textual evidence (dalil) with rational principles (qiyas) and the concept of shubhah (legal doubt) in imposing expiation.

Key Terms
الغِيبة (al-ghībah): Backbiting: mentioning a fellow Muslim in a way he dislikes in his absence.
ذهاب الثواب (dhahab al-thawāb): Loss of reward – the interpretation of the hadith regarding ghibah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of backbiting (ghibah) during fasting?
A) The fast becomes invalid and must be made up.
B) The fast becomes invalid and requires kaffarah.
C) The reward of the fast is nullified, but the fast itself remains valid.
D) The fast remains valid and reward is intact if one repents.

Key Takeaway
Backbiting destroys the reward of fasting but does not invalidate it; kaffarah is only for subsequent intentional eating.

Sawm — Fasting
advanced
10 min
Eating After Cupping, Touch, or Kiss: The Role of Shubhah
Rulings on eating after cupping, touching, kissing, etc., with exceptions based on shubhah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the concept of shubhah (legal doubt) to determine when kaffarah is waived for a fasting person who eats after mistakenly believing his fast was already broken by cupping, touching, kissing, or oiling his mustache.

Core Idea
Shubhah from a fatwa or a misinterpreted hadith can waive kaffarah even if the belief was wrong.

The passage concludes with a complex discussion of scenarios where a fasting person believes his fast is already invalid due to certain acts (cupping, touching, kissing, sleeping together, oiling mustache). If he then eats, normally kaffarah would be due. However, if he acted on a fatwa from a qualified jurist (faqih) or misinterpreted a hadith, the kaffarah is waived because of shubhah. This lesson examines the conditions and limits of this exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling: eating after believing the fast is broken
The base ruling: if a person undergoes cupping, touches with desire, kisses, etc., and then eats thinking his fast is already invalid, kaffarah is due because he intentionally broke the fast without a valid excuse.
The passage states: 'لزمته الكفارة' (kaffarah is due) for these scenarios.
2
Understand the exception: shubhah from fatwa or misinterpreted hadith
Kaffarah is waived if the person sought a fatwa from a faqih and then broke his fast, or if he misinterpreted a hadith (like the cupping hadith) and acted on that interpretation, without knowing the correct interpretation.
The text says: 'إلا إذا تأوّل حديثاً، أو استفتى فقيهاً فأفطر، فلا كفارة عليه'.
3
Analyze the limits of the exception for oiling the mustache
For oiling the mustache, the exception may not apply because the matter is not obscure (la yashtabihu 'ala man lahu shimmatun min al-fiqh). However, there is a conflicting narration from Qadi Khan that allows the exception if one is ignorant and sought a fatwa.
This shows a tension between the general principle and a specific case. The commentator notes the contradiction and attempts reconciliation.
4
Examine the special case of hearing the cupping hadith
If a person hears the hadith 'Afṭara al-ḥājim wa al-maḥjūm' and does not know its interpretation, and then breaks his fast, he may be excused because the Prophet's words are more worthy to follow than a mufti's. Contradicted by Abu Yusuf who says the layman cannot take it literally.
The relied-upon view is that if he knows the interpretation, kaffarah is due; otherwise, doubt excuses.

In al-Mukhtar: eating Armenian clay absolutely, and non-Armenian clay like dry clay (tifl) if one habitually eats it, and a small amount of salt, in al-Mukhtar. Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling statement from the commentary on Nur al-Idah, listing various actions that require both qada and kaffarah when performed intentionally while fasting. The author organizes these actions under the rubric of what is considered 'nutritive' or 'pleasurable' intake. First, eating Armenian clay is absolutely cause for kaffarah, as it is eaten for medicinal purposes, thus considered a complete breaking of the fast. Non-Armenian clay (dry clay) only triggers kaffarah if one habitually eats it; otherwise, it is merely sinful but not requiring kaffarah. Eating even a small amount of salt is, in the relied-upon view (al-mukhtar), subject to kaffarah, though there is a weaker narration limiting it to small amounts; this is clarified as an 'examinatory' (imtihani) issue, meaning it tests the student's knowledge of different riwayat. Corn cobs (ka'ub qawa'im al-dhurah) also require both qada and kaffarah according to al-Zandawisti. Second, swallowing the saliva of one's wife or a close friend incurs kaffarah because it is deemed pleasurable; swallowing anyone else's saliva only invalidates the fast but does not require kaffarah, as it is repulsive. Third, eating intentionally after committing backbiting (ghibah) requires kaffarah. The passage defines ghibah as mentioning a fellow Muslim in a way he would dislike in his absence. The hadith 'Backbiting breaks the fast' is interpreted by consensus as meaning the loss of reward, not the invalidation of the fast itself. Therefore, the kaffarah here is for the subsequent eating, not for the ghibah itself. Fourth, eating after cupping (hijamah), touching (mass), kissing (qublah) with desire, or sleeping together (mudaja'ah) without ejaculation – all while mistakenly believing that these acts had already broken the fast – still require kaffarah, because the person deliberately ate without a valid legal excuse. However, an exception is made if the person acted upon a fatwa from a qualified jurist (faqih) or misinterpreted a hadith; in that case, the kaffarah is waived due to the presence of doubt (shubhah). The passage further elaborates that even if the jurist's fatwa was incorrect, the layman is excused because his duty is to follow the mufti. The same applies to oiling one's mustache; if a person thought that broke the fast and ate, kaffarah is due, unless he obtained a fatwa to that effect. The discussion then turns to the hadith 'The cupper and the cupped break their fast,' which some scholars (like al-Awza'i and Ahmad) took literally, but the Hanafi school interprets it as referring to backbiting, as in the story where the Prophet passed by people cupping and they were backbiting. The passage concludes that if a person hears this hadith but does not know its interpretation, he may be excused due to doubt; if he knows the correct interpretation, kaffarah is obligatory. This entire discussion demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of balancing textual evidence (dalil) with rational principles (qiyas) and the concept of shubhah (legal doubt) in imposing expiation.

Key Terms
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Legal doubt that prevents the imposition of punishment or expiation; here, it waives kaffarah when based on a fatwa or misinterpreted hadith.
التأويل (al-ta'wīl): Figurative interpretation of a text; in this context, interpreting the cupping hadith as referring to backbiting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a fasting person who eats after cupping NOT have to pay kaffarah?
A) He never has to pay kaffarah because cupping breaks the fast.
B) He always has to pay kaffarah.
C) He does not have to pay kaffarah if he had a fatwa from a faqih that cupping breaks the fast.
D) He does not have to pay kaffarah if he did not know cupping does not break the fast.

Key Takeaway
The presence of a valid shubhah from a fatwa or a misinterpreted hadith can waive kaffarah even when the belief was mistaken.

Kaffarah for Eating After Mistakenly Thinking Fast Broken
Ruling on kaffarah when fasting person eats after various acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which kaffarah is due when a fasting person eats after mistakenly assuming his fast is broken, and identify when a shubha (doubt) waives the kaffarah.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is due for deliberately eating after a mistaken belief unless a credible fatwa or hadith creates a shubha.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah addresses a subtle area of fasting law: when a fasting person commits an act that does not actually break the fast, but then eats thinking it did. The ruling hinges on whether the person had a legitimate basis (shubha) for his mistaken belief. Here, the author systematically presents the scenarios and exceptions, relying on the principle that a fatwa or a heard hadith (without known interpretation) creates a shubha that waives the kaffarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is deliberately eating after mistakenly believing that a prior act (like cupping, touching, etc.) had already broken the fast. The general ruling is that kaffarah is wajib because the eating was intentional without a valid excuse.
The prior acts themselves do not break the fast per the Hanafi madhhab.
2
Locate the dalil and principle
The ruling is based on the principle that intentional breaking of a Ramadan fast without a legitimate reason incurs kaffarah. The exceptions are derived from the concept of shubha (legal doubt), which can arise from a credible fatwa or a heard hadith whose interpretation is unknown to the layperson.
The hadith of cupping (أَفْطَرَ الحَاجِمُ والمَحْجُومُ) is discussed as a potential source of doubt.
3
Trace the exceptions
Exceptions occur when the person acted on a fatwa from a qualified faqih (jurist) that the prior act breaks the fast, or when he heard a prophetic hadith to that effect and did not know its Hanafi interpretation. In such cases, a shubha exists and kaffarah is waived.
The definition of faqih here is broad: a follower of a mujtahid who holds the prior act to be invalidating, even if the Hanafi madhhab disagrees.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf disagrees regarding the hadith scenario: he holds that kaffarah is due even if the layperson heard the hadith without knowing its interpretation, because the layperson should not take hadith literally.
The author sides with the majority, considering the Prophet's statement itself as a strong shubha.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that kaffarah is not due when a valid shubha exists from a fatwa or an uninterpreted hadith. This is supported by al-Kamal, al-Bada’i‘, and the author of Imdad al-Fattah.
The case of oiling the mustache is nuanced; some authorities like Qadi Khan allow the exception of a fatwa even there.

Or after cupping, or after touching, or a kiss with desire, or after lying together without ejaculation, or after oiling his mustache, thinking that he broke the fast by that, except if a jurist gave him a fatwa, or he heard the hadith and did not know its interpretation according to the madhhab. And Qadi Khan said: Some scholars said this and the virtue of cupping are the same in all aspects, and the majority of scholars said that kaffarah is due in any case. So kaffarah is obligatory for eating deliberately after cupping, or eating after touching, or eating after kissing with desire, or eating after lying together and lewd direct contact without ejaculation, thinking that he broke the fast by touching and kissing, then kaffarah is due unless he interpreted a hadith or asked a jurist and then broke the fast, then no kaffarah upon him even if the jurist erred and the hadith was not established, because the apparent fatwa and hadith become a shubha. This was stated by al-Kamal from al-Bada’i‘. Or eating after oiling his mustache, thinking that he broke the fast by that, because he is deliberate and his thought was not based on a legal evidence, so kaffarah is due, even if he asked a jurist and he gave a fatwa of breaking fast by oiling the mustache or he interpreted a hadith, because the fatwa of the jurist and interpretation of the hadith are not considered here, because this is not ambiguous for anyone who has a modicum of fiqh. Al-Kamal transmitted this from al-Bada’i‘. I say: But what is in Qadi Khan contradicts this, and also he who applied kohl or oiled himself or his mustache then ate deliberately, kaffarah is due, except if he was ignorant and asked for a fatwa and was given a fatwa of breaking fast, then kaffarah is not due. So accordingly our saying 'except if a jurist gave him a fatwa' includes the case of oiling the mustache, and by 'jurist' is meant a follower of a mujtahid, like the Hanbalis and some ahl al-hadith who consider cupping as breaking the fast, so no kaffarah because the common person must follow the mufti's statement, so the fatwa becomes a shubha for him, even if it was wrong in itself. This is in al-Burhan. Or except if the one being cupped and the cupper heard the hadith, which is his saying: 'The cupper and the cupped have broken the fast' and they did not know its interpretation according to the madhhab, because the statement of the Messenger of Allah is not lesser in degree than the statement of the mufti, and the statement of the mufti is valid for us, so the statement of the Messenger is more deserving. And from Abu Yusuf it is obligatory because the common person, when he hears the hadith, it is not for him to take it literally, because it may be turned from its literal meaning or abrogated. And so if he knew its interpretation, kaffarah becomes obligatory due to the absence of shubha. Its interpretation is that he passed by them while they were backbiting and said: 'that is, their fast has gone due to backbiting' as we presented earlier. And you have known the disagreement in the issue of thinking that the fast is broken by oil.
Source pages p. 649

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on kaffarah (expiation) for a fasting person who deliberately eats after mistakenly believing that his fast has already been invalidated by certain acts. The author follows the structure of Nur al-Idah, expanding on each scenario. The general rule: if a fasting person performs an act that does not actually break the fast (like cupping, touching with desire, kissing, lying together without ejaculation, or oiling the mustache) and then eats thinking the fast is broken, kaffarah is due because he willfully ate without a legitimate excuse. However, there are exceptions: (1) If a qualified jurist (faqih) gave him a fatwa that such an act breaks the fast and he acted on it, no kaffarah is due because the fatwa creates a valid doubt (shubha). (2) If he heard a hadith (like 'The cupper and the cupped have broken the fast') and did not know its interpretation in the Hanafi madhhab (which interprets it as referring to backbiting), then no kaffarah due to the doubt. But if he knew the interpretation, kaffarah is due because the doubt is removed. The passage also discusses the case of oiling the mustache specifically, noting a difference: some authorities (like Qadi Khan) allow the exception of a fatwa, while al-Kamal from al-Bada’i‘ says no exception because the matter is clear. The author reconciles by defining 'faqih' as a follower of a mujtahid who holds that cupping breaks the fast (like Hanbalis), so their fatwa creates a shubha for the layperson. Abu Yusuf differs regarding hearing the hadith: he holds kaffarah is due because the layperson must not take hadith literally. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the one stated: no kaffarah if there is a shubha from fatwa or misunderstanding of hadith.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation required for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast without valid excuse, typically feeding 60 poor persons or fasting 60 days.
حجامة (ḥijāmah): Cupping therapy; in the context of fasting, it does not break the fast per the Hanafi madhhab.
شبهة (shubhah): A legal doubt or ambiguity that prevents the establishment of a strict ruling like kaffarah.
تأويل (ta’wīl): Interpretation of a text away from its literal meaning based on evidence; here the hadith of cupping is interpreted as referring to backbiting.
فقيه (faqīh): A jurist who is a follower of a mujtahid, capable of issuing fatwa within the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is due when a fasting person deliberately eats after a mistaken belief unless that belief arises from a credible fatwa or an uninterpreted hadith.

Kaffarah on a Woman Who Complies with Coercion in Ramadan
Ruling on kaffarah for a woman who complies with coerced intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that kaffarah is wajib on a woman who willingly enables intercourse during Ramadan under coercion, and identify the conditions (tamkin and absence of shubha) that trigger liability.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is due on the woman who willingly cooperates—her enabling (tamkin) breaks her fast with full liability.

In Ramadan, intercourse invalidates the fast and requires kaffarah. But what if a woman is coerced? Does she share the liability? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling: if she complies willingly, she is liable for kaffarah because she enabled the act. We also examine a case where she conceals the time of dawn, confirming that absence of any doubt of permissibility solidifies her responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage rules on a woman who is coerced into intercourse but does not resist; she is obligated to pay kaffarah.
The ruling is explicit: kaffarah wajib.
2
Determine the cause of kaffarah
The text states that the cause is 'violation of spoiling the fast', not the intercourse itself.
Thus, whoever contributes to breaking the fast is liable.
3
Understand the criterion of liability
Liability arises from tamkin (enabling) – the woman willingly allows the act, so she participates in spoiling her own fast.
Even if coerced, her active compliance makes her responsible.
4
Apply to the concealment scenario
The second case: she knows it is Fajr, hides it from her husband, and he has intercourse unaware. She alone is liable because she intentionally caused her fast to break.
Her fast-breaking is free from shubha (doubt), so kaffarah is due.
5
Recognize the absence of shubha
The text emphasizes that her act is 'free from the shubha of permissibility' – no excuse applies.
Contrast with cases where the woman might mistakenly think the act is allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتجب الكفارة على منْ طَاوَعَتْ مُكْرَهَاً. وتجب الكفارة على من طاوَعَتْ) رجلاً (مُكْرَهَا) على وطئها؛ لأن سبب الكفارة جناية إفساد الصوم، لا نفس الوقاع، وقد تحققت من جانبها بالتمكين من الفعل، كذا في البرهان» وفي «التجنيس» : علمت طلوع الفجر وكتمته عن زوجها حتى واقعها وهو لا يعلم به عليها الكفارة لأن إفطارها، عري عن شبهة الإباحة.
And kaffarah is obligatory upon a woman who complies with a coercer. And kaffarah is obligatory upon the one who complied with a man who coerced her to intercourse, because the cause of kaffarah is the violation of spoiling the fast, not the act of intercourse itself, and it is realized from her side by enabling the act. This is in al-Burhan. And in al-Tajnis: She knew the break of dawn and concealed it from her husband until he had intercourse with her while he did not know, then kaffarah is upon her because her breaking of the fast is free from the shubha of permissibility.
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage states two related rulings concerning kaffarah for a woman in Ramadan. First, if a woman is coerced into intercourse but she willingly complies (i.e., she does not resist but allows it), kaffarah is obligatory upon her. The reasoning is that kaffarah is for the offense of spoiling the fast, not for the act of intercourse itself. By enabling the act (tamkin), she contributed to the spoiling of her fast, so she shares the liability. This ruling is found in al-Burhan. Second, a sub-case in al-Tajnis: if a woman knows that dawn has broken and hides this fact from her husband so that he has intercourse with her while he is unaware, then kaffarah is upon her because her fast was broken without any shubha (doubt) of permissibility. The husband may be excused due to ignorance, but she is not excused because she intentionally concealed the information. Both cases emphasize that active compliance or intentional deception results in kaffarah. The passage does not provide detailed proof but states the ruling textually.

Key Terms
مكره (mukrah): A person who coerces another, with threat of harm, to commit an act.
تمكين (tamkīn): Enabling or facilitating the act; here it means the woman does not resist and allows intercourse.
شبهة الإباحة (shubhat al-ibāḥah): Doubt of permissibility; a mistaken belief that an act is allowed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Kaffarah on a Woman Who Submits to Coercion and Conceals Fajr
Two specific rulings on kaffarah for women in Ramadan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios where a woman incurs kaffarah in Ramadan fasting and explain the underlying principle (sabab al-kaffarah).

Core Idea
Kaffarah is triggered by spoiling the fast, not the act itself; enabling the act is the cause.

This passage presents two specific rulings from Hanafi fiqh on when a woman must pay kaffarah after breaking her Ramadan fast. The first involves a woman who submits to a man coercing her into intercourse; the second involves a woman who knowingly conceals the time of dawn from her husband, leading to intercourse. Both rulings hinge on the principle that kaffarah is incurred by the one whose action directly causes the invalidation of the fast, regardless of initial coercion or deception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Read the first ruling (woman who submits to a coercer) and the second ruling (woman who conceals fajr). Note the difference in circumstances.
Both lead to the same ruling: kaffarah wajib.
2
Extract the underlying principle
The author states: 'because the cause of expiation is the crime of spoiling the fast, not the act of intercourse itself.' Understand that kaffarah attaches to causing the fast to be invalidated.
This is a talil (rationale).
3
Apply the principle to the first case
In the first case, the woman enabled the act by her submission, so she caused the spoiling of her fast. Coercion does not negate her responsibility because she had the choice to resist.
4
Apply the principle to the second case
In the second case, she concealed knowledge of dawn. Her action (secrecy) led to intercourse, and her fast was broken with no shadow of permissibility (no shubha).
The phrase 'cari 'an shubhat al-ibaha' is key.
5
Contrast with potential exceptions
Consider what might exempt a woman from kaffarah: if she were forced at knifepoint with no possibility of resistance. The text implies that mere coercion does not excuse; active submission triggers liability.
This nuance is not fully expanded in the passage but is inferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتجب الكفارة على من طَاوَعَتْ مُكْرَهَاً. الخلاف في مسألة ظن الفطر بالدهن وتجب الكفارة على من طاوَعَتْ رجلاً مُكْرَهَا على وطئها؛ لأن سبب الكفارة جناية إفساد الصوم، لا نفس الوقاع، وقد تحققت من جانبها بالتمكين من الفعل، كذا في البرهان. وفي «التجنيس» : علمت طلوع الفجر وكتمته عن زوجها حتى واقعها وهو لا يعلم به عليها الكفارة لأن إفطارها عري عن شبهة الإباحة.
And the expiation is obligatory on one who complied with a coercer. The dispute in the issue of thinking that oil breaks the fast: and expiation is obligatory on a woman who submitted to a man who coerced her into intercourse; because the cause of expiation is the crime of spoiling the fast, not the act of intercourse itself, and from her side it is realized by enabling the act, as in al-Burhan. And in al-Tajnis: if she knew the dawn had risen and concealed it from her husband until he had intercourse with her while he did not know, then on her is expiation because her breaking of fast is free from any semblance of permissibility.
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Nur al-Idah addresses the obligation of kaffarah (expiation) in two scenarios involving breaking the fast during Ramadan. First, the author states that kaffarah is obligatory on a woman who submits to a man who coerces her into intercourse. The reasoning is that the cause of kaffarah is the violation of the fast (the 'crime' of spoiling it), not the act of intercourse per se. Since the woman enabled the act by her submission, she is considered to have broken the fast, and thus kaffarah is due. The text references al-Burhan as a source. Second, from al-Tajnis, a case is presented where a woman knows that dawn has entered (so fasting has begun) but conceals this knowledge from her husband. He then has intercourse with her, unaware that it is now forbidden. In this case, she must pay kaffarah because her act of breaking the fast is devoid of any semblance of permissibility (i.e., she had no excuse). Both rulings are based on the principle that kaffarah is incurred by the one whose action directly causes the invalidation of the fast, even if she was initially coerced or deceived her husband.

Key Terms
طاوَعَتْ (taawa'at): She obeyed or complied, here referring to a woman willingly submitting to intercourse despite coercion.
مُكْرَهَاً (mukrahan): Coerced or forced; a man who compels a woman to intercourse.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation; a specific penalty for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast without valid excuse.
شبهة الإباحة (shubhat al-ibaha): A semblance of permissibility; if the fast-break occurs under a mistaken belief that it was allowed, kaffarah may not be due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because she did not inform the man of the time.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah in Hanafi fiqh is based on causing the fast to be broken; a woman who enables intercourse, even under coercion or through concealment, is liable.

Kaffarah: When It Drops and Its Types
Comprehensive rulings on expiation: when it drops, types, and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which kaffarah drops and list the three valid forms of kaffarah with their prerequisites.

Core Idea
Kaffarah drops only for excuses arising without your action—self-inflicted excuses do not release you from expiation.

This lesson explains the rulings on kaffarah (expiation) after it becomes obligatory for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast. We'll explore when it drops—only for natural excuses like menstruation or illness that occur without your doing—and examine key disputes, such as whether self-wounding or coerced travel cancels it. Finally, we outline the three prescribed forms: freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Kaffarah becomes wajib when one deliberately eats or has intercourse during a Ramadan fast without a valid excuse. Note the hukm level: wajib, not fard.
This ruling is settled in the Hanafi school.
2
Locate the conditions for dropping kaffarah
Kaffarah drops if, after the deliberate break, a natural excuse occurs on the same day: menstruation, postpartum bleeding, or an illness that permits breaking fast. The key condition: the excuse must arise without one's own action.
The legal reasoning: the fast's requirement is indivisible; an excuse at the end casts doubt on the beginning.
3
Analyze the disputes on self-inflicted excuses
If one intentionally wounds himself causing illness, or throws himself from a height, scholars differ. The preferred view (al-mukhtar) is that kaffarah does not drop because the illness results from his own action, which cannot nullify a divine obligation.
Also, if one exhausts himself to extreme thirst, he must still pay kaffarah.
4
Examine coerced travel
If one is forced to travel (e.g., by a sultan) after the break, kaffarah does not drop in the apparent riwayah, contrary to Zufar. The excuse is not from the One entitled; the person could have chosen not to travel if not coerced.
Coerced travel is treated like voluntary travel in effect.
5
Identify the forms of kaffarah and their conditions
Kaffarah is: (1) freeing a slave (even non-Muslim) with full faculties; if unable, (2) fasting two consecutive months (excluding Eid and Tashriq days); if unable, (3) feeding sixty poor persons.
The order is sequential: the next option is only valid if the previous is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكفارة وما يسقطها عن الذمة بعد الوجوب
تَسْقُطُ الكفّارةُ التي لزمت من أكلت عمداً أو جامعت طائعة بطرو حيض أو نفاس أو طرو مرض مباح للفطر وهو ما يكون بغير صنع من أفطر عمداً قبل وجود العذر ثم حصل العذر في يومه أي يوم الإفساد ولأن الكفارة إنما تجب في صوم مستحق واستحقاقه في يوم واحد لا يتجزأ ثبوتاً وسقوطاً فبعروض المرض والحيض في آخره تمكنت شبهة عدم الاستحقاق في أوله كما في البرهان وقيدنا بكونه حصل بغير صنعه لأنه إذا أفطر عمداً ثم جرح نفسه فمرض بذلك مرضاً لا يستطيع معه الصوم أو ألقى نفسه من سطح أو جبل اختلف المشايخ فيه قال بعضهم سقط عنه الكفارة كما إذا مرض ابتداء وقال بعضهم لا تسقط عنه لأنه بفعل العباد فلا يؤثر في إسقاط حق الشرع كذا في التجنيس وقال الكمال والمختار لا تسقط لأن المرض من الجرح وأنه وجد مقصوراً على الحال فلا يؤثر في الماضي انتهى وذكر في جمع العلوم لو أتعب نفسه في شيء أو عمل حتى أجهده العطش فأفطر كفر لأنه ليس بمسافر ولا مريض وقيل بخلافه وبه أخذ البقالي كذا في التاتارخانية ولا تسقط الكفارة عمن سوفِر به كرهاً كما لو سافر باختياره بعد لزومها عليه في ظاهر الرواية خلافاً لزفر وهو رواية والعذر لم يجئ من قبل صاحب الحق كذا في البرهان وقال قاضي خان لو أكرهه السلطان على السفر لا تسقط عنه الكفارة وسنذكر ما إذا أفطر خشية المرض بالقتال أو بمجيء الحمى والحيض في العوارض والكفارة تحرير رقبة ولو كانت غير مؤمنة لإطلاق النص فيها وشرطها عدم فوات منفعة البطش والمشي والكلام والنظر والعقل كما في الظهار فإن عجز عنه أي التحرير بأن لم يكن في ملكه رقبة ولا يملك ثمنها صام شهرين متتابعين ليس فيهما يوم عيد ولا بعض أيام التشريق للنهي عن صيامهما فإن لم يستطع الصوم لمرض أو كبر أطعم ستين مسكيناً
Chapter on expiation and what drops it from liability after obligation. The expiation that becomes obligatory for one who ate intentionally or had intercourse willingly drops by the onset of menstruation, or postpartum bleeding, or the onset of an illness that permits breaking the fast, which is that which occurs without one's own action, from one who broke the fast intentionally before the occurrence of the excuse, then the excuse occurred on that day, i.e., the day of spoiling. Because expiation is only obligatory for a fast that is required, and its requirement in one day is indivisible in affirmation and dropping. So by the occurrence of illness and menstruation at its end, a semblance of non-requirement is established at its beginning, as in al-Burhan. We restrict it to its occurrence without his own action because if he broke the fast intentionally then wounded himself and thereby became ill with an illness that he cannot fast with, or threw himself from a roof or a mountain, the shuyukh differed: some said the expiation drops from him as if he became ill initially, some said it does not drop because it is by the action of the servant, so it does not affect in dropping the right of the Law, as in al-Tajnis. Al-Kamal said: the chosen view is that it does not drop, because the illness is from the wound and it is confined to the present, so it does not affect the past, end. It is mentioned in Jam' al-'Ulum: if one tires himself in something or works until thirst overpowers him and then breaks his fast, he does kaffarah because he is not a traveler nor ill; and it is said contrary, and al-Baqqali took that, as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. And the expiation does not drop from one who was taken on a journey by coercion, as if he traveled voluntarily, after it became obligatory on him, in the apparent riwayah, contrary to Zufar, which is a riwayah. The excuse did not come from the side of the one entitled, as in al-Burhan. Qadikhan said: if the sultan coerced him into traveling, the expiation does not drop from him. And we will mention what if he broke his fast fearing illness from fighting or by the coming of fever and menstruation among the impediments. And the expiation is the freeing of a slave, even if she is not believing, due to the absolute text concerning it, and its condition is that loss of the benefit of power, walking, speech, sight, and intellect does not occur, as in zihar. And if he is unable to do that (i.e., the freeing) because he does not own a slave nor the price of one, then he fasts two consecutive months in which there is no day of Eid nor any days of Tashreeq due to the prohibition of fasting on them. And if he cannot fast due to illness or old age, then he feeds sixty poor persons.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This chapter from Sharh Nur al-Idah explains the rulings on kaffarah (expiation) after it has become obligatory, focusing on what causes it to drop and the forms of kaffarah. The author begins by stating that kaffarah drops due to the onset of menstruation (hayd), postpartum bleeding (nifas), or an illness that permits breaking the fast on that day. The condition is that this onset occurs without one's own action. The reasoning is that kaffarah is only obligatory for a fast that is required, and the requirement of the fast on that day is indivisible. If a valid excuse (like illness) occurs later in the day, it creates a semblance of non-requirement from the start, so kaffarah is dropped. However, if one deliberately injures himself causing illness, there is a dispute: some say kaffarah still drops, others say it does not because it was his own act. The preferred view (al-mukhtar) according to al-Kamal is that it does not drop. Similarly, if one exhausts himself to the point of thirst and then breaks fast, he must pay kaffarah unless he is a traveler or ill. Then the author discusses that kaffarah does not drop if one is forced to travel (coerced by a ruler), in contrast to Zufar's opinion. Finally, the types of kaffarah are given: freeing a slave (even if non-Muslim) provided the slave has full physical and mental faculties; if unable, then fasting two consecutive months excluding Eid days and days of Tashriq; if unable to fast due to illness or old age, then feeding sixty poor persons.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation; a specific penalty for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast without valid excuse.
طرو (turuww): Onset or occurrence, here of conditions like menstruation, postpartum bleeding, or illness.
حيض (hayd): Menstruation; its onset during a fast day causes the fast to be invalid and drops kaffarah.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding; similar ruling to hayd in dropping kaffarah.
مختار (al-mukhtar): The chosen or preferred view in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view, what happens to kaffarah if a person is forced to travel (by coercion) after having already broken the fast?
A) The kaffarah drops because travel is an excuse.
B) The kaffarah does not drop because the excuse did not come from Allah.
C) The kaffarah drops only if the person is sick.
D) The kaffarah is converted to feeding sixty poor.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah drops only for natural excuses (hayd, nifas, illness) that occur without your action; self-inflicted conditions and coerced travel do not cancel it.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
12 min
The Sequential Obligations of Kaffarah: Slaves, Fasting, and Feeding
Details the three sequential kaffarah options with conditions and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three sequential kaffarah options and their conditions, and apply them to a scenario of intentional fast-breaking.

Core Idea
Kaffarah for a missed fast must be done in strict sequence: only if unable to free a slave may one fast two months; only if unable to fast may one feed.

When a Muslim intentionally breaks a Ramadan fast without a valid excuse (such as intercourse or eating/drinking), kaffarah becomes wajib. The kaffarah consists of three options in a fixed order: freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor persons. Each step is only allowed if the previous one is impossible. This lesson unpacks the conditions for each option and the evidence from the hadith of the Bedouin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger for kaffarah
Kaffarah is wajib upon intentional break of a Ramadan fast without a valid excuse. The act must be deliberate; if done due to forgetfulness or coercion, kaffarah is not required.
2
Recall the three options in order
First: free a believing or non-believing slave with full faculties. Second: if unable to free, fast two consecutive months, avoiding Eid and Tashriq days. Third: if unable to fast due to illness or old age, feed sixty poor persons.
The ability to own a slave or its price determines inability.
3
Analyze the conditions for each option
Freeing: slave must have strength, vision, speech, hearing, and intellect. Fasting: no interruption except prohibited days; must be consecutive. Feeding: provide filling food (morning/evening meal or give half sa' wheat or one sa' dates/barley per person).
Condition of inability differs: for fasting it is illness or permanent inability; for the first step it is financial inability.
4
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith of Abu Hurairah about the Bedouin who had intercourse in Ramadan shows the sequential order: the Prophet asked about freeing a slave, then fasting, then feeding. The man's inability at each step allowed him to proceed. The hadith also shows special concessions for the poor.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted by all schools for this ruling.
5
Apply the ruling to a contemporary scenario
A person breaks the fast intentionally. If he can afford to buy and free a slave, he must do that. If not, he must fast two consecutive months (excluding Eid and Tashriq). If he is unable to fast due to a valid reason (not mere inconvenience), he must feed sixty poor persons as described.
Inability must be real; mere preference for an easier option is not sufficient.

Source Text
